GENERAL EDITOR'S PREFACE 
THE Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan-that Institute of Indian Culture in Bombay-needed a Book University, a series of books which, if read, would serve the purpose of providing higher education. Particular emphasis, however, was to be put on such literature as revealed the deeper impulsions of India. As a first step, it was decided to bring out in English 100 books, 50 of which were to be taken in hand almost at once. Each book was to contain from 200 to 250 pages. 
It is our intention to publish the books we select, not only in English, but also in the following Indian languages: Hindi, Bengali, Gujarati, Marathi, Tamil, Telugu, Kannada and Malayalam. 
This scheme, involving the publication of 900 volumes, requires ample funds and an all-India organisation. The Bhavan is exerting its utmost to supply them. 
The objectives for which the Bhavan stands are the re- integration of the Indian culture in the light of modern knowledge and to suit our present-day needs and the resusci- tation of its fundamental values in their pristine vigour. 
Let me make our goal more explicit : 
We seek the dignity of man, which necessarily implies the creation of social conditions which would allow him freedom to evolve along the lines of his own temperament and capa- cities; we seek the harmony of individual efforts and social relations, not in any makeshift way, but within the frame- work of the Moral Order: we seek the creative art of life, by the alchemy of which human limitations are progressively transmuted, so that man may become the instrument of God, and is able to see Him in all and all in Him. 
The world, we feel, is too much with us. Nothing would uplift or inspire us so much as the beauty and aspiration which such books can teach. 
vi 
GENERAL EDITOR'S PREFACE 
In this series, therefore, the literature of India, ancient and modern, will be published in a form easily accessible to all. Books in other literatures of the world, if they illustrate the principles we stand for, will also be included. 
This common pool of literature, it is hoped, will enable the reader, eastern or western, to understand and appreciate currents of world thought, as also the movements of the mind in India, which, though they flow through different linguistic channels, have a common urge and aspiration. 
Fittingly, the Book University's first venture is the Mahabharata, summarised by one of the greatest living Indians, C. Rajagopalachari; the second work is on a section of it, the Gita by H. V. Divatia, an eminent jurist and a student of philosophy. Centuries ago, it was proclaimed of the Mahabharata: "What is not in it, is nowhere". After twenty-five centuries, we can use the same words about it. He who knows it not, knows not the heights and depths of the soul; he misses the trials and tragedy and the beauty and grandeur of life. 
The Mahabharata is not a mere epic; it is a romance, telling the tale of heroic men and women and of some who were divine; it is a whole literature in itself, containing a code of life, a philosophy of social and ethical relations, and speculative thought on human problems that is hard to rival; but, above all, it has for its core the Gita, which is, as the world is beginning to find out, the noblest of scriptures and the grandest of sagas in which the climax is reached in the wondrous Apocalypse in the Eleventh Canto. 
Through such books alone the harmonies underlying true culture, I am convinced, will one day reconcile the disorders of modern life. 
I thank all those who have helped to make this new branch of the Bhavan's activity successful. 
1, QUEEN VICTORIA ROAD, 
NEW DELHI: 
3rd October, 1951. 
K. M. MUNSHI 
AUTHOR'S PREFACE 
LORD Sri Venkateswara of the Seven Hills of Tirupati is worshipped throughout India. No comprehensive temple Monograph giving all particulars of this temple is available at present. An attempt has therefore been made in this book to prepare a temple Monograph and give authentic information about the temple and the Lord in a very com- prehensive manner, studying the temple and the Lord from the historical, literary, sculptural, religious and other points of view. This book is dedicated to the twin feet of Sri Ven- kateswara, the Lord of the Seven Hills. 
I have to acknowledge with thanks the kind encou- ragement given to me in the writing of this book by Sri P. V. Narasimha Rao, Minister for Education, who was previously Minister for Law and Endowments in the Gov- ernment of Andhra Pradesh. This book could not have been written but for the able stenographic assistance of Shri R. Thippanna, Anantapur. I am also grateful to my Sans- krit Guru, Sri Subramanyam Sastri of Anantapur and to Mrs. Rama Devi Sitapati for valuable assistance in the preparation of this book. Thanks are also due to the T. T. Devasthanam authorities, for assistance and facilities given, in particular to Sri K. Vasudeva Rao, I.A.S., Endow- ments Commissioner, Govt. of Andhra Pradesh. I acknow- ledge with thanks the excellent photographs of Tirupati presented to me for use in this book by Sri Raju of Raju Photographic Studios, Anantapur. 
I also take this opportunity to give my respectful pra- nams and obeisance to Jagadguru Sri Sankaracharya Swamiji of Kanchi Kamakotipeetam. 
I am grateful to Dr. K. M. Munshi in particular, and the Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan, Bombay for undertaking the publication of this work in their Book-University series. 
HYDERABAD, 2-10-1968. 
PIDATALA SITAPATIAUTHOR'S PREFACE TO THE SECOND EDITION 
The first edition of the book Sri Venkateswara, the Lord of the Seven Hills, Tirupati, was well received both by the public and the press and was sold out quickly, with the re- sult that a second edition is now being brought out by Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan, Bombay. I am grateful to late Dr. K.M. Munshi in particular, and the Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan, Bombay, for undertaking the publication of this work in their Book University series. Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan has played, and is continuing to play, a major role in the Cultural Renaissance of this country and the credit for visualising, motivating and organising all this creative activity should go to Dr. K.M. Munshi, Kulapati of the Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan. 
Lord Sri Venkateswara of the Seven Hills, Tirupati, is worshipped in India and abroad. He is the Eternal Cosmic One that is manifested at Tirupati and pervades the entire Universe. This book is dedicated to this Eternal One. 
The writing of this monograph on the ancient temple of Tirupati was taken up and completed by me while I was at Anantapur, and as District Collector of Chittoor. Sahit- ya Ratna Sri P.V. Narasimharao, the scholar-statesman of Andhra Pradesh, was then the Minister for Law and En- dowments of the Government of Andhra Pradesh. Sri Narasimharao is the architect of the model statute for reli- gious and charitable endowments i.e., the Andhra Pradesh Charitable and Hindu Religious Institutions and Endow- ments Act of 1966. It was primarily his encouragement which helped me in bringing out this book and it gives me great pleasure to record my heart-felt gratitude to him. The Telugu translation of this book is on the anvil, the work having been taken up by Mrs. P. Ramadevi Sitapati conjointly with me, and I hope the Telugu version will see the light of the day as soon as possible. 
This temple monograph has been revised, and it is hoped, gives authentic information about the temple and the Lord in a comprehensive manner, studying the temple 
X

and the Deity from the historical, literary, sculptural, and other points of view. 
I acknowledge with thanks the stenographic assistance of Sri R. Tippanna of Anantapur in the preparation of the book and Sri Raju of Raju Photographic Studios, Ananta- pur, for the excellent photographs of Tirupati presented to I am thankful to my colleague, Sri M. Chandramouli Reddy, I.A.S., Executive Officer, at present at Tirupati, for facilities afforded. The assistance of Sri M. Sriniva- sulu Naidu, Peshkar of the Tirupati temple, is gratefully acknowledged. I acknowledge the assistance given to me in the compilation of the book by Mrs. P. Rama Devi Sita- pati and my daughter Chir. P. Jyothirlakshmi. 
Several suggestions made in the first edition of the book as well as suggestions made by me as District Collec- tor, Chittoor, have been or are in the process of being ac- cepted by the temple authorities. It is hoped that a day will soon dawn when every devotee, whatever be his reli- gion or nationality, will have the blessing and a 'Happy Darshan' of the Lord at all times with facilities for medi- tation and prayer without botheration of payment of fees.

SRI VENKATESWARA SWAMY 
Lord Venkateswara 
Goddess PadmavatiCHAPTER I 
INTRODUCTION 
Sri Venkateswara or Balaji is Omnipresent, Omniscient and Omnipotent. He is everywhere and in the seeker's heart. His abode is Sri Vaikunta. He has however mani- fested Himself in Tirumalai, near Tirupati-Dharma Samstha- panarthaya Sambhavami Yuge Yuge-for the benefit and mukti of his devotees. Enshrined in Tirumalai and in the heart of every Man, the Lord Sri Venkateswara of Tirumalai and His consorts Sri Lakshmi and Sri Padmavathi Amma, rule the Universe. He is verily the Light of the Universe! 
Tirumalai is actually a small town located on the seventh hill of the Lord of the Seven Hills. The temple of Balaji is located here, constructed and developed by gene- rations of royal devotees and commoners, around the self- manifested idol of Lord Sri Venkateswara. The nearest rail- head to Tirumalai is Tirupati. Tirumalai is sometimes called 'Eguva Tirupati'; the town with the railhead at the bottom of the seven hills is however called 'Diguva Tirupati'. Both the Tirupatis are in the Chittoor District of Andhra Pradesh, an important State of Southern India. 
Tirupati is a small growing town. Devotees desiring to worship Lord Balaji have first to reach this town. Tiru- pati is a small station on the Southern Railway of India, seven miles away from Renigunta, an important railway junction on the Madras-Raichur broad-guage line. Reni- gunta junction is about 135 kilometres away from Madras. Devotees from Northern India can make their way direct to Madras1 and then make their way to Tirupati by taking 1. An intermediate station on the Delhi-Madras route where the pilgrims can break their journey is Gudur, if they do not desire to proceed to Madras. Connecting trains are available from Gudur to Tirupati. 
S.V.-1 
2 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
one of the many convenient trains running from Madras to places like Raichur and Bombay, via Renigunta. They may also reach Tirupati by road, taking a bus in Mint Bus stop at Madras. Pilgrims travelling by car from Madras may reach Tirupati by travelling via Satyavedu, Kalahasti and Renigunta (96 miles) or by reaching Tirupati via Tirut- tani, Puttur and Renigunta. 
On detraining at Tirupati, the pilgrim would have arrived at the foot of the Seven Hills of the Lord of Tirumalai. Tirumalai is fourteen miles away from Tirupati. A beauti- ful ghat road connects the two towns. This journey may easily be done in any of the innumerable temple buses run by Tirupati-Tirumalai Devasthanams Trust Board. The Enquiry Office of the T.T. Board at Tirupati will on request also make arrangements for transport and lodging of pilgrims at Tirumalai. The T.T. Devasthanam has many furnished cottages for lodging visitors and pilgrims to Tirumalai on payment basis. Free accommodation can also be obtained by pilgrims by contacting the Devasthanam authorities both at Tirupati and Tirumalai. The Devas- thanam has a reception office at Tirumalai working round the clock and the godly souls working in the reception department have never been known to fail any pilgrims visiting Tirumalai to worship the Lord. 
The town of Tirupati in Andhra Pradesh lies in the North Latitude 13°-38' and the East Longitude 79°-25'. The town of Tirumalai located on a range of Eastern Ghats called the Seven Hills lies in the North Latitude 13°-41' and East Longitude 79°-21'. The Tirupati on the plains is itself a pilgrimage centre with a famous temple for Lord Govinda- raja and Lord Raghunatha. The famous Alarmel Mangai 
1. T. T. D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. VI, Part I, Inscriptions of Venkatapatiraya's time, pp. 153-155 (English translations given for all inscriptions in the book). 
INTRODUCTION 
3 
temple of Sri Padmavathi, the consort of the Lord of Tirumalai, is at a distance of about 3 miles from Tirupati. There is also a famous old temple for Sri Tippaladisvara- mudaya Mahadeva (Lord Siva) in Yogimallam village near Tiruchanur (its name in the Chola era was Tiruchcho- kinur or Yoginur). According to custom pilgrims who worship the Lord of Tirumalai also worship his consort Padmavathi at Alarmel Mangapuram. 
The pilgrim who desires to walk up to Tirumalai can do so by taking the traditional sopana-margam to the Hill. The distance to be covered by foot is about seven miles. On his way to Tirumalai, the pilgrim will have to cross several hills. The ascent of the first hill is rather arduous in view of its steep gradient. The first structure on the top of the first hill which he sees en route is the Galigopuram. This Galigopuram was constructed by one Matli Kumara Anantaraja of the Matli family of Chiefs belonging to Rayachoti Taluk of Cuddapah District. Two inscriptions on the eastern and western walls of the 'patala mantapam' in Alipiri at the foot of the hill in Tirupati (the first in Telugu and the second in Tamil) mention in detail the charities of this royal benefactory.1 
The inscriptions also show that Anantaraja con- structed the Agra-gopura on the sopana-margam or pathway to Tirumalai. It would appear that the sopana-margam was 1. The first inscription is in Seesamalika verse and reads as 
follows: 
"Anantaraja, son of Chennamma and Matha Tiruvengada- natha of Deva-Choda family who was the moon to the ocean of the Solar race and who exercised authority over the tract of country lying between the Venkata (Tirupati) and Ahobila hills." 
Extent of his country:- 
Like Kapardi (Siva), he fought out fiercely the battles at Palagiri Animela, Pileru, Chappalli and Vemupalli; destroyed the armies of the enemies at Kottagala, Palakada, Gundluru and Mushturu; and also at Kumallakalva." 
4 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
also constructed by him. It is said that there was once a sepa- rate pathway to Tirumalai near the Kapila Thirtham; Matli Raja, however appears to have formed new pathway from Alipiri up to the Galigopuram. The above inscription also shows the charities of this royal benefactor. It is of interest to note that philanthropists existed in those days who main- tained annasatras or feeding houses for pilgrims proceeding from Sethu or Rameswaram to Badrinath in the Sitalayas or Himalayas. Anantaraja himself maintained twelve such annasatras in Cuddaph district. 
Proceeding further the pilgrim has to manoeuvre another steep hill called the Mokallakonda. It is a very difficult ascent; on the top of the hill is a small mantapam with a small temple for Sri Ramanuja. Pilgrims usually rest here and have on opportunity to enjoy the picturesque beauty of the surroundings. According to the Sri Venkatachala Ithi-

According to this book, a Rishi by name Vishvambara in the town of Kalavingam had set up images of Sri Rama, Lakshmana, and Sita and was worshipping these deities in his native town. When some disturbances occurred in the town, he felt that the images would not be safe and he is said to

List of various gifts performed by Him: 
He performed- 
(1) Rajata-dana (gift of silver), (2) Suvarna-dana (gift of gold), 
(3) Tula-dana (weighing himself in scales against precious 
metals and stones), 
(4) Mahi-dana (gift of lands), 
(5) Go-dana (gift of cows), 
(6) Bahu-kanyaka-dana (Marriages of many thousands of girls) to Brahmans whom he enabled through these danas to perform sacrifices and other dana-dharma. 
His charity at the foot of Tirupati hills: 
He was Lord of Anantarajapura with its gopura built by him, ruling the surrounding tract of country, being entitled Katakapuri-churakara and purified by the bath in the water of the Ganges brought every year. 
He constructed a huge tank at the hill-path near Chen- nuru (in Cuddapah). Beside the lovely and spacious Sesha- hill he constructed this Sripada-mantapam adored by Brahma, Isvara, Sanaka, other Devas and Sages, to stand as long as the moon and the stars endure. 
6 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
have directed some Srivaishnavas to take the images for safe custody to Tirumalai. It is said that while Tirumalainambi was expounding the Ramayana and explaining the incident of Vibhishana seeking protection of Rama, the Vaishnava priests brought the images of Rama, Lakshmana, Sita, Vibhishana and Angada and placed them in front of Sri Ramanuja. Sri Ramanuja and Tirumalainambi are then said to have taken these images to Tirumalai and arranged for their worship there. 
There are several other mantapams on the sopana- margam to Tirumalai. One such is the Ghanta mantapain on the way. According to tradition, there was a ghanta or a bell in this mantapam and Sri Ranganatha Yadavaraya of Chandragiri (1336-1356), a local Chief, was in the habit of taking his food at Chandragiri only after hearing the bell indicating that the Mahanaivedyam had been offered to the Lord of Tirumalai. It is this Chief who constructed for himself a royal palace on Venkatachalam. 
Proceeding further up, the pilgrim gets his first electrify- ing vision of Tirumalai, the Kaliyuga Vaikunta on earth, his journey's end. He has a bath in the holy Swami Pushkarini and is ready for the darshan of Sri Venkateswara, the Katya- valambita murthi and the Lord with the Varada hasta who is ever ready to come to the succour of his devotees. 
CHAPTER II 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
In this Chapter, it shall be our endeavour to join the queue of devotees entering the temple to obtain 'darshan' of the Lord in the temple. We repeat with the devotees the names of the Lord. "Om Namo Narayanayah' is the mantra on the lips of several of the devotees. 'Yedu Kondala Vada, Venkataramana, Govinda, Govinda' is a cry which we hear often. 'Ramaramana Govinda, Govinda' is another ecstatic shout that we hear as we make our way into the inner temple. 'Vaddi Kasula Vada, Govinda, Jai Balaji'2 are some of the other sounds which are music to our ears, as we walk in with a breathless heart into the sixteen-pillared Tirumamani Mantapam near the 'Bangaru Vakili' or the golden gate. Here we get the first electrifying vision of the stately Lord Sri Venkateswara with eyes that shower grace and love on Humanity. We pass through the 'Bangaru Vakili' and step into the closed Tiruvilankoyil Mantapa or Snapana mantapa as it is called. Passing through the Ramar Medai passage, we step into the closed Sayana Mantapa next to the Garba Griha or sanctum sanctorum. We now have the darshan of the Light of the Universe and the Lord of Tirumalai: 
दिवि सूर्यसहस्रस्य भवेद्युगपदुत्थिता । 
यदि भाः सदृशी सा स्याद्भासस्तस्य महात्मनः ॥ 
'If the effulgence of a thousand Suns arose at once in

of the Seven Hills'. 
2. (Tel.) Vaddikasula Vada stands for one who obtains 'interest'. The Lord has the reputation of collecting his dues from his devotees with interest. Balaji is the name by which the Lord is known in Northern India.

SRI VENKATESWARA 
the firmament, it would be somewhat like the brilliance of this great Atman'. 
(Gita, Chap. 11-Verse 12). 
We worship the Lord and pray to him- 
१. वन्दे श्री नयनोत्पलस्य शशिनं शेषाचलाधीश्वरं ब्रह्माद्यचतपाद- पद्मयुगलं लोकैकनेत्रोत्सवम् । वासिष्टः सनकादिभिर्मुनिगणैः संस्तूयमानं सदा भक्तानामखिलार्थदायिनमहं श्रीवेङ्कटेशं गुरुम् ॥ 
Lord of Seshachala! Our obeisance to you who art the Moon to the lotus-like eyes of Lakshmi! Thy twin feet are worshipped by Brahma! Thou art the Only Delight of the Universe! Thou art always extolled by the Sages Vasista, Sanaka and others. Thou Bestower of all that is desired by the devotees! Oh! Lord Venkateswara ! Our GURU! We bow down to thee! 
Before attempting a description of the Lord's images in Tirumalai it would be relevant to acquaint ourselves briefly with some of the important principles of the Hindu Silpa Sastra connected with the making of Vishnu images in particular. 
The sastras have laid down the standards of excellence and beauty and the defects to be avoided while making images of the Lord. Their idea of beauty has been sublime. According to Sukraniti an image is ideal only when it elevates the lookers on to a higher plane of action and medi- tation. The eyes of images have particular appeal to the devotee and as such the eyes should be perfect bespeaking satisfaction and should neither be directed upwards, down- wards or closed. According to the Pratima Mana Lakshanam, the mouth should be 'slightly smiling, pleasant and possessed of all good signs'. The mouth should also not be sculptured as one that is 'passionate, impetuous, wrathful, sour, bitter or circular'. Again, according to the Pratima Mana Lakshanum 
1. Maricha Samhita (Sanskrit) 'Sri Vimanarchana Kalpa'- 
Madras (1927). 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
9 
the head sculptured like an umbrella brings prosperity. Eternal prosperity is assured by an image with beautiful lines of eyebrows and forehead. The neck has to be conch like, the body in the posture of a lion, the arms like the trunk of an elephant, and thighs like the plantain tree. A beautiful belly and beautiful feet are excellences in any idol. Sukraniti has also laid down that images should be beautiful. Considerable freedom was however given in many cases to silpins to carve images following the Sastraic principles as far as possible. They had however to keep in mind that "the characteristic of an image is its power of helping forward contemplation and yoga'1 while making idols and images. 
The Vishnu image is generally shown in one of the three poses-Sthanaka, Asana and Sayana. Sthanaka is the standing pose, Asana is the sitting pose, and Sayana is the reclining pose. According to the Maricha Samhita,2 Vishnu images can be classified into nine categories; the three main categories are Sthanaka, Asana and Sayana. There are sub-categories for each main category such as (1) Uttama, (2) Madhyama and (3) Adhama. The Gheranda Samhita however is said to mention on Lord Siva's authority that there are eightyfour hundred thousand asanas and mentions in particular, of thirty two important poses, such as Sidham (Perfect posture), Padmam (lotus posture), Yogasana, Swastikasana, Varasana etc. These are perhaps the varieties of combinations in which images can be made, depending on the type, mudra etc. The agama sastras such as the Vaikhanasa Agama, the Pancharatra Agama, the Tantrasara and Vishnu Dharmottaram lay down the principles on which Vishnu images are to be made. There are four 
1. Principles of Indian Silpa Sastra by Prof. Phanindra Natha 
Bose, The Punjab Sanskrit Book Depot, Lahore, 1926. 2. Maricha Samhita (Sanskrit), Sri Vimanarchana Kalpa, 
Madras (1927). 
10 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
important types of Vishnu images, namely Yoga, Bhoga, Veera and Abhicharika types. Yogis worship Yoga images whereas Bhoga (which literally means pleasures) images are worshipped by householders and give worldly pleasures - bhoga. The Veera images give power while Abhicharika varieties are worshipped to conquer enemies and bring misfortune to foes. Vishnu images have usually four arms, the ayudhas or weapons held in the hands usually being the Chakra, Sankha or conch, the Bow and Arrows and the Gada or club. The other weapons of Vishnu include the Khetaka or the shield, the Katari or the sword, the Parasu or the battle-axe, Hala or the plough, and sometimes the whip (for Gopi-Krishna) images. The hands are usually in the Abhaya, Varada and Katyavalambita poses. It is however relevant to note here that the Yogasastra recognises twenty five kinds of mudras in images. The Abhaya pose is the one in which the Lord holds his hand aloft, with the right palm facing the devotee with all the fingers of the hand pointing upwards. This is a pose in which the Lord assures his protection to the devotee - relieving him from fear i.e. Bhaya. The Varada hasta is the pose in which the Lord holds his hand (left hand) with the palm facing the devotee with all the fingers of the hand pointing downwards. This pose signifies that the Lord is a giver of boons. According to the Mayasastram however, "when the tip of the thumb is placed at the end of the breast, it is the attitude of abhaya or protection of the God. If the hand is on the waist, it is said to be the attitude of giving vara or boon". The Katyavalambita hasta pose is the one in which the Lord keeps his hand (left hand) on the kati or waist. This is a pose 
1. Also mentioned in Gheranda Samhita. The mudras are Maha- mudra, Nabho-mudra, Uddiyana, Jalandhra, Mulabandha, Mahabandha, Mahavedha, Khechari, Viparitakari, Yoni, Vaj- roni, Saktichalani, Tadagi, Mandavi, Sambhavi, Panchadharana (Five dharanas), Pasini, Kaki, Matangi and Bhujangini.THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
11 
that indicates that the Lord's protection and grace is available to those worshipping him with constant devotion. Accord- ing to another interpretation, it indicates that the Samsara is knee-deep only to those devoted to Lord's feet. Vishnu images are shown to carry several ornaments such as the Padma or the lotus, Kireeta or the crown, Makara Kundalas or crocodile ear ornaments, Keyura or armlets, Kankanas or wristlets, Udarabandhas or ornaments around the waist, Katibandhas or ornaments around the waist and the hips, the sacred thread etc. There is usually a mole on the right chest called the Srivatsa, and a garland reaching up to the knees called the Vaijayantimala. Sree Koustubam is a gemstudded jewel on the chest which is sacred to Lakshmi. The consort of the Lord is usually carved or exhibited near the Srivatsa and the Sree Koustubam. 
The Yoga idols are usually in the Yoga mudra and not unusually accompanied by the consorts Bhudevi and Sridevi. In the sthanaka or standing pose, the Lord has four arms with Chakra and Sankha in the upper hands at the rear: two other lower hands in the front usually are in the Abhaya and Katyavalambita poses. Bhudevi and Sridevi are usually present. 
The Yoga idol in the sitting pose has also four arms of white colour with a crown of hair on the head. The Lord is usually in the Yogasana pose. The Yogasana is described by Gheranda Samhita as follows: while squatting "turn the feet upwards, place them on the knees; then place the hands on the asana with the palm turned upwards; inspire and fix the gaze on the tip of the nose". The best example perhaps of the Yogasana murthi in the sitting pose is that of Sri Badari Narayana of Badrinath, also called the Badri Vishal form. In Badrinath, the Lord is seated on a lotus in the yogasana pose with two hands in the yogamudra. The 
12 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
thumbs of both the hands touch one another while the palms of the hand face upwards in this mudra. The upper and rear arms hold the Chakra and Sankha. The consorts of the Lord along with others such as Garuda, Thumbura, Narada, Vigneswara, Brigu and Markandeya are also shown usually along with the yogasana murthi. The sayana or reclining yoga idol has only two hands; the right hand is usually placed near the pillow under the head whereas the left hand is shown alongside the sayana murthi. The typical yogasana murthi in the reclining pose is that of Sriranganatha of Srirangam in South India. 
The Bhoga idols of Lord Vishnu have also four arms with Sankha and Chakra in the standing pose. The hands are usually in the Abhaya, Varada or Katyavalambita poses. Sridevi and Bhudevi are also usually carved beside the Bhoga type idol. In the sitting pose, the Bhoga idol is usually seated in the Sukhasana, an asana in which the right leg is shown as hanging down and the left leg folded to touch the right thigh. It is also usual to depict the consorts of the Lord as sitting on the left thigh of the idol. There are usually four arms in such idols, the upper ones holding the Chakra and Sankha while the two hands at the front are in the varada, abhaya or simhakarna poses. Where Sri Lakshmi is shown as seated on the lap of the Lord, it is usual for the Lord's lower left hand being shown in the pose of encircling her waist. The hand rests on her left thigh. The simhakarna pose is the one in which the fingers of the Lord are folded to resemble the ears of a lion. The reclining Bhoga murthi has usually two or four arms and a well-built body. In this type of idol, Sri Lakshmi is usually depicted as seated near the shoulders of the Lord. Brahma is usually shown as seated near the navel of the main idol; Bhudevi is usually seated near the feet of the Lord. 
The Veera type of Vishnu image in the standing pose is 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
13 
similar in several aspects to that of the standing yoga murthi. The Veera murthi however stands alone or is surrounded by the Sun, the Moon, Brahma and Siva. The sitting Veera murthi is usually seated on a simhasana, i.e. a lion-seat. His left leg is bent whereas the right leg is extended in front. Sri Lakshmi and Bhudevi are also shown. The reclining Veera murthi has four arms of which one arm is used as a pillow (usually the right hand) whereas the other right hand holds the Chakra. One of the left hands is stretched out parallel to the reclining murthi while the second left hand holds the conch. 
The Abhicharika variety idols are usually dark-com- plexioned and look vicious to show ugra. The standing variety is depicted as having two or four arms. The sitting Abhicharika idol is usually shown on an unornamented plain pedestal and has either two or four arms. The reclining idol is usually shown lying on the floor with two four arms. The consorts of the Lord are not in attendance in this type. Rishis and the demons Madhu and Kaitabha are also shown in the act of supplication.1 
The above gives an account of the making of Vishnu images in general. The Lord's image at Tirumalai is a Sthanaka or standing figure. The worship in the Sri Venka- teswara temple at Tirumalai follows the Vaikhanasa Agama. It is therefore relevant and necessary for us to inform our- selves about the rules laid down in the Vaikhanasa Agamas for Vishnu images in the Sthanaka pose. According to Maricha Samhita,2 Vishnu images in the Sthanaka pose can be Yoga, Bhoga, Veera or Abicharika murthis. In each type, there would be three sub-types-namely the Uttama, Madhyama and Adhama Murthis. 
1. A Hand Book of South Indian Images, by T. N. Srinivasan, 
T. T. Devasthanam, 1954. 
2. Maricha Samhita, Sri Vimanarchanakalpa 
Venkateswara Mudranalaya, Madras, 1927. 
(Sanskrit), Sri 
14 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
The Yoga-Uttama-Sthanaka murthi should be Syama (dark) in colour with four arms; the Sankha and Chakra should be exhibited; one of the right hands should be in the Abhaya hasta pose, while one of the left hands should be in the Katyavalambita pose. On the right, the sages Brigu and Markandeya should be seated. Brahma with four arms should be shown as standing near the right side facing north with his ayudhas such as Aksha Mala and Kamandala; two of his hands should be in the Abhaya and Katyavalambita poses. Siva should be shown facing south with four arms, two of them in the Abhaya and Katyavalambita poses and the other two holding a Mriga (Deer) and a Parasu (Axe). The complexion of Siva should be white. 
In the Yoga-Madhyama-Sthanaka murthi, the Pari- varadevathas Brahma and Siva should be absent. 
In the Yoga-Adhama-Sthanaka murthi, the sages Brigu and Markandeya should be absent. 
The Bhoga-Uttama-Sthanaka murthi should be dark in colour with four arms. The Sankha and Chakra should be exhibited. One of the right hands should be either in the Abhaya or Varada Hasta poses. One of the left hands should be in the Katyavalambita simhakarna pose. Siva and Brahma should stand to the right; Sri Devi or (Lakshmi) with a lotus in her left hand and a Prasaritha Dakshina Hasta (extended right hand) should stand to the right while Bhudevi with Prasaritha and Dhruthotpala hastas should stand to the left. The Parivara Devas and sages include Brigu, Veda Vyasa, Maya, Samhladini, Vyayagini, Thumburu, Narada, Kinnara, Mithuna, Yaksha, Vidya- dhara, Sanaka, Sanath Kumara, Surya and Chandra. 
In the Bhoga-Madhyama-Sthanaka murthi, Thumburu, Narada, Yaksha and Vidyadhara should be absent. 
In the Bhoga-Adhama-Sthanaka murthi, the Sun and the Moon should be absent. 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
15 
The Veera-Uttama Sthanaka murthi should be Syama in colour with four arms. The sankha and chakra should be exhibited. Brahma and Siva should be on the right and left sides. Similarly Brigu and Markandeya, Kishkinda and Sundara, Vyajaka, Sanaka, Sanath Kumara, the Sun and the Moon should be present. 
In the Veera-Madhyama-Sthanaka murthi, Kishkindha, Sundara, Sanaka and Sanath Kumara should be absent. 
In the Veera-Adhama-Sthanaka murthi, the Sun, the Moon and the sages should be absent. 
All the Abhicharika-Sthanaka murthis should have either two or four arms. The colour of the murthi should be grey (Dhuma Varna-colour of smoke). The murthi should have dark lips, withered or dried up limbs, should exhibit thamo Guna; should have eyes turned upwards. The Parivara Devas, Brahma etc. should be absent. It should however be surrounded by Paisachas or evil spirits, and should not have any auspicious qualities. 
With the foregoing knowledge in the background, we may now attempt a description of the Lord's idol at Tiru- malai. In the first instance it should be stated that the Lord manifested himself on the Tiruvengadam hill in the greatness of his love and mercy to come to the succour of his devotees on the earth. 
The story of his manifestation in a Divya-Vimana on the banks of the Swami Pushkarini is described in detail in several of the Puranas such as Varaha Purana, Bhavish- yottara Purana etc.; these legends are described in detail in Chapter VIII of this book. The book Sri Venkatachala Mahatmyam which is an anthology of the accounts of the Lord's leelas described in the various Puranas informs us that Lord Srinivasa manifested himself in a celestial Vimanam on the Swami Pushkarini located on the Lord's Kridadri or Seshachalam and that this Kridadri or Venkatachalam 
16 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
was specially brought down to earth from Sri Vaikuntam, the Lord's abode. The manifestation was in the yuga of the Sweta Varaha Kalpa. In this age Brahma was the first to worship the Lord. The Lord then became an idol assuming the archavatara in Kaliyuga. This idol was discovered in an ant-hill by one Tondaiman with the assistance of one Rangadasa and was first worshipped by the Sage Vaikhanasa. If the above claim that the Lord's image is a self-manifested figure is accepted, the question of examining it from the standpoint of the Agamas and Silpasastras does not arise at all. It would however be interesting to study the Lord's image first and examine how far it conforms to or differs from the standards of construction, excellence etc. laid down in the Agamas, particularly the Vaikhanasa Maricha Samhita. 
As stated earlier, the Lord's image is in the Sthanaka or standing pose. The Lord is standing on a high lotus pedestal. The height of the Lord has never been recorded, but cannot be less than nine feet from the tip of the 'makutam' i.e. the crown to the bottom of the lotus pedestal as can be seen clearly on a Friday when 'abhishekam' is done after removing all the gold 'kavachas' etc. ornamenting his figure. The priest performing the worship, about five feet tall, standing on a stool two and a half feet high is not able to perform 'abhishekam' on the Lord's Makuta without assistance from a priest standing behind the idol. Keeping in view this fact and that the lotus pedestal on which the Lord stands is itself below the floor in the sanctum-sanctorum, the Lord's figure from the tip of the crown to the base of the lotus pedestal, must measure between nine and ten feet. 
The image is perfection personified, and it would not be incorrect to say is the most handsome and perfectly featured idol of India. 
According to tradition, the idol is a manifestation of 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
17 
the Lord in Salagrama Sila. It is worthy of note here that granite and a kind of reddish igneous rock are the stones abundantly available in the area around Tirumalai hills. The idol of the Lord is liberally anointed frequently with civet or 'punugu' oil; this application of oil makes the idol dark in colour and does not enable us to make an accurate assessment of the material of the image. The material of the idol could be granite or the red igneous rock mentioned above. There is a famous Sivalinga in the village of Gudimallam, seven miles from Renigunta in Chittoor District. The Sivalingam called Shri Parasurameswara made of reddish igneous rock is unique in the whole of India in that it is about five feet in height and has on it in front a beautiful image of Siva carved in bas-relief. This image is sthanaka, having two arms, the right one holding a ram by its hindlegs and the left a waterpot while a battle axe rests upon the left shoulder. On the head a covering resembling a turban of plaited hair is worked out. The face is very lovely, chubby with prominent nose, high cheek bones and oblique eyes (Virupaksha). There are kundalas in the ears and ornaments of good workmanship round the neck. It has no yagnopavita. Though there is the representation of a small cloth round the loins, the image is nude. The figure stands astride on the shoulders of a demon Apasmarapurusha. Behind the image is the linga portion which has a close resemblance to phallus. This linga is considered very old and is said to belong to the first century before Christ.1 This is an early Satavahana period's sculpture. The village of Gudimallam is about 30 miles from Tirumalai. The material of the Lord's image at Tirumalai appears to be similar to that of the material of the Sivalinga at Gudimallam. Both the images have a high polish and shine like mirrors. 
1. A Hand Book of South Indian Images by T. N. Srinivasan, 
T. T. Devasthanam, 1954. 
18 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Reddish igneous rock is also a stone native to Tirumalai. It may therefore be possible that the Lord's image is made up of reddish igneous rock. 
We may now attempt a description of the body of the Lord of Tirumalai. The Lord's majestic beauty is best seen at the time of the Friday Abhishekam when the jewels and other paraphernalia do not cover our view of Him in his celestial glory. It is at this time that we see the eyes of the Lord in the 'Sama dristi pose' showering divine grace on the expectant devotees assembled to offer worship to Him. The eyes neither look up nor downwards, but straight into the devotees' eyes (as laid down in the Sukraniti). The face is beaming with joy and wears a smile. An aura of medi- tation and abundant love is the constant atmosphere around the Lord, wearing a makuta or crown which is more than 20 inches high. The Lord's figure is richly adored with flowing locks of hair or jata juta and some of these locks of curly hair rest on his shoulders. The nose is delicately carved and is neither prominent nor flat. The mouth of the Lord is also exquisitely shaped. According to the 
the Pratima Mana Lakshanam the mouth 'should be made slightly smiling, pleasant and possessed of all good signs. One should abso- lutely avoid the construction of the mouth which is pas- sionate, impetuous, wrathful, sour, bitter or circular. The Lord's mouth is in a smiling pose and would conform to the idealised standard laid down by the above work. The chin and the ears are carved proportionately. The ears wear beautiful ear ornaments. The chest of the Lord is magnificent in cut and size and should, if measured, be between 36 to 40 inches in width, while the waist would be between 24 to 27 inches. The neck is conch-like and the body in the pos- ture of a lion and exquisitely shaped. The belly is also beautifully modelled. The Lord's image has four arms, the 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
19 
upper two being carved to hold the chakram and the conch; the chakram and the conch are not integral parts of the main idol. The upper right arm holds the Sudarshana Chakra; the upper left arm holds the Panchajanya, the conch of Lord Vishnu which is blown by Him in times of war. The lower right hand of the Lord is in the Varada Hasta pose, while the lower left hand is in the Katyavalambita pose. Actually the fingers of the left hand rest on the left thigh, with the thumb of the hand almost parallel to the waist line. The pose as explained already is a reminder to the devotees that those who worship the Lord's feet with constant devotion will be blessed and protected by Him.1 While the idol itself is not exactly stand- ing in the tribhanga pose, as in the case of Sri Rama idols, the body near and below the waist has taken a slight tilt to the left and the knees themselves are bent and open slightly outwards, giving the idols peculiar grace and charm. Mother Lakshmi is carved on the right chest of the Lord in the sitting pose and is an integral part of the mula murthi. The yagno- pavitam and a set of four necklaces or ornaments of the neck can also be clearly made out on the idol. The arms have armlets with finger-like projections which appear to be Nagabhushanams or Cobra-ornaments. A cobra is also said to be coiled around the Lord's right arm. The figure is depicted as wearing a dhoti from the waist downwards, while the upper portion is not covered by any dress or Vastram. The nipples of the Lord on his chest are button- like and are prominent. There is however a Katibandham or waist band and this waist band is about 2 inches thick. The legs and feet of the Lord are beautifully shaped, strong and lissome. As indicated earlier, both the knees are bent 
1. We may remember here that the Srivaishnavites' invocation to the Lord in the Dvaya Mantra is Sriman Narayana Charanau 
Saranam Prapadye; Srimathe Narayanaya Namaha. 
20 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
and open slightly outwards, giving the stately figure charm and grace, that words cannot adequately describe. The feet are models of perfection and have ornaments near the anklets. The Lord's image has on the shoulders marks resembling 'scars made by the constant wearing of the bow and a pack of arrows'. 
The perfectly modelled image of the Lord is personified Beauty and is indeed a Divya Manohara Murthi. The image bears some resemblance to the famous Bodhisattva Padmapani painting in Cave 1 of the Ajanta Hills. The Bodhisattva has of course only two arms; but if the Bodhi- sattva is shown with four arms and with his neck erect and his eyes in the Sama Dristi pose instead of the Avanita Dristi pose, the Bodhisattva would appear as if it is a study of the Lord at Tirumalai. The resemblance can only be ascribed to chance and no inference whatsoever can be made linking up the two. 
We may now examine how far the image conforms to the yardsticks of construction, exhibition etc. laid down in the Maricha Samhita. 
The figure is syama in colour, and has four arms. not a Yoga-Sthanaka Vishnu, as the right hand according to Maricha Samhita has to be an Abhaya Hasta. In fact, the Lord only exhibits a Varada Hasta and not an Abhaya Has- ta. The other deities mentioned as essential for a Yoga- Sthanaka image are absent. The image is also not a Bhoga or a Veera or an Abhicharika murthi as one or the other of the characteristics mentioned in the Maricha Samhita are not satisfied by the Murthi. 
One can therefore argue that the Lord's image is a super- Agamic manifestation, that is, one that existed before the Agamas came into being. Another plausible argument is that the image is a class by itself and not a simple VishnuTHE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
21 
image. It is one that combines in itself the Hari and Hara aspects of the Supreme Spirit. 
Nature of the Image of the Lord of Tirumalai:-Is Lord Venkateswara of Tirumalai a manifestation of Vishnu or Siva? Is he a manifestation of Subrahmanya Swamy or Shakti? These are the questions which time and again agitate the minds of the devotees who intensely believe in the Lord of Tirumalai as the One and Only God who can bestow on them Dharma, Artha, Kama and Moksha. Sri Vaishnavas hold that the Lord is Vishnu and Vishnu alone, while Saivas who see in Him their Istha Daivam Siva, hold with equal enthusiasm that He is Siva. Some again hold the view that the image is that of Skanda. A few Sakti worshippers also believe that the deity is Parasakthi. Several devotees of the Lord are again of the view that He is the combination of Vishnu and Siva elements and is in fact a Hari-Hara murthi. There are again others who hold that the Lord Venkateswara is manifestation of Paravasudeva. 
The controversy about the Hari and Hara nature of the Lord was actually argued by the contending parties in the 11th or 12th centuries A.D. before a Yadava Raja of Nara- yanavanam in the Jayakonda Chola Mandalam. The Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala1, a Sanskrit work of the 12th century, describes in detail in its first three stabakas a scholarly discussion carried on at a high intellectual level long long ago, between the Vaishnavites led by Sri Ramanuja and the Saivites about the nature of the Lord's image at Tirumalai-whether it was a Saivite image or the image of Vishnu. The very first Yadava Raja who is noticed by us in 
1. Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala is said to have been written by 
Anantarya, a disciple of Ramanuja (1017-1137 A.D.). 
Anantarya may therefore be taken as a contemporary of Ramanuja and the book might have been written in the first half of 12th century. 
22 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
the Tirupati-Tirumalai Devasthanam Inscriptions is one Tirukkalati Devaraya whose reign has been calculated to begin in the year 1191 A.D. The discussions reported in the Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala could not have taken place before him as Sri Ramanuja is said to have been born in the year 1017 A.D., lived for 120 years and expired in the year 1137 A.D. It is possible that the intellectual combat took place before an early ancestor of Tirukkalati Devaraya. A brief summary of the arguments advanced by both the con- tending parties is given below: 
The image of the Lord in Tirumalai is that of Skanda or Kumara Swami. The holy Pushkarini on Tirumalai it- self is therefore called Swami Pushkarini. Swami is really an appellation commonly used for Skanda or Subrahmanya Swami. The shortened form 'Swami' is used instead of calling the Pushkarini 'Kumara Swami Pushkarini'. It is not unusual for nouns to be used thus in the shortened form. For example, the nouns Bheema Sena and Sathya Bhama are usually used in the shortened forms as Bheema and Bhama. The Vamana Purana also mentions that Skanda performed penance on Venkatachala on the advice of his father Siva. The image of the Lord at Tirumalai is that of Skanda and not Vishnu. Venkatachalam is primarily a Varaha Kshetram and as Vishnu has already manifested himself here in the Boar form, the image of the Lord at Tirumalai can only be that of Skanda. 
The image of the Lord has not got the characteristic weapons of Vishnu such as the Chakra and the Sankha. Skanda came to perform tapas on the banks of the Push- karini and has manifested himself without his weapons or his extra hands. The Vaishnavites had taken possession of the temple of Subrahmanya and had converted it into a Vishnu temple. The presence of the matted locks or Jata 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
23 
Jutas on the image and the Nagabhushanams (snake ornaments) prove that it is a Saivite image. Puja is also done in the temple with 'bilva' leaves; bilva leaves are used only for worship in Saivite temples and not used for Vishnu worship. The image has also a crescent mark on the head indicating that the image is Siva if not Kumara Swami. 
The arguments said to have been put forward by Sri Ramanuja on behalf of the Vaishnavites may be now given briefly. 
The term 'Swami' need not apply to Kumaraswami. It only means that Swami Pushkarini is the 'Swami' in 'thirthams' and thus a Lord among holy thirthas. The holy thirtha associated with Kumaraswami is Kumaradara. The Varaha Purana also mentions that Swami Pushkarini is the sporting tank of Vishnu brought down to earth from Vaikuntam. All the puranas state conclusively that the deity on the Pushkarini is the Lord of Lakshmi. Skanda came to worship Vishnu at Tirumalai. It would also be wrong to hold that the deity that came to worship Vishnu is deified on Venkatachalam. The Lord's image has four arms and one face whereas Skanda should normally have six faces and twelve hands. In the verses of Nammalvar the deity is recognised clearly as Vishnu. The image does not bear the Sankha and the Chakra because Lord Vishnu in his infinite divine grace parted with the weapons to assist his devotee Tondaiman Chakravarthi in battle against his ene- mies. This is confirmed in Brahmanda Purana. While it is true that worship is done in the temple with bilva, it can be argued that bilva is acceptable to Lakshmi and what is acceptable to Lakshmi is naturally acceptable to her Con- sort, Vishnu. Vishnu images can also have jata jutas as laid down in the Bhagavata. The nagabhushanams cannot be strong arguments as Padma Purana mentions of instances 
24 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
of Vishnu wearing Naga figures. The Bhavishyottara Purana also mentions that Naga jewels were presented by Akasa Raja to his son-in-law and the wearing of these jewels cannot be said to be peculiar. The image of the Lord has Vakshastala Lakshmi and also the Srivatsa mark. Even the early Alwars who stressed the Saiva features of the image speak of him as Narayana only. The Puranas also mention that Brahma and Rudra came to worship Sri- nivasa when He manifested Himself on Tirumalai. It is true that the image has a mark on its face resembling that of the crescent-moon. This however cannot mean that this is a Saivite character. The image is certainly not even a Hari-Hara manifestation and is clearly a Vishnu figure. 
The Ithihasa Mala then states that the local ruler was fully convinced by the arguments and gave his award in favour of the Vaishnavas. Before this, to put the matter beyond doubt, Ramanuja is said to have requested the King that the weapons of Vishnu and the weapons of Siva should be made and left in the temple and the matter left for finali- sation by the Lord of Tirumalai Himself. Accordingly the weapons of Vishnu and Siva were placed before the Lord and the temple doors closed. That night Ramanuja is said to have approached the Lord as Adisesha and prayed to the Lord to assume the Vaishnavite weapons. And Lo! when the temple doors were opened the next morning, the Lord was found wearing the conch and the chakra! This legend from the Ithihasa Mala informs us that the controversy about the Hari-Hara character of the Lord existed even in the 12th century. Sri Ramanuja, himself a Srivaishnava, seems to have made up his mind to worship the Lord at Tirumalai as Lord Vishnu. The Saivite pandits of his age were certainly no match to this genius and Sage of Visistha Advaita, and there is no wonder that he won in the battle 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
25 
25 
of wits, puranic lore and yogic powers and arranged for the worship of the Lord of Tirumalai from thenceforth as VISHNU. 
Another current view about the Lord of Tirumalai is that the image is the representation of a female deity. Hol- ders of this view usually quote a verse from the Devi Bha- gavatam which describes the Lord as 'Sri Venkateswari', the only deity for Kaliyuga. Certain practices in the temple such as the use of turmeric paste for the abhishekam of the Lord, the fact that the abhishekam for the Mula Vigraham is done on Fridays, the fact that 'tomala seva' is called 'Bhagavati Aradhana' and that Srisukta mantras are read during this abhishekam of the Lord are relied upon by them to make out a case that the Lord is Shakti or Kali or Durga. Uniquely enough, the temple Vimanam has the 'Sakteya symbols' on it-the lion-and not the Garuda, as one would normally expect. The Lord is called 'Bala' or 'Balaji' in the north and as 'Bala' stands for a girl, these contend that the presiding deity at Tirumalai is the Divine Prakriti Her- self. 
Again, out of the several conflicting views about the nature of the Lord's image, the view that the Lord is a Hari- Hara Murthi is perhaps nearer the Truth, as the image com- bines the qualities of Vishnu and Siva. The fact that the deity is primarily being worshipped as Lord Vishnu from Ramanuja's time in the 12th century, would not in anyway detract the case of the supporters of the above view, for the Lord has always been considered even during this period as Vyakta Vishnu and Vyakta-Avyakta Siva. The peculi- arities of the Lord's image make us pause and consider whether the Lord of Tirumalai is really not a combination of Siva and Vishnu. The image has four arms; the upper two arms do not really have the chakra and sankha as inte- 
26 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
gral parts of the image. These would appear from tradi- tion as well as from the traditional accounts of the Sri Ven- katachala Ithihasa Mala to have been fixed on the Lord's hands by Sri Ramanuja during one of his three visits to Tirumalai in the 12th century A.D. The characteristics of the image namely the crescent-moon mark on the makuta of the Lord, the Jata Jutas or matted locks common to Siva, the nagabharanams and the cobra on the right arm of the Lord, certain other peculiar customs followed in the temple such as worship by using 'bilva', the presence of the Sakteya symbols on the Vimanam of the temple, the fact that a Garuda shrine came to be constructed in the tem- ple only after the fifteenth century are all strong pointers that the Lord has several 'Saiva' Gunas or aspects. 
Several Puranas no doubt say that Venkatachalam is a Vishnu Kshetram. The Sveta-Varaha Murthi and the Lord Venkateswara are the deities of Tirumalai. The acceptance of the Lord as a 'Hari-Hara Murti' will in no way conflict with the claim of the Puranas that Venkatachalam is a Vishnu Kshetram. The Lord's Hill also bears the name Srisailam and Srisailam is a hill sacred to Lord Siva. Accord- ing to Sri Dave "In the famous Dwadasa Jyotirlinga stotra of Sri Sankaracharya, there is a description of Mallikarjuna on the Srisaila Mountain which is part of the Seshachalam Hills and Srisaila itself is used as a name for Tirupati." It is true that the Puranas view the range of hills from Tirupati to Srisailam, famous for its Mallikarjuna Temple as one unit. According to Dr. S. Krishnaswami Aiyangar? "some of the Puranas dealing with the holy shrine at Tiru- pati liken the range of hills, the Nallamalais and their con- tinuation, to a huge cobra lying about at ease, of which al- 
1. Immortal India, Vol. I, by J. H. Dave, Bharatiya Vidya Bha- 
van, Bombay, 1957. 
2. History of Tirupati, Vol. I & II, T. T. Devasthanam, 1952. 
THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
27 
most the end of the tail is marked by the great Siva shrine of Srisailam not far from the Krishna river, beyond the 16th degree of north latitude and a considerable way down the river from Kurnool. Coming up on the back of this huge snake one next strikes the Vishnu shrine of Ahobilam which is at the beginning of the trunk of the snake. At the back of the hood stands the hill of Tirupati, and almost at the opening of the mouth again the great Siva shrine of Kala- hasti. This Puranic description is rather graphic and accu- rate, although the features marked happen to be only the four holy places of Kalahasti, Tirupati, Ahobilam and Sri- sailam. We mentioned already the cluster to the west being called Seshachalam hills; probably the name is given from this feature of the whole group lying about like a snake, or rather a cobra lying at ease in the Sun. This curious feature probably had its own share in creating the peculiar impres- sion upon people journeying from a distance, and is pro- bably responsible for the names given which, at first sight, may seem fanciful". 
From an objective and scientific point of view the evidence in the Puranas again should be taken with caution. Every research student in the Puranas is aware of the innu- merable interpolations and changes made in them and hence the legendary accounts in Puranas can only be informative and not authoritative. The Puranic evidence and statements have also no relevance and need no consideration when we accept the claim that the Lord's image is a self-manifested One. 
We have then the more reliable evidence of the Alwars. It is well-known that the early Alwars who were themselves Vaishnavas have in the songs composed by them conceded the 'Hari-Hara' aspects of the Lord of Tirumalai. Thus Poygai Alwar sings about the Lord of Tirumalai as follows: 
28 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
"His name, Aran (Hara) and Naranan (Narayana); His vehicle, the bull and the bird; His word, the book and Marai; His house of residence, the hill (Kailasa) and the waters (ocean); His function, destruction and protection, the weapon in hand, the trident-spear and the disc. His form, though One, is Fire and dark cloud."1 
There are several other verses by the above Alwar in the same strain. 
The two other Alwars, Bhutam Alwar and Pay Alwar, have sung several verses in the same strain. 
"He has in his form the flowing matted locks (jatai) the high peaked crown, the shining dagger and the chakram, the snake coiling him, and the golden sutram round his waist. In Him, my Father on Tirumalai, the two forms have grace- fully blended into One."2 
"The golden-hued Holy One (Siva) and the One who rose to the skies to encompass this earth, Trivikrama Vishnu, the two may pass as separate Ones, but the one is really in 
the other."3 
Nammalvar himself concedes the unity of the One- that the Lord of Tirumalai is a combination of Rudra, Brahma etc. 
"He has merged in Himself Rudra, Brahma and Lakshmi after losing their separate entity. He pierced by his growth the highest heavens to encompass the creation. Is there a Higher Divinity than that of Vishnu ?"3 
Some of the views of the nature of the Lord Venkate- swara's image have been indicated above. Devotees who flock to Lord Venkateswara at Tirumalai worship him in 
1. History of Tirupati by Dr. S. Krishnaswamy Aiyangar, (1952) 
page 30.

pati by Sri T.K.T. Veera Raghavacharya (1953).

THE LORD IN THE TEMPLE 
29 
various forms as their 'Ishta Daivam' as Lord Vishnu, Skan- dha or Subrahmanya, Siva, Sakthi, a combination of the Tri- murthis Brahma, Vishnu and Siva, or as Venkateswara a combination of Hari and Hara. There can be no finality in the arguments of any of these devotees nor is it desirable to pursue such arguments of conflict and impertinence when the Lord is One and Only One. 
In the Krishna Avatar Tattvam,' the Lord has made it clear that there is no difference between Hari and Hara and that they are One. From the Gita, we learn that there is one God and that this Lord is manifested everywhere. 
From the Hindu point of view, the Lord is ONE and answers devotees by whatever name the Lord's Chaitanyam or Active Principle is invoked. Lord Venkateswara is this Absolute One. 
Lord Venkateswara thus is the Lord of Lords, the One and only God. And as the One, He answers those who turn to him and pray to him. He is our father in Heaven of the Christian faith, the Allah of the Islamic faith, and the One that goes by various names in the Hindu pantheon and the numberless other paths in the Universe that lead to Him. He is the Narayana, the All pervading One, a Katyavalam- bita Murthi, who protects and releases all from bondage. Sarva Dharman Parityajya Mamekam Saranam Vraja is the message of this Absolute One, who says 'Seek Me the One for thy protection and I shall deliver thee from sin and bondage.' May this Lord bless us all; Sarvey Jana Sukhino Bhavanthu. 
1. Krishna Avatar Tattvam by Janamanchi Seshadri Sarma in XI Volumes (Telugu). According to the Padma Purana, Vishnu, Brahma and Siva are considered to be one; devotees to Vishnu are enjoined to possess Bhakti for Vishnu, Siva and Brahma, as they are all manifestations of the One. 
CHAPTER III 
THE DEITIES OF THE GARBHA GRIHA 
In the preceding chapter we have mentioned that the figure of Sri Lakshmi forms an integral part of the Mula Murti of Tirumalai. The Lord of Tirumalai is decorated daily with flowers and ornamented with several precious jewels, apparel and other paraphernalia. These latter in- clude a big emerald 3" in diameter called 'Meru Paccha' considered to be the biggest emerald in the world, a gold crown decked with precious gems, makara kundalams or gold ear-rings shaped like a crocodile, gold covers studded with precious gems for the conch and the chakra, a neck- lace containing a pair of tiger claws, mounted in gold case, a makara kanti, two nagabharanams or cobra jewels in gold for the arms of the Lord, a Lakshmi-garland-a big necklace with 108 Lakshmi images depicted on it, a Tulasi necklace, a Salagrama garland which contains Salagramas encased in gold on which the Sri Venkateswara Sahasrana- mas are engraved, a vanki-like gold ornament near the knees, a pair of gold anklets, several gold necklaces with alluring patterns and designs, a cobra-jewel just below the chest portion, gold covers studded with gems 
for the 
Varada and Katyavalambita hasthas, a sword by name Suryakatari, a yagnopavitam in gold, gold covers for the two feet of the Lord, an exquisitely carved Dasavatara belt around the waist depicting the Lord's ten incarnations (the Suryakatari actually hangs from this belt) along with two gold sculptured plaques of Shri Lakshmi and Padmavati on the chest of the Lord. The Shri Lakshmi plaque is exhibit- ed on the right chest of the Lord. It is relevant to note here that the Vishnu Purana mentions how Shri LakshmiTHE DEITIES OF THE GARBHA GRIHA 
31 
became the Vakshasthala Lakshmi (the one that resides on the chest of Lord Vishnu). It is said that Shri Lakshmi came out of the cosmic ocean when the Devas and Asuras were churning it, to obtain nectar or Amritam, and that Shriman Narayana then made Her his spouse. This epi- sode is beautifully described in the Narayaneeyam as follows1 : 
जगदीश भवत्परा तदानीं कमनीया कमला बभूव देवी । 
अमलामवलोक्य यां विलोलः सकलोऽपि स्पृहयाम्बभूव लोकः ।। Oh! Lord of the Universe ! Then arose (out of the ocean) the charming Goddess Kamala that is yours. Seeing her spotless and pure (beauty), everyone became unsteady and longed for her. 
त्वयि दत्तहृदे तदैव देव्यै त्रिदशेन्द्रो मणिपीठिकां व्यतारीत् । सकलोपहृताभिषेचनीयं ऋषयस्तां श्रुतिगीभिरभ्यषिञ्चन् ॥ 
To that Goddess whose heart was lost to thee, Deven- dra offered a (Manipeetam )—a throne of gems; the sages crowned her chanting the Vedas (using) the articles brought by all. 
अभिषेकजलानुपातिमुग्धत्वदपाङ्गैरवभूषिताङ्गवल्लीम् । मणिकुण्डलपीत चेलहारप्रमुखैस्तामम रादयोऽन्व भूषन् ॥ 
Decked by the Gods with ear ornaments set with gems, Peet vastrams and other such articles, She is bedecked by thy sidelong lovely glances falling on her simultaneously with that of the water drops of the coronation ceremony. 
वरणस्त्रजमात्तभृङ्गनादां दधती सा कुचकुम्भमन्दयाना । पदशिञ्जितमञ्जुनपुरा त्वां कलितव्रीडविलासमाससाद ।। 
The Kucha-Kumbha-Mandayana, that is the one whose gait is slow due to the burden of her large-sized breasts

holds the marriage garland with the buzzing bees clustering round it, and with her anklets tinkling charmingly on her feet, She approached you with coyness. 
गिरिशद्रुहिणादि सर्वदेवान् गुणभाजोऽप्यविमुक्तदोषलेशान् । अवमृश्य सदैव सर्वरम्ये निहिता त्वय्यनयाऽपि दिव्यमाला ॥। Discarding Gods like Shiva, Brahma and others with merit but who are not beyond all blame, She garlanded you, the eternal 'Ramya' murthi. 
उरसा तरसा ममानियैनां भुवनानां जननीमनन्यभावाम् । त्वदुरोविलसत्तदीक्षणश्रीपरिवृष्ट्या परिपुष्टमास विश्वम् ॥ 
And you quickly honoured her with thy embrace; and the mother of the Universe thinking ever of Thee and showering her glances on thee and shining on thy 'Vaksha' or chest made the worlds prosperous. 
The Sri Lakshmi-murti on the Lord's chest is repre- sented as is usual with such figures represented individually -as a seated image in the Padmasana or lotus pose. She has four arms; the two upper arms hold lotus buds; the right lower arm is in the abhaya pose, the one in which she guarantees freedom from fear to her devotees. The other lower arm on the left is in the Varada hasta pose. 
As already indicated, Sri Padmavathi who is no other than Adi Lakshmi adorns the Lord's left chest. Padmavathi is worshipped as Alarmel Mangai in the Padmavathi tem- ple at Tiruchanur near Tirupati. She is the Mangai who emerged out of the lotus pond at Tiruchanur on a lotus and hence her name Alarmel Mangai or Padmavathi. This figure has also four arms; the upper two arms hold lotus buds while the lower arms are depicted in the abhaya and Varada hasta poses as in the case of Sri Lakshmi. Accord- ing to the Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala-a Sanskrit work of the 12th century A.D., Sri Ramanuja, the propounder of 
THE DEITIES OF THE GARBHA GRIHA 
33 
the Visishtadvaita doctrine of philosophy, made a gold neck- lace containing the image of Padmavathi and put it round the neck of the Lord of Tirumalai. This must have been a 
few years before his death in 1137 A.D. 
In addition to the above murthies, there are several other deities in the sanctum sanctorum of the temple. They are described below in brief. 
1. Manavalapperumal or Bhoga Srinivasa (THE KAUTUKABERAM): This is a silver replica of the mula vigraha; the conch and chakra however are not detachable in this murthi as in the case of the mula vigraha. The idol has a six-pointed yantram installed on it. This image is the Bhoga Srinivasa. It is in the standing or sthanaka pose with four arms; the two upper arms hold the chakra and the conch, the two lower arms are in the Varada and Katyava- lambita poses. This idol receives all the Bhogams and Upacharams or services in the temple and is an idol which by its very nature can give material prosperity and such-like boons to devotees. This idol is to be seen near the mula vigraham. 
This image was made and consecrated in the temple in the year 966 A.D. The donor was the Pallava Queen Samavai and the inscription recorded at the instance of the princess on the northern wall of the first temple prakaram, which is perhaps the earliest in Tirumalai, runs as fol- lows1:- 
"Hail, Prosperity! In the 14th year of the reign of Koppatra-Mahendra-Panmar, I, Samavai alias Kadavan- Perundevi, queen of Sattivitankan (Sakti-vitankan) alias Sri Kadapattigal and daughter of Pallavapperkadaiyar, founded this charity, so as to last as long as the moon and the sun endure, having arranged for the daily propitiation 1. THE DEITIES OF THE GARBHA GRIHA 
35 
all these (ornaments) were presented and ablutions were also conducted and who was consecrated, comprises the pieces of land purchased from the Sabhaiyar of Tiruchchu- kanur situated in the Tirukkudavur-nadu in Sri Venkata- kottam and from Lakshmananambi residing in the Matham and for which was also paid money to the God Tiruvilan- koyil-Perumal and which was made tax-free; the piece of land purchased from the Sabhaiyar; the pieces of land known as Nandi-Erippatti, Madupputtai, Lakshmananambi- Adaikonda-Nilam (land held by Lakshmananambi) Madhu- sudan-Aviyarai and Purushottamanpatti, aggregating to 3 patti of land; measured with the Kadikkai-Kol (measuring pole) and reckoned to be 3000 Kuli as equated with the rod of 16 spans; and this land has been purchased after pay- ing the purchase money to the Sabhaiyar and to the deity, and made tax-free and presented to serve for the propitia- tion of Manavalapperumal. 
"All these services the managers of the temple (Mada- patyam) on Tiruvenkatam shall have conducted and the Sabhaiyar shall protect the land from being taxed. 
"The feet of those that protect this charity shall be borne on my head. The protection of the Srivaishnavas (is sought for this). This is the writing of Sattandai." 
The Pallava Queen Samavai also endowed the temple with lands in Tiruchchukanur (the present Tiruchanur)1 towards expenses for conducting a festival twice a day prior to Margali-tirudvadasi (Mukkoti-dvadasi). 
The idol was consecrated in the Snapana or Tiruvi- lankoyil mantapam of the temple. The consecration was done by keeping open a power line consisting of gold and silver cords in between the mula murthi and the bhoga murthi. The power line exists to this day. The daily ritua- 
1. T. T. Devasthanam Inscriptions, Vol. I, pp. 16-17. 
36 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
listic worship of the Lord of Tirumalai appears to have be- come more regular from the time of the consecration of this Bhoga Srinivasa. 
This image gets abhishekam every morning to the ac- companiment of mantras. It is anointed with perfumed oils and given baths of milk, sandal-wood paste, honey, turmeric etc. At night this deity is put to bed during a seva called the Ekantha Seva, when the idol is placed on a silver cradle- bed in the Sayana mantapam or Mukha mantapam before the Garba Griha; milk etc. are offered during the cere- mony to the accompaniment of music and songs composed originally by Tallapaka poets. Scented sandalwood paste is also applied to the image at night every day as a daily ritual. The Ekantha Seva Bhogam is performed to the idol only for eleven months, Dhanurmasa Ekantha Seva being reserved for the Krishna idol described separately. 
2. The processional image of Sri Malayyappan Swami or Malaikuniyaninran Perumal:-(Utsava Murthi):-Shri Malayyappan Swami or Malai Kuniya Ninran Perumal is the processional deity of the Lord of Tirumalai at present. The image is a standing figure and a replica of the mula-vigraha. The two upper arms hold the chakra and the conch; the lower right arm is in the Varada hasta pose and the lower left arm in the Katyavalambita pose. The image is richly decked with precious gems and jewels made of platinum and gold. The image does not stand alone; Sridevi and Bhudevi images accompany the Utsava murthi. The deity is about 3 feet high, and stands on a lotus pedestal and platform (about 14 inches high). To the right of the deity is Sridevi, in the standing pose. The deity would be about 29" high, the pedestal on which she is standing being about 4" high. This is also a pancha-loha figure. The deity has two hands, the left hand being in the Kataka hasta pose, that is the one 
THE DEITIES OF THE GARBHA GRIHA 
37 
in which she holds a flower, usually a lotus. No lotus how- ever is held by her actually. The right hand hangs loosely rather gracefully and the fingers are held in the Gajakarna pose (Prasaritha Dakshina Hastha). The other consort of the Lord, Bhudevi is also in the standing pose and is similar to that of Sridevi. The only difference is in the pose of the hands. Here it is the right hand that is in the Kataka hasta pose and the left hand hangs loosely and gracefully and the fingers assume the Gajakarna mudra-(Prasaritha Vama Hastha). The earliest mention of this image on the temple inscriptions is near about the period 1339 A.D. One inscription found on the north wall of Sri Varada Raja Swami shrine in Tirumalai temple, reads as follows: "Hail, Prosperity! In the 19th year of the reign of Tribhuvana Chakravarthi Nayanar Sriranganatha Yadavarayar, for the purpose of Malaikuniyaninran Perumal and Nachchimar be- ing seated in the chediyarayan-Pumantapam....etc.." indi- cates conclusively the existence of the processional image Malai Kuniya Ninran and his two consorts, about the period mentioned. According to legend, the image with the ac- companying consorts was found in a mountain glade and hence the name Malai Kuniya Ninran. Malai Kuniya Nin- ran also appears to be one of the names by which the Lord of Tirumalai was known in the early ages i.e. the One who stood on a hill which 'bowed very low'. According to Sri Veera Raghavacharya,' this image is no other than the Pan- charatra Tiruvilankoyil Perumal of Tiruchukanoor and the Koluvu Srinivasa (described below) the image of Tiruman- tra Salaipperumal of Tiruchchekanoor. 
3. Ugra Srinivasa or Venkatatturaivar:-(Snapana Murthi). Ugra stands for anger and this image of Ugra 
1. History of Tirumalai in two Volumes by Sri T. K. T. Veera 
Raghavacharya, published by the T. T. Devasthanam, 1953. 
38 
ages. 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Srinivasa typifies the destroying aspect of the Lord. This is perhaps the earliest image of Tirumalai temple not taking into account the mula-vigraha of the Lord, and was the processional image of the Lord of Tirumalai in the earlier The image is about 14 feet high and is standing on a pedestal of about 7" high. Later it was superseded by the Malayappan idol. The chakra in this image is held by the deity in a slightly tilted condition which could perhaps be called the Prayoga Chakra. The Prayoga Chakra Mudra is one in which the chakra is held as if it is to be used im- mediately. The deity has two consorts, Sridevi and Bhudevi, both standing figures, having two hands, one in the Kataka hasta pose and the other hanging loosely and gracefully. The Nachiyar images would be about 16 to 18" high. The image at present is brought out in procession only once a year and that too rather clandestinely before the dawn on Kartika Dwadasi day. According to the Vaishnava archa- kas, the rays of the Sun should not touch this murthi; if that happens, it will culminate in the end of the world! 
4. Koluvu Srinivasa (Bali Beram):-This image is the Koluvu Srinivasa or the Durbar Srinivasa, who holds court everyday in the temple in the Tirumamani mantapam. His functions are akin to that of a guardian deity in the temple. He supervises and keeps himself posted with all receipts of revenue of the temple. He is not interested in the expenditure aspect and thus combines in himself only some of the more pleasing functions of the Accountant-General and the Public Accounts Committees of today! 
The image is sometimes called Bali Beram. This deity holds court after Tomala Seva is performed to the Lord of Tirumalai. The deity has a Simha Toranam around Him. He has a silver chair and a gold umbrella. The almanac 
THE DEITIES OF THE GARBHA GRIHA 
39 
is daily read out to Him. The collections made in the Hundi of the temple are reported to Him everyday. 
5. Sri Krishna Image:-The Garbha Griha has also a beautiful image of Lord Krishna in the Navanita Nritya pose along with his consort Rukmini. In the Navanita Nri- tya pose, Lord Krishna is shown as a dancing child standing on his left foot on a lotus pedestal; the left leg is gracefully bent at the knee. The right leg is lifted and does not rest on the pedestal. It is also bent at the knee. The right hand holds a ball of navanita or butter, while the left hand is stretched out gracefully in a beautiful dancing pose. 
Mention of this image is made in the temple inscriptions first in the year 1100 A.D. The other inscriptions refer to the offerings made to Krishna on Sri Jayanti day and also during the several Bramhotsavams. This image receives the Ekantha Seva in the temple in Dhanurmasam instead of the Bhoga Srinivasa idol. The Bhoga Srinivasa receives the Ekantha Seva services during the rest of the year. 
6. Sri Rama image with Sita, Lakshmana and Sug- riva: Four copper icons of Sri Raghunatha (Rama), Sita. Lakshmana and Sugriva also find place in the sanctum sanc- torum. These images were in all probability installed in the 12th century A.D. by Ramanuja, as the Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala mentions a legend about the installation of the deities. Sri Raghunatha is however mentioned in the available inscriptions only in 1476 A.D. The image of Sri Rama is in madhyama tala and in the tribhanga pose; the left arm is raised to hold the bow and the right arm gracefully slopes downwards to hold an arrow. The image is in the standing pose and has a kireetam or crown on it. The image of Mother Sita is to the right of Sri Rama and is in the standing pose. Her right hand is gracefully ex- tended downwards; her left hand holds a lotus bud. The 
40 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Lakshmana or Ramanuja image is a smaller replica of the Sri Rama figure, with crown and jewels to suit. The Sug- riva figure is in the standing pose and in the anjali hasta pose -the one in which the hands are folded in an attitude of prayer. 
Beautiful copper figures of Angada and Hanuman are exhibited separately in the Ramar Medai platform outside the shrine in the Antarala mantapam. Opposite them are equally beautiful copper images of Vishwaksena, Ananta and Garuda which are worth seeing. 
7. The Chakrattalwar:-The Sudarshana Chakra or Chakrattalwar also figures in the sanctum sanctorum. This deity always precedes the Utsavamurthi in processions and his functions may be likened to those of a pilot-God going on his security rounds before the Lord comes out in majestic godly procession.CHAPTER IV 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
It shall be our object in this chapter to make a brief study of the temple as it exists at present and appreciate the several beautiful bas-reliefs and sculptures in the temple. The historical background will also be studied in brief. The Vimanam on the sanctum of the temple famous as the Anandanilaya Vimanam and the sanctity of the Vengadam hill will be discussed separately. 
The sketch given at the end gives the topography of the temple proper. It is seen that the temple covers an area of about two acres and is rectangular in shape (with sides 413 feet long and 263 feet wide). There are two Gopurams-the Outer and Inner Gopurams-in the temple on the two outer prakarams. The circumambulatory path between the outer and inner Gopurams is the Sampangi Pradakshinam and the circumambulatory path between the inner Gopuram and the main shrine is the Vimana Pradak- shinam. A third and incomplete path around the sanctum is the Mukkoti Pradakshinam. 
The outer Gopuram:-The main gateway of the temple called Mukha Dwaram is about 11 feet wide. Direct ins- criptional evidence is not available to fix the date of cons- truction of this Gopuram. An early inscription1 in Tamil of the Queen of Yadavaraya2 Vira Narasimha Devar found 
1. T. T. D. Epigraphicals Series, 11/7. Vol. I Early Inscriptions 
(1931) No. 381 T.T. p. 116. 
2. The Yadavarayas flourished in Tondamandalam comprising of 
the areas of Kalahasti, Tirupati, Karvetinagaram, and portions of Chingleput and Chittoor Districts of the present Andhra Pradesh and Tamil Nadu States. They were devotees of the Lord of Tirumalai and Lord Siva of Kalahasti.
