SRI VENKATESWARA 
on the right side front face of the Gopuram reads as follows: "Hail, Prosperity: In the 12th year of the reign of Tribhu- vanachakravartigal Sri Yadavaraya Vira-Narasingadevar, (his) queen Yadavaraya Nachchiyar presented 64 cows and 2 bulls for 2 Nandavilakku for Tiruvenkatam-udaiyan in the month of Panguni. This (service) shall continue as long as the moon and the sun last. May (this) the Srivaishna- vas protect!" This inscription would indicate that the Go- puram was in existence or was in the process of construction in the early years of the thirteenth century. The Gopuram has apparently been renovated in the modern times and the figures on the Gopuram at present appear to be covered by white cement and look crude and modern. A figure of Gandhiji, the father of the Indian Nation, also finds place on the Gopuram as it stands at present. 
PRATIMA MANTAPAM 
Statue of Venkatapatiraya and others:-On entering through the Mukha Dwaram, the first big mantapam to our left is the Ranganayakula mantapam. A small mantapam, adjacent to the Ranganayakula mantapam which may be called the Pratima Mantapam houses several statues of royal devotees. On the left side of the mantapam (to our left as we enter the temple) are three statues. One is a copper statue of King Venkatapatiraya. This figure is about five feet high and is standing on a copper pedestal about two feet high. The crown worn by the King is about 12 inches high. The figure is bare-chested and wears large-sized pearls in the ears. A Rudrakshamala in two rows is worn on his neck. He wears an embroidered loin cloth. He has a beau- tiful katibandham around his waist. Portions of his ankles and legs are naked. There are a pair of anklets on the figure which is in the anjali hasta pose. In T.T. Devastha- 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
43 
nams Inscription' No. 671 found on the south wall of the third Prakara in Tirumalai temple, King Venkatapatiraya is mentioned as 'Sriman Maharajadhiraja, Rajaparameswara, Sri Veera Pratapa, Sri Veera Sringarayadeva, Maharaya'. He was a great devotee of Lord Venkateswara, his Guru being one Kumara Tatacharya. He was known as Venkata-I and was an Araveedu dynasty king. He governed the Chandra- giri Rajya as Viceroy for some years. He did not make any endowments to the temple in his name. He however, seems to have issued coins bearing the figure of the Lord of Tiru- malai with the inscription 'Sri Venkatesaya Namaha'. He was at Chandragiri in the year 1572, and the statue itself can be safely said to belong to the 16th century. 
Next to this statue of Venkatapatiraya, are two beau- tiful statues of the Vijayanagar King Achutaraya and his Queen Varadaraji Amma. Both these figures are in the anjali hasta pose. The King's statue is about 64" high and wears the Vaishnavite vadagalai mark. There are three necklaces on his neck, one of which appears to be made of pearls. He has a loin cloth gracefully tied around his waist covering his figure up to the knees. He wears ornaments on his hands, shoulders and feet. The figure of his consort is about 54" high from the top of platform on which she is standing. This is the most graceful and beautiful statue- barring of course the Mula murthi-found in the Lord's temple. Graceful garments-the saree and the blouse cover her body without hiding her magnificent chest. The figure is very delicately carved. The feet are slender and beautiful. Her hair is prettily made up. Her eyes are in the samadrishti pose and the circular ear-rings she wears augment her beauty. She wears three necklaces and several other ornaments on her shoulders, waist, etc.

Venkatapatiraya's time, pp. 26-41.

SRI VENKATESWARA 
King Achyutaraya was the half brother and successor of Sri Krishnadevaraya on the throne of Vijayanagar. He was a devotee of Lord Venkateswara and made several rich endowments to the temple. He appears to have visited the temple at least thrice. He was actually coronated once in the Tirumalai temple. During his first visit to the temple (31-1- 1533), he performed archana for the Lord himself while the priests recited the Srinivasa sahasranamam. This is recorded in an inscription1 on the south wall of the third prakara in Tirumalai temple, as follows:-"Hail, Prosperity! this is the silasasanam executed by the trustees of Tirumalai, as ordered by the emperor Achyutaraya Maharaya on Friday, combined with the star Asvini, being the 7th lunar day of the dark fortnight of Kumbha month in the year Nandana, current with the Saka year 1454, while Sriman Maharaja- dhiraja, Rajaparameswara, Sri Veera Pratapa, Sri Veera Ach- yutaraya Maharaya was ruling the earth. ing the archana for Sri Venkatesa and while the archakas (temple priests) were reciting the Srinivasa Sahasranamam (1008 appellations of God) in the presence of the queen Varadaraji Amma and her son Kumara Venkatadri. 
After the performance of the Kapila-pasu (Kapilapasu- mahadana) and Svarna-Varsham (svarnameru-mahadana) the emperor presented the above-mentioned ornaments with snapana-tirumanjanam (holy bath) to Sri Venkatesa." 
In addition to the above, Achyutaraya made several other presents to the temple. He instituted two new festivals, Lakshmidevi Mahotsavam for the Lord of Tirumalai and his consort, and the punarvasu tirunal for Sri Rama, Sita and Lakshmana deities in the temple. He constructed the granite steps for the Kapila thirtham in Tirupati. It is he that renamed Kapila thirtham as Chakra thirtham. He also deputed a number of damsels from Tirupati to serve Lord Venkateswara. His Queen Varadaraji Amma gifted away several villages to the temple. 
Statues of Krishnadevaraya and his consorts---There are three statues of Krishnadevaraya and his consorts on the northern side of the Pratima Mantapam referred to above. These figures are made of bronze. The figure of Krishna- devaraya is about 68" high from the top of the crown to the lotus pedestal on which it is standing. The figure is bare-chest- ed and is in the anjali hasta pose. The fingers on each hand wear two rings. There are ornaments on the hands, shoulders and the legs. The figure of the King is warrior- like and extremely graceful. The figure of Chinnadevi standing to his right is the best bronze figure in the temple. The queen is shown as wearing four necklaces in addition to the Mangala sutram. The blouse she wears, the bangles on her hands and the other ornaments give her peculiar grace 
46 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
as well as charm. The figure of Tirumaladevi also in the anjali hasta pose is equally beautiful. 
Sri Krishnadevaraya (1509-1530), the great Vijayanagar emperor, visited Tirumalai and worshipped the Lord at least seven times. He considered Lord Venkatewara as his patron God and dedicated his work Amukthamalyada to Him. He however was a devotee of Siva also and made several endowments to the Siva temples in the country. His first visit to the temple was in the year 1513 A.D. when he presented a crown of gold set with navaratnas (nine varieties of gems) and twenty five silver harati plates to the Lord. This is recorded in the inscription1 dated 10-2-1513 A.D. which reads as follows: "Hail! May it be prosperous! On the 5th day of the bright fortnight of the Phalguna month, in the year Angira (Angirasa), corresponding with the Sali- vahana Saka era 1434, Sriman Maharajadhiraja, Rajapara- mesvara, Sri Veera Pratapa Sri Veera Krishnaraya, Maharaya presented a Kiritam (crown made of gold) set with nine kinds of precious stones to God Tiruvengadanath (Sri Ven- katesa) in the following description:-

1,339 pearls weighing 163 ratulu or carats,

During his first visit as well as his second visit, which was also in the year 1513 A.D. he was accompanied by his two Queens. During his third visit he granted five villages to the Lord for nitya-naivedya purposes. The Sanskrit prasasti or introduction which precedes some of his inscrip- tions singing his praises, gives glowing accounts of his ancestry and some of the deeds performed by him. Sri Kri- shnadevaraya performed several religious charities at Srisai- lam, Ahobilam, Bezwada, Rajamundry, Amareswara, Kala- hasti, Tirupati, Kanchi, Srirangam, Kumbakonam, Rameswa- ram, Gokarnam, etc. He constructed gopurams at Kalahasti, Tiruvannamalai, Chidambaram and put up pillars of victory at Simhachalam and Srikurmam. He was the greatest of the Vijayanagar emperors and was himself a man of letters and a patron of literature. His military exploits are too well 
48 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
known to need detailed mention here. During his other visits he made several endowments to the Lord. He had the Divya vimana of the Lord's temple gilded with gold. Of his two queens, Tirumaladevi was the senior and Chinnadevi the junior. He married Annapurnadevi after Chinnadevi's death. From Nuniz however we learn that he had four wives; as an oriental potentate he must have espoused several other damsels. It is relevant to note here that Sri Krishna- devaraya's inscriptions in the temple are recorded in Telugu, Tamil, as well as Kanarese, indicating his overlordship of the area where these languages were spoken. 
Sri Krishnadevaraya was the second son of Narasa Naika. He was crowned in the year 1510 A.D. He led successful campaigns against the Chiefs of Kanchi, Gangaraja of Ummettur in East Mysore, and Bijapur. He captured Udayagiri and Kondaveedu forts. He made three campaigns against Kalingas in the years 1513-1516 A.D. On the defeat of the Gajapati of Kalinga, Sri Krishnadevaraya assumed the title of Gajapati Sapthanga Harana i.e. the destroyer of the seven limbs of the Gajapati's sovereignty. He won a deci- sive battle in his conflict with Bijapur in the year 1520. The Portuguese chronicler Nuniz describes the camp of Krishnadevaraya at Raichur battle as follows: "It is a mystery how there is abundance of everything at Raichur, a barren and sandy place....Indeed no one who did not understand the meaning of what he saw will ever dream that a war was going on but would think that he was in a prosperous city." Nuniz also mentions that the King's table service ware con- sisted only of silver and gold vessels. Sri Krishnadevaraya was a staunch vaishnava, but he was also devoted to Lord Siva and patronised Saivas, Madhvas and Jains. He loved his people and was zealous in the advancement of literature and art. According to Paes, Sri Krishnadevaraya is the 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
49 
most feared and perfect King that could possibly be, cheer- ful, dispassionate and very merry; he is one that seeks to honour foreigners and receives them kindly, asking about all their affairs whatever their condition may be. He is a great ruler and a man of much justice, but subject to sudden fits of rage.... He is by rank a greater lord than any, by reason of what he possesses in armies and territories, but it seems that he has (in fact) nothing compared to what a man like him ought to have, so gallant and perfect is he in all things." 
Vijayanagar attained the height of prosperity and pres- tige during Sri Krishnadevaraya's time and he is the greatest of all the South Indian sovereigns. 
Ranganayakula Mantapam:-To the left of the Pra- tima Mantapam, we notice a big mantapam called the Ranga mantapam measuing about 108' north to south, and about 60' east to west. A portion of this mantapam is at present being used for the office of the Peshkar of the T.T. Devasthanams and for the T.T. Devasthanams Publications Sales Depot. The Lord's kalyanotsavam is sometimes per- formed in this mantapam. The mantapam has a small shrine about 12' square which is kept closed always. This shrine was used to worship an utsava vigraham of Sri Ranganatha of Srirangam when the idol came as a refugee to the Tirumalai temple in the early years of the 14th cen- tury when the muslims were overrunning South India with iconoclastic sword and fire. The mantapam therefore is presumed to have been constructed about the year 1340-1350 by the local yadavaraya ruler Sri Ranganatha Yadavaraya. There is however no direct inscriptional evidence to show that Sri Ranganatha Yadavaraya constructed this mantapam. 
1. These have been shifted after the recent accident, (in the 
year 1967 AD.). 
50 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
An inscription1 on the door jamb-wall of Padikkavali gopura in the temple indicates that King Yadava Sri Ranganatha constructed 'in his sixteenth regnal year a royal mansion for himself on the Venkata hill, abounding in flower gardens filled with honey-sucking bees....' His reign has been cal- culated to have begun during the period between 1336 and 1360 A.D. As he was the local monarch it is possible that the mantapam was constructed by him, especially when credence is given to the local legend that Sri Ranganatha utsava vigraham was brought and worshipped in the temple in the troubled years of that period. Dr. S. Krishnaswamy Aiyangar2 has also stated on the authority of Kovil Olugu-an account of the history of the temple at Srirangam-that the utsava vigraham in the temple was brought to Tirumalai hills for safety during the period of threatened Muslim invasion. 
The Ranga mantapam is rather bald in sculptures and does not deserve special mention in this direction. Only the upper portions of a few pillars are worth noticing. The mantapam however is very large indicating that congrega- tional worship was perhaps made in this premises, in the olden days. 
Tirumalaraya mantapam:-The Tirumalaraya manta- pam adjoins the Ranga mantapam and is about 108' long and 40' wide. Actually the mantapam can be demarcated into two portions, the southern portion constructed on an elevated basement and the northern portion at a much lower level. The southern portion was constructed by Saluva Narasimha in the middle of the 15th century. The southern mantapam is higher than the Rangaraya mantapam. This mantapam is unique in that it has a beautiful four-pillared sub-mantapam 1. T.T.D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, Early Inscriptions (1931) 
page 138. 
2. History of Tirupati by Dr. S. Krishnaswamy Aiyangar.THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
51 
in its middle. The pillars of this sub-mantapam are complex structures comprising of four pillars each-the major pillar being about 6" square and the three minor pillars being almost circular in shape having about sixteen facets. The pillars depict warriors riding horses standing erect on their hind-feet over lions. The minor pillars which emit musical sounds when struck are themselves mounted on miniature lions. Some of the riders have attendants carrying umbrellas to denote their royal status. The bas-reliefs on the sub- mantapam are those of Sri Narasimhaswamy killing Hiranya- kasipu, Sri Lakshmi Narasimhaswamy in the standing pose giving abhaya to Prahlada, Srinivasa, Krishna dancing on the snake Kaliya, Sri Vishnu with Lakshmi seated on his lap, Vishnu riding on the Garuda and Sri Rama as Dhanurdhari Rama i.e. Sri Rama holding the kodanda or bow. The main pillars of the main mantapam show riders on lions while the lions themselves are depicted as standing on ele- phants. A particularly impressive bas-relief on one of the mantapam pillars is that of a couple in which the husband is consoling his grief-stricken wife. Another unique bas-relief depicts the Gopi-vastrapaharanam scene. Sri Krishna is shown as perched on a tree. He holds the clothes of the Gopikas in his hands. The Gopis in the nude are portrayed around the tree. One Gopi is shown as praying to the Lord with her hands held aloft on her head in the anjali hasta pose while another with more initiative is trying to climb the tree to retrieve her clothes. Another impressive bas-relief is that of Lord Krishna playing on the flute. He holds the flute with two hands. The figure which has two more hands holds the chakra and sankha (upper right hand holds the chakra while the upper left arm holds the sankha). A mesmerised herd of cows is also shown as listening to the divine music of Lord Krishna. 
52 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
The southern or inner portion of the mantapam was constructed in the middle of 15th century for celebrating the Unjal festival (Swing festival). A festival called Dolamahot- sava-anna-anjal-tirunal' was instituted by Saluva Narasimha in the year 1473 and we can safely presume that this festival was celebrated in the elevated southern portion of the Tiru- malaraya mantapam. This mantapam was renovated later by Araviti Bukkaraja Ramaraja Srirangaraja Tirumalaraja in the 16th century and hence the name Tirumalaraya manta- pam. The relevant inscription2 citing the construction of the mantapam is to be found on the east wall of the first Gopuram in the second prakara of Tirumalai temple. This inscription states that Tirumalaraja made provision for the celebration of the Dolotsavam or swing festival for Sri Venkatesa in the Salivahana Saka year 1483 (corres- ponding to 17th December, 1561 A.D.). In the prasasti, (eulogy) it is mentioned that he conquered the Turkish Chief Tulukkanam Alla. Other festivals like Vasanthotsavam were celebrated in this mantapam during Tirumalaraja's time. Sri Malayappan, the processional image of the Lord, holds durbar in this mantapam to this day during the Brah- motsavam festival days. 
Saluva Narasimha, the king who constructed the southern portion of the Tirumalaraya mantapam-also called Saluva- raya mantapam, commenced his career as the hereditary Governor of Chandragiri. He was the ablest warrior of the Vijayanagar empire. When the Government at headquarters became weak due to the assassination of Virupaksha II, he became the leader of a bloodless Saluva revolution and usurped the throne of Vijayanagar. This however turned 
1. T. T. D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. II, Inscriptions of Saluval 
Narasimha's time, 197 T.T. pp. 89-98.

53 
to be a revolution in the interests of the public. Saluva Narasimha strengthened the empire in many ways and be- came a great ruler. He was a lover of horses and imported horses from abroad. It is said that he was so liberal as to pay for such imported horses which died on the way to India. According to Nuniz, his total reign was for 44 years i.e. dur- ing the period between 1450 and 1493. He was a devout Vaishnava but also performed abhisekams to Lord Siva on the Shivaratri day. He is the author of the famous Rama- bhyudayam. He was a patron of literary gems such as Pina- veerabhadra and Rajanatha Dindima. His titles in the early years were 'Mahamandaleswara Medinimeesaraganda Katari Saluva'. In later years he assumed royal titles such as 'Maha- Arasu' (Great King) and Rajadhiraja (King of Kings). Saluva actually means hawk and he was indeed a hawk among men. Ferista has described him "as a powerful Raja possessing the country lying between the Karnatic and Telin- gana, extending along the sea coast to Machilipatam." Tabataba has said that "owing to (his) numerous army, and the extent of the dominions he was the greatest and most powerful of all the rulers of Telingana and Vijaya- nagar with all the apparatus of war-elephants like moun- tains of iron" and defences "like the ramparts of Alexander."1 
Saluva Narasimha as well as his Queen Srirangambadevi along with other members of the royal family made several endowments to the temple. Thus in the year 1453 an en- dowment was made to maintain Ramanujakutams in the temple for the free supply of prasadams to pilgrims. Pro- vision also was made among others for the supply of the

In inscription No. 66 T.T. found on the southern wall of the first prakaram of the Tirumalai temple, it is also shown that the King granted five villages in the Chandragiri Rajya for the Tiruvidaiyattam for Tiruvenkatamudaiyan. Ar- rangements were also made for the feeding of pilgrims visit- ing the temple. In inscription bearing No. 273 G.T. the king described as Sriman Sarvabhauma (universal monarch or emperor) Nrisimha, the son of Sri Veera Gundakshiti- pati, is shown as having installed "an image of the propi- tious Shri Lakshmi Narasimha Swamy on the pathway over the Seshadri hill on 20th April, 1485." 
The second king who is connected with this mantapam bearing his name, was the first of a ruling race belonging to the Araveedu family of the Vijayanagar kingdom. He ac- quired power as the Chief Minister of Sadasivaraya in 1565 A.D. after the death of his brother Aliya Ramaraja who was a great power in those days in the politics of Vijaya- nagar. The Hindu army was led in the famous battle of Rakshasa-Thangadi (battle of Tallikota in the year 1565) by this Tirumala, his brother Aliya Ramaraja and Venka- 1. T.T.D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. II, Inscriptions of Saluva Narasimha's time, Inscription No. 54 T.T. found on the western wall of the first prakaram of the temple, pp. 53-55. 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
55 
tadri. The battle was lost as a result of the treachery of two muslim generals in the Hindu army, according to Caesar Frederic. Ramaraja who fought valiantly though he was aged 96 years at the time, was wounded and killed in the battle. Tirumalaraja who himself lost an eye in the battle became the regent for the kingdom of Vijayanagar. The capital was transferred by him to Penukonda in Anantapur district in the year 1567. Sadasivaraya, the sovereign, was murdered in the year 1570 (by one of the sons of Tirumala- raya) and Tirumalaraya ascended the throne of Vijayanagar at Penukonda. In spite of this blemish on his escutcheon, we can say that Tirumalaraya tried his best to uphold and retrieve the prestige of the Vijayanagar empire which was at a low water mark as a result of its crushing defeat in the battle referred to. He was also a man of letters. His name is immortalised in the mantapam in the temple. 
The statues of Lala Khamaram, his mother and wife:- In a portion of the Tirumalaraya mantapam we find three statues of Lala Khamaram (Thoder Malla), his mother Matha Mohana De and his wife Pitha Bibi. Their names are inscribed on the shoulders of the figures. The first two are brass-plated figures on wooden blocks while the third is a brass figure. Not much is known about these devotees who are all in the standing pose in the anjali hasta mudra. The figures have no aesthetic value and are also getting eroded by the action of time. Only the figure of Pitha Bibi is better preserved. She is shown as wearing a blouse and a vastram from the waist downwards. Her cir- cular ear ornaments are impressive. The figure of Lala Khamaram has an impressive moustache and beard. 
The other constructions in the Sampangi Pradakshinam on the north, south and west consist of mantapams or rooms which are used for storing provisions, idols of the Ratham 
56 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
of the Lord etc. Thus the jaggery room is a room where jaggery is stored, the pattadai is a place where the Lord's prasadams are given and the Ramanuja Kutam a place where food is freely distributed to pilgrims. The Padipotu, a room next to Ramanuja Kutam, is a place where sweets, fried and baked preparations are made for the Lord. The room next to the Padipotu is the yamanothurai where flower garlands are made to specifications for the Lord of Tiru- malai. The Theppai samans or the articles of the Lord's Theppai are also stored in the rooms on the northern por- tion of the Sampangi Pradakshinam. 
The four small mantapams in the Sampangi Pradak- shinam standing in the four different corners, were construct- ed by Saluva Narasimha in the 15th century. These mantapams were used formerly for seating the processional image of the Lord of Tirumalai and to offer food or Naive- dyam to the deity for distribution later freely to all pilgrims. 
AINAMAHAL AND PULA BHAVI 
Moving a full round in the Sampangi Pradakshinam starting from the Tirumalaraya mantapam, we notice an important mantapam to the left of the Dhvajastambham (while we face east), which is the Aina Mahal or Hall of Mirrors. The Pula Bhavi is located to the north of this Mahal. 
The Pula Bhavi:-Poovu in Telugu means a flower and Pula Bhavi means the Well of flowers. It is a step-well used for disposing of the used flowers and flower garlands of the temple. This practice was initiated by Sri Ramanuja when he visited the Tirumalai temple in the 12th century A.D. The connected legend is described in the Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala. According to this authority, Chakravarthi Thondaman was the local ruler in Kaliyuga. He was in the 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
57 
habit of visiting the Lord of Tirumalai by using a subterra- nean passage called the Narasimhabilam. On one occasion the King pursued by his enemies rushed to the temple at mid-night for safety. The Lord of Tirumalai was sporting at this time with his consorts Sridevi and Bhudevi. Seeing the king entering the holy premises, Sridevi hid herself in the Vakshasthala of Srinivasa while Bhudevi sought refuge in the Pula Bhavi or Well of flowers dug by one Rangadasa for watering the flowers in the Nandanavanam or garden attached to the temple. This legend is mentioned in the Varaha Purana also. Sri Ramanuja after listening to this legend set up images of Srinivasa and Bhudevi on the step- well, and ordained that the used flowers, flower garlands, Tulasi etc. of the temple should from thence be dropped in the well in honour of Bhudevi. 
Ainamahal:-It is a mantapam of later origin (37'.6 X 43') with a mukha mantapam before it. The mahal as it stands at present is fixed with huge reflecting mirrors. The mantapam is also fitted with innumerable electric lights. The illuminated Ainamahal is a beautiful sight. 
Dhvajastambha Mantapam:-The next important man- tapam in the Sampangi Pradakshinam which deserves our notice is the Dhvajastambha mantapam, which is the second mantapam as we enter from the Mukhadwaram or main door of the temple. The mantapam gets its name from the Dhvajastambham or the Lord's flag-pole installed in the mantapam. The Lord's Balipitham is also to be seen in this mantapam. 
This mantapam is unique in that it has some lovely bas-reliefs on its pillars which most pilgrims miss. A unique bas-relief is that of a Trimurthi figure with four arms. The two upper arms hold the chakra and the sankha (chakra is in the right hand and the sankha in the left). The lower 
58 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
right hand is in the abhaya hasta pose. The lower left hand is in the katyavalambita pose. The deity is seated on a bird which could either be a swan or a peacock. The figure is shown in the sukhasana pose with the right leg stretched on the bird while the left leg is shown as hanging down gracefully. Normally in the sukhasana pose which is a variation in the padmasana form, the right leg is shown as hanging down while the left leg is folded. This figure can easily be taken to be a representation of Shanmukha Skanda. The figure has vaishnavite weapons. This cannot however be a serious objection, as Skanda or Kumara Swamy is himself Vishnu.1 The figure is beautifully carved and deserves notice. 
There are several other figures of Srinivasa, Anjaneya and Yoga Narasimhaswamy on the pillars of this mantapam. There is one particularly impressive nude figure of a woman holding a suka or parrot in her right hand.; A paricharika or a maid on her left is shown as removing the skirt of the main figure, from below the knee downwards. There is another figure of Krishna perched on a tree with the clothes of three Gopikas in his hands. The Gopikas are portrayed in the nude as praying for their clothes. One Gopika how- ever has successfully climbed the tree and has reached the feet of the Lord. Another bas-relief shows Anjaneya carry- ing the Sanjeevi mountain in his hand. 
As in other temples, there are several nude figures and mithuna sculptures in this mantapam (showing the act of love). 
Dhvajastambham:-The 
Dhvajastambham and the Balipitham are situated in the Dhvajastambha mantapam in front of the second or inner Gopuram of the temple. It is a long timber pole circularly shaped encased in gilded 
1. According to the Puranas. 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
59 
copper sheets. It stands on a cutstone structure covered with gilded copper sheets. 
There are some miniature sculptures on the Dhvaja- stambham which deserve notice. One is the figure of Krishna dancing on the hood of the Kaliya snake. Two elephants are seated by the Lord's side. Other figures on the base of the Dhvajastambham include the Garuda, Anjaneya, Krishna lifting up Govardhana hill, etc. Garuda is shown in the anjali hasta pose. The Vaishnavite symbol and the Chakra are also shown. A dancing figure of Bala Krishna in the Navanitha Nritya pose is also seen here. 
The Balipitham in front of the Dhvajastambham is made of cutstone and is square in shape. This is also gilded. 
Sri T. K. T. Veera Raghavacharya is of the opinion that the Dhvajastambham and the Balipitham were first erected inside the temple and that it is rather strange to find it in the Sampangi Pradakshinam area. According to the Agamas, the Garuda, the Dhvajastambham and the Bali- pitham are to be located in the first avaranam or compound in the temple. Where there is a second avaranam in the temple, Garuda would be "in the second quarter of this avaranam and the Dhvaja and Balipitham would come in the third quarter of the avaranam. This second avaranam was built about 1190 A.D. Garuda, the Dhvajastambham and Balipitham would therefore have been within the second avaranam until the date of construction of the Tirumamani mantapam about 1417 A.D. As that construc- tion left little space for the Dhvajastambham and Bali- pitham, they were shifted to the third avaranam. The Vaikhanasa Agama however recognises this arrangement also as a last resort; but they should be in the third quarter and not in the first where we find them now." 
It only remains for us to consider why the outermost 
60 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
circumambulatory path is called the Sampangi Pradakshi- nam. Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala already referred to throws some light on this. It mentions that the Lord of Tirumalai commanded Thondaman Chakravarthi to construct a temple for Him. The Lord's idol was dis- covered under a tamarind tree on the Vengadam hill; there was also a champaka tree on the premises. The King desired to construct the Lord's shrine without removing or hurting the trees, but did not see his way to do so until the trees voluntarily moved out of his way enabling him to build the shrine. Ramanuja on the basis of this legend recognised that both the trees are fit for worship. It is possible that champaka trees were then planted in the outer avaranam and gave their name to the Pradakshinam. At present however no trees exist in the temple premises. The legend referred to above is also mentioned in the Varaha Purana. 
The inner Gopuram:-At the entrance of the inner Gopuram we find two interesting bas-reliefs on the temple walls. One of these shows the Lord's devotee Hathiram1 playing dice with the Lord of Tirumalai. Srinivasa is shown as comfortably seated on an asanam. He has four arms. The upper right and left arms hold the chakra and sankha as usual. His lower left hand rests gracefully on his left knee. The lower right hand directed towards the left holds the two dice in the palm. The Lord is shown as seated in the sukhasana pose with his right leg folded on the seat while the left leg is shown as hanging down gracefully. Opposite to this is another interesting bas-relief depicting Sri Rama holding court. Shri Rama is shown as seated on a platform in the sukhasana pose. His right hand is in the Gnanamudra pose. His consort Sita is shown as seated next to him in 1. A great devotee of Lord Venkateswara. A 'mutt' in his name 
exists at Tirumalai to this day.THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
61 
the sukhasana pose. Her right hand is in the kataka hasta pose holding a lotus bud, while her left hand hangs down gracefully. Two other figures, obviously Lakshmana and Bharatha, are shown in anjali hasta pose. 
A figure to the 
right of Sri Rama holds the chatra or the royal umbrella. Two paricharika figures are shown as fanning the Lord. The figure of Anjaneya is also shown in the picture as pray- ing for the blessing of his Master. 
The Inner Gopuram:-The base of the inner Gopuram measures 24' x 36' x 9'. There is a compound wall starting from this Gopuram and the measurements of this inner prakaram are 160' X 235' as indicated in the sketch. The walls of the prakaram bear several inscriptions. There is no inscription directly giving us material to fix the date of the construction of this Gopuram. Sri T. K. T. Veera Raghavacharya, on the basis of the earliest inscription found on the Gopuram, is of the view that the Gopuram was con- structed between the period 1160 and 1170 A.D. Entering the inner Gopuram we step into the circumambulatory path called the Vimana Pradakshinam. 
STRUCTURES IN THE VIMANA PRADAKSHINAM 
Sri Varadarajaswamy temple:-The Varadarajaswamy shrine is located in the Vimana Pradakshinam to our left as we enter the temple premises from the inner Gopuram doorway. It is a very small shrine measuring about 20' x 15' and houses the image of Sri Varadarajaswamy. There is a Tamil inscription1 on the northern wall of the shrine which runs as follows:-"The sum of 100 Panam was paid into the Sri-Bhandaram on the 23rd day of the Tai month to- wards (the expenses of) the Masitirunal (which is to be conducted) in the year Prabhava (and which was) instituted 
1. T. T. D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, Early Inscriptions (1931), 
pp. 172-173. 
62 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
in the name of Harihararaya, by Mullai-Tiruvenkata-Jiyar who executes this charity from the income of the village of Pungodu. As the debit against the interest (Poliyuttu) on this sum of 100 Panam, the Amudupadi Sattuppadi and other articles shall be issued (for this festival) from the Sri-Bhandaram in the manner of the festivals instituted previously." 
This is the signature of the Tirunira-ur-Udaiyan, the temple accountant. 
The date referred to in the inscription has been worked out as the 18th January, in the year 1388 A.D. It can there- fore, be safely presumed that the shrine was in existence before this date. While discussing the shrine for the Sri Ranganatha utsava vigraha it was already mentioned that the utsava vigraha of Sriranganatha came to Tirumalai temple for safe custody in the early years of the 14th century when the Muslims were over-running South India. Kanchipuram, an important city with the famous shrine for Sri Varadaraja- swamy, was also on the route of the Muslim invaders. It is also well-known that during the days of the Khilji imperial- ism, Mallik Kafur, the General of Alla Uddin Khilji invaded South India and proceeded as far as Rameswaram. Later the armies of Mohammed Bin Tuglak invaded South India (during the period 1327) and sacked Srirangam and Madura when Mabar became one of the provinces of the Tuglak empire and one Jalaluddin Ashan Sha became the Governor In view of the unsettled conditions of Mabar or Madura. it is possible that the deities at Kanchipuram were also brought to Tirumalai for safe custody. If this view is correct, we can assume that the Varadarajaswamy shrine was built at Tirumalai during the period indicated above, for housing the idols of Varadarajaswamy of Kanchipuram. This view is supported by tradition. 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
63 
The idol of Varadarajaswamy in the temple is in the standing pose. It stands on a pedestal about 10" high which itself rests on a platform about 4" high. The figure has four arms; the upper right and left arms hold the chakra and sankha while the lower right hand is in the abhaya hasta pose. The left hand appears to be in the katyavalambita pose. 
Potu Rooms:-Next to the Varadarajaswamy temple, is the main kitchen of the temple called the Potu rooms (61' x 30'). The Lord's prasadams are cooked here every day. The cooking is done in new earthen pots. It is interest- ing to note that the kitchen is in charge of Sri Vakuladevi, the foster-mother of Srinivasa. There is an idol of Sri Vakuladevi at the entrance to the kitchen. The idol is inside the kitchen. This figure is in the padmasana pose and has four arms. The upper two arms are in the kataka hasta mudras and hold lotuses. The lower right hand is in the abhaya hasta pose while the left is in the varada hasta pose. This deity is also called Madapuli Nachiyar. 
Bangaru Bhavi:-In front of the Potu room and adjoin- ing a corridor running east-west in front of the Potu room, is a well covered with gilded plates called the Bangaru Bhavi. The water for abhishekam of the Lord and for cooking the Lord's prasadams is taken from this well. Bangaru Bhavi is a Telugu word which stands for a Well of Gold. 
The legend connected with the Bangaru Bhavi is given in the Fifth Stabaka of the Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala already referred to. Water for abhishekam of the Lord in the olden days was being obtained from the Papanashanam thirtham in the Tirumalai hills. Once Sri Alavandar present at Tirumalai noticed that Tirumalai Nambi who was at this time performing the duty of carrying water for the temple was not in a position to do so due to sickness. Sri Alavandar 
64 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
therefore prayed to the Lord that He should accept the water from the well in the temple constructed by one Rangadasa. Sri Ramanuja who heard the above legend during his visit to Tirumalai also ordained that the waters of Papanashanam Thirtham, Akasha Ganga Thirtham and the well called Sundaraswamy well (or Bangaru Bhavi) are holy and can be used for all purposes in the temple. The water from the Bangaru Bhavi has been used in the temple ever since. 
The 
Kalyana Mantapam:-The Kalyana mantapam is a large mantapam in the southern portion of the Vimana Pradakshinam and measures about 80' X 36'. The man- tapam consists of 29 pillars one of which forms part of the corridor wall found to the east of it. The Kalyana Man- tapam has a beautiful sub-mantapam at its western corner raised on four slender pillars of polished cutstone. mantapam has also a small shrine. From a study of the sculptures in the mantapam, we can say that the mantapam was constructed in the 16th century. As the name itself indicates the mantapam is being used to perform the Kalya- nam or the marriage ceremony of the Lord of Tirumalai. This mantapam has very lovely bas-reliefs and deserves detailed examination. The mantapam shows unmistakable traits of excellence of the Vijayanagar school of architecture. In the main mantapam there is a unique bas-relief of Pavana Narasimha or Holy Narasimha on one of the mantapam pillars. The bas-relief shows Lord Narasimha destroying the demon Hiranya Kasipu. The murthi is shown in the sukhasana pose with the right leg folded while the left leg is dangling freely. The Lord has sixteen arms, fourteen of which hold various weapons of the Vishnu such as Chakra, Sankha, Khadga, Khetaka, Parasu, etc. Two arms are exclusively used in destroying the demon (by extraction of his vital organs such as the intestines, etc.). The mantapam 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
65 
has several other exquisitely carved figures on its granite pillars. One such is the figure of Trivikrama. Here the Lord is shown with four arms; the upper right hand stretch- ed into the ether (with the pointing finger held aloft) indi- cates that he has occupied the skies. The upper left arm holds the sankha. The lower right arm holds the chakra. The lower left hand is in the katyavalambita pose. The left leg of the Lord is firm and erect. The right leg is in a lifted position. One of the Lord's feet is on the head of Bali Chakravarthi. There is another beautiful bas-relief in which the figure of Lord Vishnu is shown with six arms, riding on Garuda. There is another exquisitely carved figure of a hunter and his consort making love to each other. The lady's left hand holds a bow while she embraces her lover with her right hand. The lover's left hand is around the waist of the lady and rests on her hip. He holds two arrows in his right hand. Another figure is that of Vishnu in a dancing pose. He is resting his weight on his right leg while the left is shown in the air bent at the knee. His right hand is in the abhaya hasta pose while the left hand is flung into the air in a graceful dancing pose. There is another preci- ous figure showing Sudarshana Chakra or Chakrattalwar with sixteen hands holding various weapons. Other note- worthy figures in the mantapam are Krishna in the Nava- nitha Nrithya Bhangima, Kaliya Phani-mardana Krishna, a woman in a dancing pose, two monkeys fighting with each other, Garuda etc. The sub-mantapam has four pillars; each pillar is itself a complex pillar with a main pillar square in shape to which are attached three smaller pillars. These are musical pillars producing musical notes when sounded. This mantapam has several other exquisitely carved figures of Vishnu and his consorts, Lord Srinivasa, Sri Rama, Krishna with his two consorts Rukmini and Satya Bhama, 
S.V.-3 
66 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Vishnu in the sitting pose with Lakshmi seated on his lap, 
etc. 
The main pillars are themselves carved perfectly show- ing warriors riding on lions; the lions themselves are set on figures of elephants, etc. These pillars are also pillars of the complex type having smaller pillars attached to them. 
The Corridor:-The Corridor near the Pula Bhavi has some good bas-reliefs which are described below in brief. A unique bas-relief is that of Lakshmi Narasimha. The Lord is shown as a merry celestial. His left hand rests above his hip while he is shown as embracing Lakshmi with his right hand. The fingers of his right hand rest in the area between the breasts of his consort. Another worth- seeing bas-relief is that of a Veera Nari or a brave woman holding a bow. 
An excellent and unique bas-relief is Vishnu in a grace- ful dancing pose similar to that of dancing Nataraj. The figure has four arms. The right and left upper arms hold the chakra and sankha. The lower arms are held in Natya Bhangimas. The figure is resting on one leg while the other is shown in a graceful dancing pose. The other figures on the pillars in the corridor show Gopala Krishna, Krishna in the Navanita Nritya pose, Sri Rama with a bow and one arrow, Maha Lakshmi with four arms, Srinivasa and Garuda in the anjali hasta pose. The mantapam is used for per- forming the kalyanotsavams and for seating the processional deity and his consorts during the Brahmotsavam festival. The idols are then kept in the small shrine in the mantapam. The idols also remain in this shrine in the month of Decem- ber during the adhyayanotsavam festival. It is also rele- vant to point out that one Tallapakkam Tiruvenkadayyar, son of Siru-Tirumalai Iyyangar of Bharadwaja Gotra endow- ed the temple for the performance of the marriage festival 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
67 
of the Lord in Tirumalai. From the inscription, it would appear that the marriage festival was performed for a period of five days. It would also appear that the Lord's processional image was seated in a swing on the last four days of the Kalyana Utsavam. It is also interesting to note that arrangements were made at this Kalyana Utsavam for distribution of chandanam, camphor, etc. to the devotees and to the sumangalis or married women (excluding widows) in particular. 
Vahanam Stores:-Adjoining the Kalyana mantapam, we find a mantapam on the western side of the Sampangi Pradakshinam, housing the vahanams of the Lord of Tiru- malai. The vahanams are vehicles on which the Lord of Tirumalai rides on special occasions. The Lord of Tirumalai had in earlier days two big foursided wooden cars; these cars are utilised on the eighth day of the Brahmotsavam festival. 
The northern side of the Vimana Pradakshinam:-Some of the open mantapams on the northern and western side are used for seating pandits in Vedas etc., who recite the holy books such as the Vedas, the Ramayana, Bhagavadgita, etc., during the day time. There are several rooms on the nor- thern side of the Vimana Pradakshinam. In the corner we find the 'Deepala room' or the room of lights. Next to it, is the Gammikars' room or the room of cooks and the Kaikolas' room or the room of lighting servants. Most of these rooms are kept closed now. The Vimana Venkateswara i.e. Lord Srinivasa on the Vimanam of the temple is visible near the Kaikola room. Pilgrims stand on a small cutstone step here and see the figure of Lord Srinivasa on the Vimanam of the Garbha Griham. 
Next to the Kaikola room, is the Pallakki room, where 
1. T.T.D. Epigraphical Report (1930) p. 30. 
68 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
the Lord's palanquin is stored. There is another small room here in which copper plate engravings of songs composed by Tallapakam Annamacharyulu and his descendants are 
preserved. There is a small room called Sabhaiarai where the Lord's wardrobe and the cot on which he is put to bed at night are stored. 
Sri Ramanuja's shrine:-The date of construction of this shrine for Sri Ramanuja is not mentioned directly in any inscription. There is however an inscription1, on the southern wall of the second prakaram in Tirumalai temple which mentions offering of food to Lord Raghunathan at Tirumalai and to Sri Ramanuja. The inscription in favour of one Satakopadasar Narasimhadasar informs us that he made arrangements for the offering of one Tirupponakam to be offered daily to Raghunathan of Tirumalai, and "12 Tiruvolakkam (to be conducted) on the 12 days on which Udaiyavar-Emperumanar hears the Adhyayanam (recitation) of the Tiruvaymoli in the month of Chittirai, 11 Tiruvolak- kam on (his) 11 monthly (birth) star (days), 1 Tiruvolak- kam to be offered in the shrine of the said Udaiyavar- Emperumanar, on the (day of) Nammalvar's annual birth- star Vaisakha in the month of Vaikasi after his hearing the Tiruvaymoli". This inscription also requires that the food offering should be first made to Tiruvenkatamudaiyan i.e. the Lord of Tirumalai and then to the Udaiyavar. The food offerings are to be made on the Tiruvadhyanam and monthly birth stars of Udaiyavar. This inscription has been dated 23rd November, 1476. We may therefore, safely pre- sume that Ramanuja's shrine came into existence in Tiru- malai about the year 1476 A.D. Sri T.K.T. Veera Ragha- vacharya is however of the view that the Ramanuja temple at Tirupati as well as at Tirumalai were perhaps constructed 1. T.T.D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. II, Inscription of Saluva 
Narasimha's time, 200 T.T. pp. 129-139. 
THE TEMPLE AND ITS SCULPTURES 
69 
simultaneously, that the Ramanuja temple at Tirupati was in existence in 1220 A.D. and that the Ramanuja shrine at Tirumalai was also constructed at about the same period. He is of the view that an idol at least might have been in- stalled in the temple on the earlier date, and that the present shrine was constructed some time before 1476 A.D. There is some substance in this hypothesis that the image of Rama- nuja was available for installation in the temple on the ear- lier date. This view is strengthened on a perusal of Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala which mentions about the set- ting up of an image of Ramanuja in Venkatachalam. The legend mentions that one Anantarya after hearing about the death of Ramanuja prayed to Lord Srinivasa that He might permit the setting up of Ramanuja's image at Venkatacha- lam and that the Lord's sanction was obtained. As indicated earlier, the work belongs to the 12th century. If we credit the legend in the Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala we should agree that an image of the preceptor was ready for installa- tion in the temple in the 12th century itself-earlier than the period and dates suggested by Sri Veera Raghavacharya. The shrine itself might have been constructed later on. 
Ramanuja is said to have been born in the year 1017 A.D. According to traditional and historical sources, he lived to the ripe old age of 120 years. This would indicate that his niryanam took place about the year 1137 A.D. Dr. S. Krishnaswamy Aiyangar is however of the view that the traditional date for Ramanuja has been fixed a little too early and a re-adjustment of the date is necessary. There is however no doubt that Sri Ramanuja's fruitful years were in the 11th century and the early years of the 12th century. Ramanuja is the greatest exponent and founder of the 
70 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Visishtadvaita system of philosophy. He is worshipped by all Srivaishnavas. However puja as such is not done to him in the temple. As mentioned earlier, prasadam which is given as 'Naivedyam' to Sri Venkateswara is offered to this Acharya also. It is also worth remembering that the Al- wars do not find a place in the Tirumalai temple. Only Sri Ramanuja who is not an Alwar has been given a shrine in Tirumalai. This Acharya is specially honoured in the temple as he argued the Vaishnavas' cause before the Yada- varaja and proclaimed to the world that the Vengadam Shrine is a Vishnu shrine. He also laid down detailed in- structions regarding the worship of the Lord in the temple. These aspects are discussed separately in Chapter IX. 
It only remains to be pointed out that Sri Ramanuja is a follower of the pancharatra form of worship of Lord Vishnu. This is the form of worship which is followed in many Vaishnavite shrines in India, the other form being the Vaikhanasa system followed in the temple at Tirumalai. 
Sri Yoganarasimhaswamy temple:-Next to the shrine of Ramanuja is located the shrine of Yoga Narasimhaswamy. An important inscription connected with this shrine is on the west wall of the second prakaram in Tirumalai temple. It is reproduced below :2
