This festival was revived in the temple some years ago. It is a purificatory festival and is conducted on the Sravana Suddha Dasami day. It takes place for four days. 
The Vasanthotsavam:-The first Vasanthotsavam in Tirumalai was started by one Vilambi, son of a temple ac- countant. The ankurarpanam for this festival was done on the day of the Uttarabhadra star in the month of Palguni and the last day was on the day of Krithika star. The utsavam was celebrated for four days. Vasantham stands for spring and Vasanthotsavam is the spring festival. It is learnt that the Lord's silver chariot is used for this festival. 
The Nityotsavam:-The Nityotsavam of the Lord is per- formed for forty days after 'Ugadi, the Telugu New Year's day. 
Summer festival or Kodai Tirunal:-It was celebrated for a period of twenty days. This festival was being per- 
148 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
formed during the administration of the Mahants. It is not being performed at present. 
Anna Unjal Tirunal:-"Anna Unjal" stands for swan- shaped swing. This festival was first started by Saluva Narasimha and was conducted for seven days. Inscription 197 T.T. of the temple indicates that Saluva Narasimharaya instituted this festival and gives in detail the offerings made during the festival. It is obvious that this festival took place for five days. The festival started off by putting a pratima or an image into the Somakumbham or water pot while sowing the Navadhanyas. The processional image was then seated in the Unjal or Swing and various offerings were made to the Lord. The ankurarpanam was done on the sixth day of Sukla Paksha in the month of Chittirai. Homa, Punyaham etc. were done on the fifth day of the Utsavam. This festival is not being conducted in the temple at present. 
Tiruppaliodam Tirunal or floating festival:-The ins- cription referred to above (197 T.T.)1 by Saluva Narasimha also mentions about the first floating festival performed in the temple. The inscription is dated 15th June, 1473, and we may take it that the first floating festival was performed in this year. 
The floating festival usually takes place in Tirumalai for five days. The first day, Lord Krishna of the temple in the Navanita Nritya pose is the presiding deity for the floating festival. The second day Sri Rama is the presiding deity while the presiding deities for the last three days are the Utsavamurthi of the Lord of Tirumalai and his consorts Sri Devi and Bhudevi. The presiding deities are gaily de- corated and taken in procession on a "Teppa' or boat floated in the Swami Pushkarini. 
Kalyana Utsavam:-The Kalyana Utsavam is usually per- 
1. T.T.D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. II, pp. 89-98. 
FESTIVALS AND PECULIAR CUSTOMS IN THE TEMPLE 149 
formed in the Kalyana Mantapam or the Tirumala Nayaka Mantapam. In this Utsavam the marriage of Sri Malayap- pan with his brides Sri Devi and Bhudevi is performed. This is an impressive ceremony and begins with the Pancha- murthi Puja following the Vaikhanasa Agama rituals. The marriage is done with Mangala Sutra Dharana according to Vedic rites. The 'thalambralu' ceremony is also perform- ed as in regular marriages. The Nachchiyars and the Lord are then garlanded. 
This is purely an arjitham festival i.e. one which is con- ducted on payment of the scheduled fees. The donor is honoured at the end with a 'vastram' from the temple. All the devotees present at the temple are given a dakshina of one paisa along with the Lord's 'Akshintalu'. 
Customs followed in the Tirumalai temple:-Before giving a brief description of the customs peculiar to the temple as well as a recital of the daily worship done in the temple it will be relevant and useful to state that the book Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala mentions of certain rituals of worship finalised by Sri Ramanuja. These arrangements are given in the fourth stabaka of the Ithihasa Mala. They are as follows:- 
(1) The face mark of the Lord's image should be made up of Pacchaikarpuram. 
(2) The face mark of the Lord on Mondays, Tuesdays and Wednesdays should be made up of white earth. 
(3) Katuka or collyrium should be applied to the eyes. (4) The image should be dressed in flowers on Thurs- days removing all the jewellery on the Lord. 
(5) The flower dress should be removed on Fridays and the upright face mark on the Lord should be made with sacred earth. The image should be given a full abhishekam on this day. 
150 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
(6) The Nachchiyar Prabandam should be sung in the temple on Fridays at the time of the removal of the Padma- vati plaque for the abhishekam of the Lord. 
(7) The Lord Varahamurthi at Tirumalai is to be worshipped first; the first Naivedyam should be to this deity and pilgrims should worship Him first before worshipping the Lord of Tirumalai. 
(8) As the hill is holy, only those who have to serve the Lord should reside on the hill. 
(9) Jivahimsa should not be allowed on the hill; those residing on the hill at the time of their death should be re- moved to the plains. 
(10) Pilgrims should not wear sandals while proceeding to Tirumalai. 
Several of the above customs said to have been initiated by Ramanuja, are to this day followed in the temple. A few however are not in vogue. Thus while it is true that the face mark of the Lord's image is made of Pacchaikarpuram to this day, it appears that white earth is not used for the Tirunamam on the days mentioned in (2) above. The Pac- chaikarpuram namam is applied rather thickly over the eyes of the Lord and on the forehead of the Lord every Friday. This face mark is made thin on Thursday and is entirely re- moved before the weekly abhishekam or bath given to the Lord on Friday. After the Friday abhishekam, the face mark is again made with Pacchaikarpuram and not with sacred earth as listed out in (5) above. The other customs men- tioned above are followed in the temple to this day. 
A few other customs followed in the temple are as follows:- 
(1) The worship in the temple is not according to the Vaishnavite Pancharatra Agama, but according to the Vai- khanasa Agama.FESTIVALS AND PECULIAR CUSTOMS IN THE TEMPLE 151 
(2) The Lord's chin (Mula Vigraha) is daily exhibited with 1 tolas of Pacchaikarpuram dabbed on it. 
(3) The Lord of Tirumalai (Mula Vigraha) obtains 'Tirumanjanam' or abhishekam on every Friday morning. Turmeric paste is one of the articles used during this abhi- shekam for the Lakshmi figure, forming part of the Lord. 
(4) The Bhoga Srinivasa or the Bhoga Murthi in the temple however is given a daily abhishekam. 
(5) Civet oil is applied to the Lord's Vigraha obvious- ly as a preservative. 
(6) The Archana in the temple (Sahasra Archana and Ashtottara Archana) involves the repetition of the several Vishnu names which are listed out in the Varaha Purana. 
(7) During the abhishekam on Friday the mantras recited follow the Purushasukta Vidhanam. The 'Mantra- pushpa' slokas proclaiming that 'Sriman Narayana is the One and that worship to all deities reaches Him' are recited. (8) After the abhishekam, along with the recital of the Prabandham, hymns from the Taittiriya Upanishad are chanted. 
(9) The Lord's devotees usually offer their hair as a devotional offering to Lord Srinivasa. Devotees who pray to the Lord for his support, also pay 'mudupus' or offerings, according to pledges made by them. These offerings are deposited in the Lord's Hundi placed in the Tirumamani mantapam. These offerings form the main revenues of the temple. There is everyday a steady flow of gold, silver, currency notes, etc. into the Hundi and it can be said with- out exaggeration that the Tirumalai temple is the richest temple in the whole of India. This is obviously due to the steady revenues which flow into the temple Hundi every day from the Lord's devotees. There have been occasions when single devotees have themselves offered to the Lord 
152 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
sums as much as a lakh of rupees. The sincere prayer of the devotee for the Lord's assistance has always been answered by the Lord in his infinite grace and sympathy and hence the steady flow of pilgrims to this temple to wor- ship the Bhaktavatsala with the Varada Hastham. 
According to tradition, the great monistic philosopher Sankara is said to have visited the temple and installed not only a Sri-chakram under the Lord's image but Dhana Akarshana yantram-a yantram which attracts Dhana or riches. According to legend, the power or 'shakti' of the Lord is due to the above Sri-chakram and the steady flow of 'Dhana' or riches is due to the 'yantram' mention- ed above. 
(10) A unique ceremony called the 'waking up' cere- mony or Suprabhatam is performed in the temple every- day; similarly the 'Ekantha Seva' is a ceremony in which the Lord is put to bed every night. These peculiar cere- monies obviously treat the Lord as a human being. 
(11) The food offerings to the Lord are made outside the Kulasekharappadi. Only four nali of rice are said to be offered inside the Garba Griha to the Lord along with fruits, flower, milk, butter etc. 
(12) The Chandramana Panchangam or the Lunar Almanac is followed in the temple. 
Some of the more peculiar customs are discussed below briefly:- 
Application of Pacchaikarpuram on the Lord's chin:- The Lord's chin wears an application of pacchaikarpuram always. This curious custom is explained by the following legend. Anantarya, one of the disciples of Sri Ramanuja, is said to have been ordained by his Guru Sri Ramanuja to maintain a nandanavanam or flower garden in Tirumalai to ensure a regular supply of flowers for the Lord of Tiru- 
FESTIVALS AND PECULIAR CUSTOMS IN THE TEMPLE 153 
malai. He was keen on gardening as a service to the Lord, and was assisted in this work by his wife who was pregnant at that time. Once Anantarya was digging up the earth in his garden and was levelling the land; his pregnant wife was then assisting him by carrying the earth in a basket on her head. Due to her condition she soon got tired in her self- appointed task. Lord Srinivasa, the All-Seeing Deity, therefore assumed the form of a young Brahmachari and was assisting her by carrying the earth himself. Anantarya noticed the Brahmachari and thought it was rather presumptuous of him to interfere in their 'kainkaryam' to the Lord and struck him with the spade in his hand. The Brahmachari who was hit on his chin immediately vanished. Later Anantarya proceeded to the temple to worship Lord Srinivasa and found that blood was freely oozing from the Lord's image. He is said to have immediately realised that his helper was no other than the Lord Himself and quickly dressed the Lord's wound by applying pacchaikarpuram. The Lord is then said to have pardoned his devotee and said that he would wear the wound thenceforth in recognition of Anantarya's devotion. 
Another legend connected with Anantarya is the pre- sentation of a garland of flowers. It is said that once Lord Srinivasa and his consort Padmavati assumed the guise of a Prince and Princess and were sporting themselves in Anantarya's nandanavanam. Disturbed that his flowers were being collected and spoiled, Anantarya kept watch one night, discovered the couple in the garden and tried to capture them. Lord Srinivasa dodged him and disappeared, but his consort Padmavati was caught and tied to a champaka tree. The next day Anantarya discovered who his captive was and took her back to the temple with all honours. 
154 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Hearing this legend, Ramanuja is said to have ordained that the Lord of Tirumalai should be taken in procession on the seventh day of the Brahmotsavam round the town and that on the eighth day, Anantarya should be presented with a garland of flowers from the temple. This practice is being followed to this day. 
The Tamil Prabandham:-The chanting of the Tamil Prabandham is another unique custom followed in the temple. The Tamil Prabandham as well as the Vedas are recited not only during the Adhyayanotsavam, but also at other times. Thus, for example, when Tirumanjanam or abhi- shekam is done to the Lord on Friday, the plaque on which the Padmavati figure is carved is removed and at this time the hymns composed by Andal Alwar which form a part of the Tamil Prabandham are recited by the priests. Sri Rama- nuja appears to have initiated the practice of chanting the hymns of the Prabandham. The Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala mentions that Ramanuja arranged that the Adhyayana should be performed daily in the temple by first reciting the verses of Perialvar and later the hymns of other Alwars. 
Tonsure ceremony in Tirumalai::-Another unique custom of Tirumalai is the Tonsure ceremony. Pilgrims to Tirumalai usually offer the hair on their head to the Lord as a devotional offering. Persons in need of the assistance of the Lord usually take a vow and when they visit the temple offer their hair at the Kalyanakatta special hall erected for this purpose. A bath in the Pushkarini follows the Tonsure ceremony; the pilgrims then enter the temple and worship the Lord. 
The Tirumalai temple inscriptions mention about food offerings etc. made to the Lord, but no mention is made of this curious custom of offering of hair. The custom was perhaps introduced to stress the quality of humility as well as 
FESTIVALS AND PECULIAR CUSTOMS IN THE TEMPLE 155 
renunciation of what is usually prized. It is also possible that this custom came into prominence when the Vaishnavas vigorously started their proselytising activities by setting up missions and by converting people from other sects and beliefs into the Vaishnava fold. Some fees are collected for the Tonsure ceremony and it is possible that the revenue aspect might have been one strong reason for the intro- duction of this custom in Tirumalai. Sri T. K. T. Veera Raghavacharya in his book History of Tirupati has re- produced in Appendix I, a reprint of an article published in August 1831 A.D. by an English District Collector of the region about Tirumalai in the Asiatic Journal. The reprint mentions that "offerings or counickee are made ge- nerally from interested motives, and are of every diversity of articles conceivable; gold and silver lumps, coins of all sorts, bags of rupees, copper, money, spices, asafoetida, the hair cut off the head frequently vowed from infancy, and given up by some beautiful virgin in compliance with her parents' oath". From the above it is evident that this cus- tom at least existed in the year 1831 A.D. if not earlier. 
Worship in the Temple:-The worship in the temple is strictly according to the Vaikhanasa Agama. Accord- ing to the Bhavishyottara Puranam, Vaikhanasa Rishi is said to have admired the leelas of Lord Krishna in Dva- parayugam and done tapas to obtain darshan of the Lord Krishna. Lord Krishna then appeared to him and informed him that the sage should worship him as Lord Srinivasa only. He was also directed to proceed to Seshachalam and worship Lord Srinivasa who had manifested Himself there in an ant-hill. A Sudra by name Rangadasa would help him to locate the Lord in the hill. Vaikhanasa Rishi is then said to have proceeded to Tirumalai, obtained the services of one Rangadasa, located the Lord in the ant-hill 
156 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
and started worshipping the Lord of Tirumalai. Thus Vai- khanasa Rishi was the first person to worship the Lord of Tirumalai; the temple is to this day worshipped therefore according to the Vaikhanaṣa practices only. 
The routine of daily worship in the temple is now des- cribed below briefly. 
1. Suprabhatam:-The day starts with the Suprabha- tam or the wakening of the Lord. The Suprabhatam hymns are sung before the Bangaru Vakili. Immediately after this is over, the Bhoga Srinivasa who is in bed in the Sayana mantapam is shifted to the sanctum. Navanitha Harati is done to the Lord and a mixture of cow's milk, butter and sugar is offered to the Deity. The devotees who have chant- ed the hymns of Suprabhatam are then allowed to have the darshan of the Lord. This darshan is called the Supra- bhata darshanam. Devotees are usually given as prasadam, the mixture of milk, butter and sugar offered to the Lord. The Visvarupaseva follows immediately when all pilgrims waiting outside the temple are allowed to have darshan of the Lord. The thirtham left in the thirtham vessels at night are also now distributed to the devotees. As mentioned earlier, the celestials are supposed to worship the Lord at night, and this thirtham is the thirtham of the puja perform- ed by Brahma and other celestials. 
2. Thomalu Seva or the Bhagavathi Aradhana:- Thomalu stands for flower garlands. In this Seva the Lord is richly decorated with flower garlands. This Seva follows the Visvarupaseva after suddhi (cleaning) is performed in the temple. The suddhi consists of removal of the pre- vious night's decorations, etc. These flowers are thrown into the step-well constructed by Rangadasa in the Sam- pangi Pradakshinam. 
During the Thomalu Seva water is offered to the Lord 
FESTIVALS AND PECULIAR CUSTOMS IN THE TEMPLE 157 
for his daily ablutions. Abhishekam is done to the gold covers which cover the mula-vigraham as well as to Bhoga Srinivasa. The abhishekam of Bhoga Srinivasa is done while appropriate hymns are chanted. The Lord's image is then decorated with several flower garlands. 
3. Koluvu or Durbar:-The Durbar of the Koluvu Srinivasa takes place in the morning when the previous day's revenue receipts are reported to him. The almanac is also read to the Lord. 
4. Sahasranama Archana:-During the Sahasranama Archana, the names of Lord Vishnu are recited. This is an arjitha seva and only persons who have paid the required fees as in the case of Thomalu Seva are allowed inside. 
5. Naivedyam:-The Sayana mantapam is cleaned and cooked food brought there as Naivedyam to the Lord. The Bangaru Vakili doors are then closed and the bells in the Tirumamani mantapam are rung. This is usually called 'the first bell' in the temple. Only the archakas are present in the sanctum at this time. Food is offered at this time to the Lord. Food offerings are also made to Vishvaksena, Garuda and the Nityasuris in the temple. 
6. Sathumurai:-The Nalayira Prabandham is also read at this time when only Sri Vaishnavas are present in the temple. This applies during Naivedyam time also. 
7. Dharma Darshanam:-Pilgrims are allowed free darshan of the Lord, at specified timings in the day. This is Dharma Darshanam. 
8. The Madhyanna Puja:-The Madhyanna puja is the abbreviated form of worship of the Lord in the afternoon when the Lord's Ashtottaram or 108 names of Vishnu from the Varaha Puranam are repeated. This is an Arjitha Seva. A second naivedyam is offered at this time to the Lord. 
158 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
9. Ratri Puja:-Puja is done at night also to the Lord; only Sri Vaishnava archakas are present at this time. 
10. Pavvalimpu Seva and Ardhajama Puja:-Before the Lord is put to bed, the ardhajama puja is performed when sweets etc. are offered to the Lord. 
Ekantha Seva:-Ekantha Seva is a Seva to which the public are allowed on payment. A velvet bed is spread on a Vuyyala or Swing and sweets, milk, fruits, etc. are offered to the Lord. At this time, songs are also sung. A descendant of Tallapakka poets also sings kirtans of the Tallapakka poet. Perfumed chandanam is placed on the chest of Bhoga Srinivasa as well as Alarmed Mangai on the Mula Vigraham. After the Ekantha Seva, the temple is closed. 
Pulangi Seva:-There is a special puja called the Pu- langi Seva on all Thursdays. During this puja, the Mula- murthi is clearly visible as most of the ornaments of the Lord are removed. Even the pacchaikarpuram marks on the eyes and forehead of the Lord are made thin during this Seva. 
Abhishekam:-Tirumanjanam or abhishekam is made on the Mula Vigraham on Fridays. This is a function which takes about an hour and is accompanied by chanting of man- tras, etc. The abhishekam is done to the Mula-murthi di- vested of all ornaments and clothes, except the Alarmel Mangai plaque. During the abhishekam, milk, sandal paste, turmeric, etc. are used. Honey is however not used for the abhishekam of the Mula Vigraham. After the bath, civet oil is applied to the Lord's body. Civet oil is also applied daily to the feet of the Lord. 
Sripadarenu:-The pacchaikarpuram and kasturi along with civet oil collected from the Lord's body forms the Sri- padarenu prized as a valuable prasadam of the Lord. 
FESTIVALS AND PECULIAR CUSTOMS IN THE TEMPLE 159 
Special honours:-According to age-old custom and accepted by old Amulnamas the District Collector, Chittoor, the District Judge, Chittoor and the Tahsildar, Chandragiri, are given special honours in Lord Sri Venkateswara's temple. A mafi Harathi or a free Harathi is to be given to the Lord while they worship in the temple. It is presumed that these privileges can continue to be enjoyed by them during life even after their transfer or retirement. 
Sahasra Ghata Abhishekam:-This is a unique abhi- shekam to the Lord when the Lord is given a bath with one thousand pots of water, to the accompaniment of mantras. This is a ritual which is an arjitha seva and is worth seeing by all devotees of the Lord. 
CHAPTER X 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
We may now make a very brief survey of the adminis- tration of the temple. As can be imagined, the fortunes of the temple have more or less been influenced by the fast- flowing currents of Indian history at every stage-and more particularly by the history of South India. 
Temple Administration up to 830 A.D.:-The temple was more or less a forest temple, perched on an inaccessible range of hills up to the eighth century A.D. We are there- fore not in a position to say much regarding the temple ad- ministration up to this time. Even after this period the temple remained inaccessible, and it is a fact that the Tirumalai area was itself considered, for several centuries up to the 20th century, to be an Agency tract with a malarial climate unfit for anyone to settle down for life. The conditions in the early ages of history can therefore be easily imagined. This was perhaps the reason why a proxy image of the Lord of Tirumalai was kept and worshipped in the Tiruchikanoor temple in the plains as is seen from the following inscription,i which has been dated 830 A.D. 
"Hail, Prosperity! In the 51st year of the reign of Ko- Vijaya-Dantivikramar, Ulagapperumanar of Solanur in Sola-nadu deposited 30 Kalanju (of gold) as the capital for a lamp (intended) for the image of Tiruvilankoyil Perumana- digal installed as representation of Tiruvenkatattu-Emperu- manadigal (Sri Venkateswara). (We, the assembly of) Tiruchchoginur (Tiruchanur) (situated) in Kudavurnadu (comprised in the district) of Tiruvenkata-kottam, having 
1. T.T.D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, Early Inscriptions (T.G.) 
No. 219, p. 9.THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
161 
purchased (a certain) Patti (of land) wih this capital, have caused the lamp to be set up". 
The above inscription shows that a Tiruvilankoyil- Perumanadigal, a representation of Sri Venkateswara of Tirumalai, was installed at Tiruchanur and worshipped. This arrangement was perhaps made to assist pilgrims to worship the Lord, who were not in a position to undertake the ardu- ous journey up the hill. 
In these early ages, the temple must have been visited by the Lord's devotees in large numbers only on certain special occasions such as the Brahmotsavam festival. On other days the worship must have been conducted every day by a priest or a small body of priests living down in the plains. 
The earliest inscription we have in the T.T. Devasthanam Epigraphical Series pertains to the year 830 A.D. (the one quoted above) and the administration of the temple before this period is only to be inferred from the literature of the period, from the Puranas, etc. It has already been mentioned that a certain legendary King by name Tondaiman discovered the Lord's image, and arranged for the worship of the Lord in addition to getting a temple constructed for Him. 
The references to Vengadam Hill and to Lord Venka- teswara in the Sangam literature, the Silappadikaram and the songs of Alwars have already been discussed elsewhere; these books do not enlighten us much about the adminis- tration of the temple. Worship of Vishnu-a Vedic Deity- was not unknown in this period. It is also conceivable that one of the important Divyapradesams of the Vaishna- vas (there are 108 such Divyapradesams in India sacred to Vishnu) would be neglected by lack of proper worship. We would therefore be not wrong in assuming that the Lord in Tirumalai received worship everyday during the period in- dicated above. 
S.V.-7 
162 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Temple Administration during the Pallava period:-The earliest inscription available in T.T. Devasthanam Epigra- phical Series (quoted above) is an inscription of the Pallavas. The Pallavas were the successors to the great Andhra Sata- vahanas as Rulers in South India. In the heyday of the Andhra Satavahanas, the Pallavas started as Governors of the southern areas of the great Andhra empire, and were subordinate to the Andhra Satavahanas. It is well known that the early Satavahanas were mentioned in the inscriptions of Asoka (about 274 B.C.) and had Paithan on the Godavari as their capital. The early Satavahanas were patrons of the Vedic religion. The later Satavahanas who continued reigning in India up to 200 A.D.-for about two centuries- possessed and ruled the whole of Deccan from the Arabian sea to the Bay of Bengal, Berar and Malwa, while their empire in the North extended up to the Vindhyas. Yagna Sri, one of the rulers in the later part of the second century A.D., inflicted defeat on Saka satraps and was even at home on the Indian ocean. His shipmarked coins indicate that there was extensive maritime commerce in his period. It was during Yagna Sri's reign that the first Pallava, Bappa Deva, became the Governor or Maharathi of the southern areas of the Andhra empire. When the power of the Andhras disappeared, the Pallavas with Kanchi as capital, asserted themselves and became in their turn masters of South India. The areas around Vengadam were under them up to the end of the 9th century, when the Cholas succeeded the Pallavas as the masters of South India. 
The Pallava inscriptions of the temple throw some light on the temple administration of the period. An im- portant inscription is that of the Pallava Queen Samavai, who consecrated the idol of Bhoga Srinivasa in the temple. This inscription has been quoted in Chapter III. This in- 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
163 
scription which is dated 966 A.D. mentions the administrative machinery of the temple at Tirumalai. The relevant portion of the inscription reads: ".... All the services, the managers of the temple (madapatyam) on Tiruvenkatam shall have conducted and the Sabhaiyar shall protect the land from being taxed..." From this, it is obvious that there were some Madapatyadars or managers in the temple and that the Sabhaiyar referred to is perhaps the village council of Tiru- chchukanur, which appears to have had control over the affairs of the temple at this time. 
Traditional accounts of the temple administration during Sri Ramanuja's time (1017-1137):-Sri Ramanuja is said to have been born in the year 1017 A.D. According to tradition he lived for about 120 years. While we are not sure about the dates, it is certain that he visited Tirumalai once or twice during the early years of the 12th century A.D. The work Sri Venkatachala Ithihasa Mala mentions of certain arrangements made by him for the Lord's worship in the temple; and these have already been catalogued in Chapter IX. Ramanuja ensured that his regulations were carried out by appointing an Ekangi (or bachelor) to look after the temple administration. It is said that the Ekangi was given the yellow robes of a Sanyasi along with the image of Sri Rama, a seal with the mark of Hanuman for sealing the Lord's treasury, and a lock and key to lock up the Lord's temple at night. 
The devotees worshipping daily at the temple must have been limited at this time, and it is possible that this one Ekangi-administrator was able to cope with the temple ad- 
ministration. 
Temple Administration under the Cholas:-The Cholas were the successors of the Pallavas in South India. Aditya I (880-907) became the first important Chola King 
164 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
defeating the last Pallava ruler Aparajita. The founder of the Chola power was however Vijayalaya, (the father of Aditya) who was the first ruler of Tanjore. With the defeat of the last Pallava King, Tondaimandalam i.e. the areas around 'Vengadam' passed under the control of the Cholas. Aditya's son Parantaka (907-953 A.D.) defeated the Eastern Chalukyas, the Pandyas of Madura, invaded Ceylon and established the Chola Empire in South India. The greatest ruler of the Cholas is Raja Raja Chola I who ascended the throne in 985 A.D. and ruled for 29 years. He defeated the Cheras, the Pandyas, the eastern Chalukyas of Vengi and the Kings of Travancore and Orissa and became the Lord of the South Indian empire extending from Bengal to Ceylon. This King also attempted the annexation of the various islands in the Indian ocean to his empire. He as- sumed the title of Jayakonda Chola; and Tondaimandalam comprising of areas around Tirumalai, Kalahasti, etc. came to be called from then onwards as Jayakonda Chola Man- dalam after him. 
The temple administration during the Chola period was run by managers as in the times of Pallavas. Their work was however supervised and controlled by the officers of this King. It would also appear that the temple servants I were called kaikkolas, as in the case of the temple servants of Tiruchchukanur. The view that the control of the temple was firmly with the King's local officers can be inferred from the following inscription which is actually an Inscription-- judgment2 on the temple affairs of Tirumalai in those days passed by a Chola Adhikari or Provincial Governor. 
"Hail, Prosperity! On the seventh day in the reign of 
1. T.T.D. Epigraphical Series, (222 G.T) Inscriptions pertaining to Tribhuvana Chakravarthigal Sri Raja Raja Devar-p. 55. 2. T.T.D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, Early Inscriptions, Pages 
30-31. 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
165 
Sri Koppatrakesari-panmar alias Sri Rajendra Soladeva, while Adhikari (governor) Korramangalamudaiyan enquired into the affairs of the temple from his seat in the assembly- hall built by us in Tiruchchukanur, a Devadanam of this God, in Kudavur-nadu, (a subdivision) of Tiruvenkata-kot- tam, (a district comprised in the division) of Perumbanap- padi (in the province) of Jayankonda-Solamandalam, having summoned Kadappankudaiyan, the Siritanattuppanimakan (subordinate administrative officer) who administered the Kudavur-nadu, and the servants of Tiruvenkatadevar, and called upon them to depose as to the condition of the perma- nent daily provision (existing) for Tiruvenkatadevar, they stated that, as per the old document registered on stone, the Sabhaiyar of Tirumundiyam, a grant to this god, received money and undertook to light 24 lamps, including one light of camphor, but that the villagers of Tirumundiyam disconti- nued to burn all of them excepting two lights which they still 
burn. 
"Thereupon Adhikari Korramangalamudaiyan ordered that, with the 20 Pon being the sum available at Tiruchchu- kanur and the 3 Pon remaining in deposit in this deity's trea- sury (as remuneration) for the Paricharakam service render- ed by them to this God, aggregating to 23 Pon, (the ghee) might be well-packed and transported to Tirumala along with the articles of daily provision, and the servants working in this behalf might be enabled to burn (the lights) without any impediment. 
"Towards this charity of the Nandavilakku, therefore, to be maintained by the Sabhaiyar of Tirumundiyam, the Srivaishnavas ordered that the 23 Pon belonging to them shall be credited to the capital fund of this God's treasury, (the ghee) shall be transported to this God's treasury (store- house) at Tirumala and the managers of the temple and the 
166 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
servants of Tiruvenkatadevar alone shall burn the lights, in- cluding the Nandavilakku and one Karpura Vilakku, as long as the moon and the sun last". 
The above inscription clearly shows that the provincial governor or Adhikari by name Korramangalamudaiyan sat in judgment over the affairs of the Tirumalai temple. The Siritanattuppanimakan-the local district officer by name Kadappankudaiyan as well as the temple servants were enquired by him about an alleged misuse or non-use of funds placed at the disposal of the Sabhaiyar of Tirumundiyam for lighting 24 lamps to the Lord of Tirumalai; the Adhikari after hearing the evidence of all parties made the Sabhaiyar pay into the Lord's treasury a fine of 23 Pon and insisted that the managers of the temple and the servants of the Tiru- venkatadevar alone should thenceforth burn the lights (nan- davilakku) for the God at Tirumalai. We can infer from the above that the temple administration at that time consisted of the temple servants and a manager, and that the Sabhaiyar or Village councillors of Tirumundiyam village had been made trustees to burn lights in the temple. The treasury of the temple was also called the Sri Bhandaram1 in those days. 
Another inscription of Tribhuvana Chakravarthigal Sri Raja Raja Devar (1216-1254)2 informs us that the famous Thippaladhisvaramudaiya Mahadevar temple at Tiruchanur had a Sthanattar (a local temple council) and that this Sthanattar was functioning as the Trustee for the temple. This inscription shows that the system of Sthanattars existed under the Cholas in Tiruchanur in 1216 A.D; it is therefore possible that this system existed in Tirumalai

also. There is an inscription1 of Tirukkalattidevan Yada- varaya which confirms this view that the sthanattars existed at Tirumalai in the early 13th century; it reads as follows:- 
"Hail! Prosperity! (This is) the arrangement ordered (settled) for the temple of Tiruvenkatamudaiyan, on the request of the Sthanattar of Tirumala, 
"by me, Tirukkalattidevan, entitled the sole shelter of the terrestrial globe, the proud cuckoo of the royal park of all learning, the honey-bee (sucking) at the lotus-feet of (God) Srikalahastisa, the ruler of the prosperous Venkagiri- pura (the city of huntsmen), the chief of huntsmen, the over- lord of Vengi, the recipient of the excellent grace obtained from Pitharadevi (Pidaridevi), the master of the Godavari, the lord of Kanupaka the best city, the best among the learned men, the sporter in Gopakarapura, the retreat of the defeated kings, the ornament of the Lunar race, Chalukya- Narayana, the abode of all (virtuous) qualities, Maharaja- dhiraja, Yadavaraja and Srimadbhujabala......all the Nanjai (wet) and the Punjai (dry) lands comprised within the four boundaries of Kudavur, together with all taxes in- cluding Kadamai-ayam, Padikaval, Vettichcharikai....Kol- vari and Pattampon; and Ponvari and Kanikkai; and Porka- langal including Kanikkai. 
"Having accordingly granted with libations of water (for) the Nimandam, we have directed that Sudarsana stones (marked with the Vishnu-Chakram) be planted along the boundaries on the four sides. This (order) shall be execu- ted". 
The above inscription shows that the Sthanattars of Tirumalai made a request to Tirukkalattidevan Yadavaraya to make adequate provision for the Lord's worship in the temple and that the King then made suitable arrangements. This 
1. T. T. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, Early Inscriptions, pp. 114-115. 
168 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Yadavaraya was a Chola vassal ruling the Tondaimandalam in the early years of the 13th century. 
Sthanattars:-Composition and functions of the Sthana- ttars:-The Sthanattar was a local temple committee, which was primarily charged with the duty of running the temple on efficient lines. It was a secular body, though some of the members of the body unavoidably performed religious func- tions also in the temple. It was an august body which acted as the executive for the local rulers in temple matters; it also acted on its own (more particularly in the later period under Vijayanagar Kings) as the trustee for the temple, re- ceiving endowments and ensuring that the services for which the endowments were made were properly carried out. 
It is however to be remembered that the Sthanattars were under the direct control of the local rulers though they functioned with some measure of independence in several cases. The local rulers issued edicts such as the one quoted below1 and these had to be perforce obeyed by the Sthanat- 
tars. 
"On a certain day in the month of Panguni in the 12th year of the reign of Tribhuvana Chakravartigal Sri Tiruven- katanatha Yadavarayar, intimation (is hereby given) to the Sthanattar of Tirumala, in pursuance of the royal edict con- verting as Sarvamanya (the village of) Tirupati (previously) granted to (God) Tiruvenkatamudaiyan as Tiruvidaiyattam- Tirunamattukkani. 
"The (following) taxes which we are accustomed to col- lect (from the said village are hereby remitted) from the month of Chittirai in the 12th year, (and ordered to be utilis- ed) for the articles of Amuduppadi and Sattuppadi, during the Narayanan-Sandhi, thus converting the said village (of 
1. T. T. D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, Early Inscriptions, pp. 129- 
130. 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
169 
Tirupati) into a Sarvamanya, so as to last as long as the moon and the sun last. This Tiruvaradhanam (propitiatory service) shall proceed from this day onwards without break or dimi- nution. (This edict shall be) engraved on stone and copper. 
"The following are the taxes remitted: 
Pandivari, Palavari, Kanikkai, Vendukolvari, Malai- amanji, Kudiraivari, Kanakkamovai, Nattuvari, Nilai- kkanikkai, Nattarvaitte-kanikkai, Valinadai-kanikkai, Akkan- kanikkai, Kartikai-Kanikkai, Pattivari, Viruttuvakai Pala- talikku-madaiyil-kollum-(vari), Nattar-madaiyil-bagukkum- vari, Vallaladevar-vari, Vasal-panam, Talai-Talaipanam, Nischayippu, Mukamparvai, Padiyarimovai, Mukamparvai- chchammadam, Sambadachchammadam, Settikal, Vanikar, Senai-angadiyar, Sekkukudimakkal, Kaikkolar-per, Sun- gavari-kollumvagai, Sunga-ennai, Pillaikal-kanikkai." 
The above inscription is an edict issued by Tiruvenkata- natha Yadavarayar (1322-1337), a local ruler of Tondai- mandalam, converting the village of Tirupati as a Sarvamanya village and arranging food offerings to the Lord (Narayanan- Sandhi). The Sthanattar of Tirumalai was informed about the arrangements made for compliance of all directions given. It is interesting to note that several local taxes were remitted in the village of Tirupati. The various kinds of taxes indi- cated in the inscription are gold taxes, grain taxes, taxes on free service, tools on animals, poll-tax, profession tax on merchants, oilmongers, weavers, licences for planting gardens, fishing ponds, poundage on stray cattle, etc1. 
Temple Administration under the Telugu Pallavas and Pandyas: The temple has a few inscriptions of the Pandya King Jathavarma Sundara Pandya I (1251-1275) of Madura. It is this king that acquired merit by fixing a gold kalasam on the temple at Tirumalai during his reign. The Chola 
1. T.T.D. Epigraphical Report, pp. 122-123. 
170 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
King Rajendra III was vanquished in battle by this Pandya. His inscriptions do not throw any light on the temple affairs of this period. However there are several inscriptions of the Telugu Pallava, Vijaya-Gandagopala of Nellore (1250- 1282 A.D.). One of the inscriptions1 dated 1254 A.D. men- tions of the 'Kaikkolai' or temple servants of Tirumalai re- ceiving gold in the Sri Bhandaram, and undertaking to pro- vide certain services to the Lord. Another inscription2 mentions of the famous Gandagopala-madai (the gold ma- dais of the Telugu Pallava Gandagopala current in the whole of South India) paid into the Sri Bhandaram. 
Temple Administration under the Vijayanagar Kings:-- The system of Sthanattars settled down during this period. Temple accountants called Tiruninra-ur-udaiyar also made their appearance having powers to maintain accounts. This is a sure sign that this temple became very prosperous during this period, needing the assistance of accountants for the maintenance of temple accounts. The temple account- ant actually first makes his appearance in the year 1380 A.D. 
Another important inscription enabling us to infer the composition of the Sthanattar of Tirumalai, is extracted below:3 
"Hail! On the 11th day of the Margali month in the Pramoduta year, current with the auspicious Saka year 1312, this is the Silasasanam executed by us, the Sthanat- tar of Tirumala, in favour of Mullai-Tiruvenkata-Jiyar, the manager of the Arisanalaiyan-Tirunandavanam (flower- garden) at Tirumala: ...... Of the prasadam offered (to God) at Tirumala, the 26 Nali and 3 Ulakku coming to your share shall be distributed as follows:- deduct- ing 15 Nali and 3 Ulakku as the portion (sesham) for the 
1. T. T. D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, pp. 91-92. (127 G.T). 2. T. T. D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, pp. 98. 
3. T. T. D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. I, pp. 175-176.THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
171 
Arisanalaiyan-Matham, (the balance of) 11 Nali being the Prasadam granted by you to the Sthanattar, the Sthana- ttar shall thus partake of it, as 4 Nirvaham for the Tirupati Srivaishnavas, 3 Nirvaham for the Sabhaiyar of Tiruch- chukanur, 1 Nirvaham for the Nambimar, 2 Nirvaham for the Koyil-kelkum-Jiyars, and 2 Nirvaham for the Koyil- kanakku Tiruninra-ur-udaiyars". 
The above inscription shows that the prasadam was shared between the Srivaishnavas, Tiruchchukanur Sabhai- yar, Nambimar (temple priests), Koyil-kelkum-Jiyars and the two temple accountants. The prasadam was divided into twelve parts and then distributed; we could perhaps infer from the above that the Sthanattar consisted of ten members, not taking into account the two temple accoun- tants. The Srivaishnavas referred to are obviously the 'Ekaki Srivaishnavas' of Ramanuja. It was only one dur- ing Ramanuja's time; and this number increased to four--- within three centuries. These Vaishnavas were obviously performing religious functions such as worship in the tem- ple in addition to their managerial duties. 
Temple Administration during the troubled period of the seventeenth century A.D.:-The last inscription of the Vijayanagar Kings available to us is in the year 1665 A.D.1 The last emperor was Sri Ranga III (1642-72). His prede- cessor Venkata II had in the year 1639 confirmed the grant of the site of the Fort St. George to the East India Company made by one Damarla Venkata, the Nayak belonging to the region of Pulicat and St. Thomas. Madras or Chennapatnam was actually named after Chenna, the father of Damarla Venkata. Sri Ranga III renewed the above concessions to the East India Company. He was respected by the English 
1. From Sri T.K.T. Veera Raghavacharya's History of Tirupati, 
Vol. II, Footnote, p. 864. 
172 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
and the Jesuits in the country as a wise man. He however lost his kingdom. Perhaps his ambition to fight too many foes at one time was one of the causes of his failure. The Nawabs of Golkonda and Bijapur succeeded in acquir- ing his territories and Mir Jumla, who became the Viceroy of Bengal and the Prime Minister of Golkonda, and the Prime Minister to Aurangzeb later obtained Tondaimanda- lam as one of the spoils. From him, it passed to the Nawab of Arcot. This was again the period when the great Shivaji shook the Moghul Empire to its foundations. Not much is known at present about the temple administration of this period. It is however worthy of note that the Muslims as well as the Mahrattas who were in possession of the Carnatic at various times during the troubled period did not interfere with the worship of the temple, realising rather shrewdly that the temple was a very good milch-cow which would yield them much revenue every year. During this period, the temple was also visited by Akkanna and Madhanna, the Ministers of the Nawab of Golkonda. 
Temple Administration under the East India Company:- After the death of Aurangzeb, in 1707, the Moghul empire collapsed in India and it was a period of unrest as far as South India was concerned. The kingdoms of Golkonda and Bijapur also disappeared, and the Mahrattas were waxing strong during this period. The British and the French were also poised against one another, each trying to establish supre- macy in the country over the other. The role of the English under Sir Robert Clive, the struggle of the French under Duplex and Bussy, to establish a foothold in South India, the Carnatic wars in which the Nawab of Carnatic was a helpless pawn, is history which is too well known to require repetition here. 
During the early years of this period, the mother of 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
173 
Bajirao and Bajirao's wife are said to have worshipped the Lord of Tirumalai at Tirupati. 
Robert Clive himself was not above utilising the revenues of the temple during this period to further the interests of the East India Company. The Nawab of Arcot had appointed a rentier in Tirumalai and leased out the revenue of the tem- ple. The revenues of the temple were assigned to the English, and it is said that the English obtained about 3 lakhs rupees in the years 1753-58 from the temple. The French also who with watering mouths looked envious and desired to appro- priate the revenues of the temple, once successfully occupied Tirupati in 1758; the reason was to strengthen themselves with the revenues of the temple. The star of the East India Company was however in the ascendant and the French were completely rooted out of the country. The Company was thus left as the sole enjoyer of the revenues of the temple. In the year 1801 during the Governor-Generalship of Lord Wellesley, some correspondence said to incriminate the Nawab of Carnatic was trumped up by the East India Com- pany and summary action speedily taken to annex the Carna- tic. The Nawab of Carnatic was quietly pensioned off, and thus the temple directly came under the administration of the East India Company. 
The temple lands were resumed immediately by the Company, and the staff in the temple were paid out of the Company's funds while the entire revenues of the temple were appropriated to the Company's coffers. An article published by a District Collector of Tirupati area in the Asiatic Journal quoted by Sri T.K.T. Veera Raghavacharya in his book History of Tirupati mentions about the temple administration under the East India Company. The relevant extracts are reproduced below, and show the rapacity with which the Company robbed the temple of its revenues: 
174 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
"The whole of the revenue of the temple, from what- ever source derived, are under the management of and ap- propriated by the circar or government. A regular establish- ment is entertained paid by salaries; and a horde of brahmins are maintained by lands, appropriated for that purpose, over the face of the surrounding country, called manniyams or estates enjoyed on very favourable terms under government, subject only to a slight jody or tax. The temple is kept up in all its dignity; and the average receipts on the account of government, for the last ten years, or fuslies, will show what a good thing we make of it. The head general officer there is called Tahsildar, whose business is the general superinten- dence over the others; to see the pilgrims are well treated and lodged in proper places; supplies kept fairly in the bazaars, ceremonies duly performed as paid for, and that no bribery, tyranny or oppression of any sort takes place. He reports to the Collector or his assistants, according to orders, on all cases of doubt or importance. Next is the Sheristadar or head native accountant, who acts under the Tahsildar, assists him in all his responsible duties, and superintends the regu- larly kept accounts of all disbursements and receipts for the use of the temple. Under him are four gumastas or native writers. The rysagu is a police clerk and is assisted by a ghola and obeys the Tahsildar in all magisterial matters. Common servants are allowed for taking care of dufters, or records, lighting, sweeping the cutchery, etc. and twenty peons at- tend the Tahsildar's cutchery and are paid a pagoda (8 shillings) a month; with the duffedars or head peons with larger salary. During the Brahmotsavam or nine days festi- val an additional sibbandi is allowed with twenty-five peons, twenty putwars (and that kind of peon in lower salaries) and five hircurraks or scouts to bring intelligence of the arrival of any pilgrim of rank, and to keep the peace among the 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
175 
enormous crowd that annually fills this part of the country. They also assist the Tahsildar with forty or fifty peons, a party of whom are constantly on the hills looking out for the thieves who congregate where the prey may be, whilst another party range the country, assisted by the village police, who apprehend suspicious characters. A company also of sepoys under a native officer are stationed as the Tahsildar may choose as permanent guards. The temple has attached to it a granary and store house under charge of Jeengar and Ekangi or his deputy. These have under them a gumasta, an allowagar or grain measurer, three peons, a sandal-wood carrier, a flower carrier, a musk extractor, a pottu, a wood- man for the cook, a golla, a sweeper, a mossoli or man to light up the place. The Tahsildar always sees that the store has a sufficient stock for any sum upon it. He keeps regular accounts of all that enters the granary. The Jeengar keeps account of all that is disbursed upon the orders of the Parapatyadar or manager of the temple. The Parapatyadar or manager supplies daily ration of food to all the numerous servants of the temple, sees that they all do their several duties and is second only to the Tahsildar in general super- intendence. He takes care that the gifts presented are duly disposed of, searches the guards and others over the place for receiving the offerings and with the result of the day's oblations accompanies them to the Tahsildar who with him seals up in gunny bags whatever is collected in money, jewels and copper plates, etc. Attached to this officer is a gumasta, allowagar and four peons, and he keeps another account of all he receives from the stores. The offerings of foods, sweets, etc., presented by the pilgrims are prepared for them by his orders and his accounts are counter-examined by the Tahsildar. The Parapatyadar, previous to drawing in any 
176 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
large quantity on the store, sends the estimate to the Tahsil- dar who signs it. 
Passing through the Bangaru Vakili or silver porch the pilgrims are admitted into a rather confined part and are introduced to the God in front of whom are two vessels, one called the gangalam or vase, the other Kopra or large cup and into these things votaries drop their respective offerings and making their obeisance, pass out through another door. At the close of the day the guards both of peons and sepoys round these vessels are searched. Without examination of any sort, offerings are thrown into bags and are sealed first with the seal of the pagoda, then by the Tahsildar and Jeengar; after which the bag is sent down to the cutchery below the hill-Govindarazu pettai. At the end of the month, these bags are transmitted to our cutchery here or huzur (meaning the presence); and they are there opened, sorted, valued and finally sold at auction. However, during the Brahmotsavam either the Collector or a subordinate must be on the spot owing to the value of the offerings, and to see that no cheating takes place for want of power of immediate re- presentation. And on this duty I was bound when I wrote from Chandragiri. I have little more to add except the The annual net average revenue of the last ten years. 
proceeds from this source is about eighty-seven thousand rupees. In 1822, collections were one lakh forty-two thou- sand and odd but this is exclusive of expenses, wherewith twenty thousand may be deducted. In 1820 or fasli 1230, the collections were 1,02,000." 
A pilgrimage to Tirupati in the year 1830:-Enugu Veera Swami's Journal gives a vivid account of a pilgrimage to Tirupati in the year 1830.1 The author who made a tour 
1. Enugu Veeraswami's Journal by Pidatala Sitapati (under pub- lication in the Oriental Manuscripts Research Institute, Direc- torate of State Archives, Hyderabad). 
177 
of the country in this year stayed at Tirupati for several days and some of his observations on Tirupati given below are worth noting. "The shrines of Lord Govindarajaswami and Lord Kodanda Ramaswami are located at Diguva Tiru- pati. The shrine of Lord Kodanda Ramaswami does not get any grant or help from the government. The shrine of Lord Govindaraja is with acharyapurushas, under the gene- ral control of the government. The Vedas are being taught here in a school. Sadavarthi is being given here to Gosains and others, thanks to the benefactions of Chandulal and two other pious souls. There are two or three Ramanujaku- tams also here and Srivaishnavas are given free prasad here daily. This is a big place and a township. All articles are available in the market here. Artisans and workmen with various kinds of skills live here. The waters of Narasimha theertham only are potable. Kapila theertham is located at a distance of two Ghadiyas from Tirupati and is an enchant- ing place. There is a perennial waterfall here and this water is ponded at the bottom of the hill; the theertham itself is enclosed in a spacious mantapam. This mantapam is very suitable for giving feasts to brahmins. The benefactions of Chandulal in the sacred places around this region are to be particularly mentioned. 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
"In view of the cold, I stayed on the hills during the day time one day only. The distance between Diguva Tiru- pati and Tirumalai where the Lord's temple is located is 24 amadas. The ascent upto the Galigopuram along with the descent from Galigopuram is rather arduous. There is level land thereafter on the pilgrim's walk to the hills. The ascents and descents in the pilgrim's walk on the rest of the way however are not as difficult as on the climbs at Galigo- puram. There are drinking water facilities and several man- tapas on the way. A bairagi stationed near the Galigo- 
178 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
puram is supplying free butter-milk to pilgrims. An idol of Sri Rama is being worshipped here by the bairagi at the Galigopuram. The East India Company is obtaining a lakh of rupees as revenue at the Tirupati temple by way of offer- ings made to the Lord. Every proceeding and rite on the hill entails the payment of suitable fees to the government here. While the Lord thus is bestowing his blessings on his devotees cleansing away their sins, they are also out of pocket due to the system of collection of various fees here! Lord Srinivasamurti who has manifested Himself here is a Divyamangalamurti. This Lord, it appears, has been wor- shipped from ancient times by celestial and great personages. There is a Mahant's Matham here built very spaciously- which is the headquarters of Gosains and bairagis. This Mahant has disciples in large numbers all over the country. The houses on the hill are congested and small. The cold wind blows here during the period between Chaitram and Jyestham. This cold wind is injurious to the health of the inhabitants on the hill. There is also much nuisance due to monkeys here. Boars also move rather freely among the people on the hills. During the cold-wind season, men do not stay on the hills in large numbers. Paying a fee of Rs. 220 Aratirupavada worship was performed by me, with offerings of Prasad to the Lord." 
Veeraswami's account shows that the East India Com- pany was appropriating to itself a lakh of rupees every year from this temple. The system of collection of fees, it ap- pears, was as unpleasant to Veeraswami in 1830 as it is now to many of us. The cold winds are still there now at Tiru- malai, but thanks to the developmental measures undertaken after Independence by the Andhra Pradesh Government, the hazards to health have vanished. The nuisance due to monkeys and pigs still continues! 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
179 
The management of the temple was in accordance with the Madras Regulation VII of 1817. This was a regulation for the appropriation of rents and produce of lands granted for supporting mosques, temples, etc. and for the custody of escheats. This regulation empowered the Board of Revenue to superintend the endowments and ensure that the revenue from the original endowments were put to proper use. 
The District Collector working under the Board of Revenue became the authority for such superintendence. Under sec- tion 8 of the above regulation, the District Collector became the ex-officio agent of the State Government for the above purpose. 
The administration of the Tirupati temple during the Company's time was according to a set of rules called the Bruce's Code. 
This state of affairs continued till the year 1841 when the Board of Directors of the East India Company decided that the Company should not interfere in the affairs of the tem- ples in India. The T. T. Devasthanam was consequently trans- ferred by a sanad to the Head of the Hathiramji Mutt at Tirupati. 
Temple Administration under the Mahants of the Hathiramji Mutt:-During the management of the temple by the Mahants, the Swami Pushkarini as well as the Mar- kandeya, Agni, Yama, Vasishta, Varuna, Vayu, Kubera and Galava thirthams were renovated. The other inscriptions in Volume VI of the T. T. Devasthanams Inscriptions show that the Kapila thirtham as well as the surrounding mantapams were renovated during the time of the Vicha- ranakarthas. Sri Mahant Sevadasji is also said to have constructed the outer Gopuram of the temple at Tirumalai called the Padikkavali Gopuram along with certain other 
1. T. T. D. Epigraphical Series, Vol. VI, (350 T.T.), pp. 219-220. 
180 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
mantapams. This was in the year 1878 A.D. Obviously the mention is regarding his renovation of the outer Gopuram. Inscription No. 242 in the same Volume also informs us that one Ramalakshmanadasa fixed a gold kalasam on the Vimanam of the temple in the year 1908. 
"Two rest houses were (also) constructed at Tirupati and 14 sheds put up at Tirumalai, besides fifteen quarters in the thousand-pillared mantapam in Tirumalai. Sanitary conveniences were (also) provided and the pathway made safer by the installation of electric lights throughout the route in 1929,"1 under the Mahants. 
From the Ananda Nilaya Vimanam Bulletin quoted above, it is also learnt that several suits were filed against the Mahants for rectifying 'certain alleged abuses' and that the District Court, Chittoor, settled a scheme which was later amended by the Privy Council in appeal. Soon after- wards, the Madras Hindu Religious Endowments Act 1927 was enacted and the scheme of the Privy Council was deemed as the scheme framed under the above Act. In practice this scheme finalised by the Privy Council also appears to have worked rather unsatisfactorily. The T.T.D. Act of 1932 was therefore passed to improve the administration of the Devasthanam. This Act vested the administration of the T.T.D. in a Committee of seven members. A Com- missioner was also appointed to supervise the affairs of the temple by the Madras Government under this Act. This Act of 1932 was superseded by the Madras Hindu Religious and Charitable Endowments Act of the year 1951. 
Temple Administration under the T.T.D. Act, 1932 and the Madras Hindu Religious and Charitable Endowments Act:-Sections 80 and 85 of the Madras Hindu Religious 
1. T. T. D. Bulletin-Ananda Nilaya Vimanam Samprokshanam 
Special Supplement dated 26-8-1958.THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
181 
and Charitable Endowments Act, 1951, deal exclusively with the administration of the T.T. Devasthanams. Section 80 envisages the appointment of a Board of Trustees for the Devasthanams consisting of five members appointed by the State Government. The members are to hold office for a term of five years. The Act also provides for the appoint- ment of an Executive Officer who "(1) shall have power to manage the properties and affairs of the Devasthaaams; (2) shall be responsible for the proper custody of all the records and of all the jewels, valuables, moneys, funds and other properties of the Devasthanams; (3) shall arrange for the proper collection of the income and for the incurring of expenditure; and (4) may, in cases of emergency, direct the execution of any work or the doing of any act which is not provided for in the budget for the year and the immediate execution or the doing of which is, in his opinion, necessary for the preservation of the properties of the Devasthanams or for the service or safety of the pilgrims resorting thereto; and may direct that the expenses of executing such work or the doing of such act shall be paid from the funds of the Devasthanams. The executive officer shall report forthwith to the Board of Trustees any action taken by him under clause (4) and the reasons therefor." 
Under the Act, the Commissioner, Hindu Religious and Charitable Endowments, has certain powers, such as deciding disputes, appropriation of Endowments, etc. 
Section 85 lays down the purposes for which the Devas- thanam's funds may be used and is reproduced below: "The funds of the Devasthanams may be utilised for any purpose permitted by any other provision of this Act and also for all or any of the following purposes:- 
(i) the maintenance, management and administration 
of the Devasthanams;

The Andhra Pradesh Charitable and Hindu Religious Institutions and Endowments Act of 1966:-The previous Act of 1951 is no longer in force now as a new Act (Act No. 17 of 1966) called the Andhra Pradesh Charitable and Hindu Religious Institutions and Endow- ments Act came into force recently. Chapter 14 of the above Act makes express provisions for the administration 
183 
of the T. T. Devasthanams. Sections 85 to 91 of the Act lay down the provisions governing the T. T. Devasthanams. Section 86 lays down that the administration of the temple should be by a Board of Trustees consisting of not more than 11 persons to be appointed by the Government, three of whom are to be members of the State Legislature, one person to belong to the scheduled castes and another to be a woman (subject to certain provisions made in Section 86). The Members of the Board of Trustees hold office for a term of three years. Section 87 directs the Government to appoint an Executive Officer and where necessary a Deputy Execu- tive Officer for the temple. Under section 87 (2) the Execu- tive Officer is empowered to delegate statutory duties to his Deputy Executive Officer. Sections 88 and 89 lay down divi- sion of punitory powers on the employees of the Devasthanam between the Executive Officer and the Board of Trustees. Section 91 lays down the purposes for which the Devastha- nam funds may be used, and is re-produced below:-
