The enabling provisions of the Act have been used to good purpose by the Devasthanam Committee and several improvements made to the temple. Some of the more im- portant achievements1 are indicated below:- 
Information Centres:-Information Centres and Choul- tries have been opened by the Devasthanam not only at 
1. Material for this portion of the Chapter has been taken pri- marily from the T.T.D. Bulletin-Ananda Nilaya Vimanam Samprokshanam Special Supplement dated 26-8-1958. 
186 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Tirupati and Tirumalai but in other important cities in India. Several enquiry offices have also been opened to assist the pilgrims desirous of proceeding to Tirumalai to worship the Lord of Tirumalai. 
Accommodation:-Prior to 1934 accommodation for pilgrims in Tirupati and Tirumalai was rather meagre. The Devasthanams have tackled this problem vigorously and several cottages, choultries, guest houses, etc. have been constructed both at Tirupati and Tirumalai. There is however need for more accommodation. 
Sanitation and water supply:-Sanitation and water supply has been improved; protected water is supplied to Tirumalai from the Papanasanam water works. The sani- tation of the town has been improved, malaria wiped out and a drainage scheme introduced. 
Transport to Tirumalai:-Prior to the opening of a Ghat road between Tirupati and Tirumalai in 1944, pilgrims could reach Tirumalai only by climbing the hill by foot by taking the old sopana-marga. After the opening of the fourteen mile-long Ghat road, pilgrims can reach Tirumalai by road quickly and safely. The Devasthanam which started with two buses to transport pilgrims in the year 1944 has now an impressive fleet of buses. There is need however to reorganise the Department and provide for more buses as indicated separately. As a result, the revenue of the tem- ple which was about 11 lakhs of rupees in 1933 has risen to about Rs. two crores. The Devasthanam's main income is from the offerings of the devotees in the Hundi, arjitham fees paid by the pilgrims for special Sevas etc. It also de- rives income from the auctioning of human hair offered to the Lord. It is said that the Devasthanams obtained about 6 lakhs of rupees in 1962-63 by way of selling human hair and another 51 lakhs by sale of prasadams. The Devas- 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
187 
thanam is said to have obtained 11 lakhs of rupees by way of sale of gold and silver, offered to the Lord (Hundi collec- tions) in the year 1962-63. 
Food and drinks:-The Devasthanam is also maintain- ing several canteens for the benefit of the pilgrims. The sale of vegetarian food and soft drinks itself for the year 1962-63 is said to have come to about Rs. 6 lakhs. 
Education:-The Devasthanam maintains several educational institutions such as the Oriental College, three high schools, three colleges for men and women, several elementary schools, a Veda Patashala and Nadaswaram Patasala, a training school for sculpture and an Arts Col- lege at Delhi. A Public School has also been recently opened in Horsely Hills in Chittoor District. The Devas- thanam is said to be contributing about 2 lakhs of rupees per annum for the maintenance of the Sri Venkateswara University at Tirupati. A Gosala and a dairy with about 300 Sindhi cows are being maintained by the Devasthanam. 
T.T.D. Press and Publicity Department:-A printing press is run by the Devasthanam for printing religious lite- rature. The publicity department conducts religious dis- courses. The thousand-pillared mantapam at Tirumalai has been converted into a Sadas and religious discourses, Harikathas and Bhajans are held in the Sadas every day. Learned Pandits, famous musicians etc. are invited to this Sadas often. 
Other amenities to Pilgrims:-The entire route from Tirupati to Tirumalai is illuminated by electric lights. The Devasthanam provides free accommodation and free medical aid to pilgrims at Tirumalai. 
Research: The Oriental Institute started in 1938 does valuable research work in Sanskrit, Telugu and Tamil and 
1. It is learnt that this school has been closed now. 
188 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
has collected several rare and valuable manuscripts. The Institute is at present under the management of the Sri Ven- kateswara University. 
Charitable Institutions:-A Leprosy Home was started in the year 1939, and is under the charge of a medical officer. An orphanage started in the year 1943 is also run by the Devasthanam. 
Ruia Hospital:-A philanthropist of Bombay, Sri Radhakrishna Ruia, donated five lakhs of rupees for a three- hundred-bed hospital at Tirupati. The Devasthanam do- nated ten lakhs of rupees for this project in addition to giving a four hundred acre plot for the hospital. This hospital is very well equipped. 
Improvements to the temple1:-"The Dhvajastambha of Sri Govindarajaswami temple and the Balipeetam in Tiru- mala temple were gold gilded in 1934-35. A gem-set flower was prepared for being fixed in the Gold Kireet of Sri Venkateswara Swami in the year 1939. A silver car was made at a cost of Rs. 1 lakh in 1941. The Maharatham was also made for Tirumalai Sri Varu in the year 1942. A gem-set kireetam at a cost of 10 lakhs of rupees was prepared in the year 1945. The Makara Thoranam of Sri Varu was refixed in 1947. A gem-set Vaikuntha Hastha at a cost of 3 lakhs of rupees was made and adorned in 1954." The conch and chakra of the Lord have also been set with invaluable gems recently. 
"Renovation works were (also) started from 1950 on- wards. The inner Gopuram of Sri Govindaraja shrine, the Padikavali Gopuram of Sri Ramaswami temple, the gold gilding of the Kalasam of the Gopuram of Sri Padmavati temple at Tiruchanoor, remaking of the Bimbas of the 
1. From the T.T.D. Bulletin-Ananda Nilaya Vimanam Sam- prokshanam special supplement dated 26-8-1958. 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
189 
Mukha mandapam in Govindaraja shrine and Padmavati temple, the construction of the Padikavali and the inner Gopurams of Sri Venkateswara temple are some of the items worthy of mention." 
The Ananda Vimanam for the Lord's temple was also renovated and regilded as already indicated in Chapter VI at a cost of Rs. 18 lakhs. 
However more remains to be done. Suggestions for giving better facilities and amenities to pilgrims are indicated below: 
(1) Motto for the Administration:-The main objective for the administration should be the welfare of the pilgrims. All steps should be taken to afford the pilgrims all facilities for worshipping the Lord with full devotion and peace of mind. 
(2) Sanctity of the Temple:-Steps should be taken to keep up the sanctity of the temple at Tirumalai. In this connection the reported observations of His Holiness the Jagadguru Sri Kanchi Kamakoti Sankaracharya Swamy is relevant. Referring to the recent tragedy at Tirumalai, Sri Sankaracharya said that "it was his personal opinion that it was the cumulative result of the apacharams (wrongs) committed by some devotees willingly or unwillingly, though innocent people died in the tragedy. It was common know- ledge that Tirumalai was being used for excursions and picnics by young men and women, as a pleasure resort in- stead of as a place of worship." (The Hindu). All steps should be taken to see that the sacred atmosphere in Tiru- malai and Tirupati is kept up. Committing of nuisance in and around the temple knowingly or unknowingly should be severely discouraged and punished. 
(3) Increasing of Number of Hours of Dharma Darsa- nam. Steps to avoid congestion of pilgrims:-Projections of 
190 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
pilgrim traffic visiting the temple on any day during the next ten years indicate that the pilgrim traffic may go up to the alarming figure of 50,000 to 60,000 a day. Even at present the minimum number of pilgrims that visit the temple on festival days works out to about 20,000. On ordinary days the pilgrim traffic will be between 10,000 to 15,000. Assum- ing that five seconds time is given to each pilgrim, the time to be set apart for the darshan of 20,000 pilgrims visiting the temple a day works out to 28 hours. Thus even a full day is not enough for darshan of the Lord. Even if the time is reduced to 21 seconds for each pilgrim, the time within which 20,000 pilgrims can have darshan works out to 14 hours. At present the time given for dharmadarshan is only 12 hours. This indicates the urgency to increase the number of hours for dharmadarshanam. 
The temple should therefore be open throughout the day and night and closed only for an hour or two between 12 midnight and 1 A.M. in the morning, to accommodate agamic prescriptions if any. 
(4) Arjithams and Dharmadarshanam:-The recom- mendation given in this para flows out of the recommen- dations made above. While it is conceded that certain pujas such as the Sathumarai etc. have to be compulsorily performed in the temple, there is no reason why valuable tem- ple time should be denied for dharmadarshanam by catering to arjitha seva pilgrims i.e. sevas in which money is collected and darshanam privilege given only to pilgrims who have the capacity to pay. After making allowance of time for the pujas and rituals which are a 'must' according to the Agamas, the time available could be reserved only for Dharmadar- shanam. The temple of Sri Venkateswara should not be converted into a commercial concern where admission of pilgrims is done by collection of fees. The OmnipresentTHE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
191 
and all-loving God should be accessible to all devotees freely. It is also felt that by eliminating arjitham pujas there will not be any significant reduction in the revenues of the temple. Devotees will continue to give their 'Mudupus' and offerings to the Lord whether arjitham pujas are open to them or not. 
(5) Arjitham Festivals:-It is also for consideration whether arjitham festivals should not be performed in the Kalyanamantapam before the Utsava Vigraham only and not before the Moola Vigraham Deity. The 'Sankalpam' could perhaps be done before the Lord in the 'Garbhagudi' and the detailed rituals performed before the Utsava Vigra- ham. It would be relevant in this connection to point out that worshipping a proxy image is a historically accepted fact. Inscriptional evidence of the temple itself will show that a proxy image of Lord Venkateswara was being wor- shipped in the Tiruchanur temple in the year 850 A.D. to assist pilgrims who were not in a position to undertake the difficult journey up the hill to Tirumalai. 
(6) Second door-way for the Temple:-In view of the increasing number of pilgrims it is also necessary to consider whether a second doorway can be opened in the temple, to function as an exit to the pilgrims who visit the temple. It is a well known fact that the temple of Srirangam has got gopurams and gopura dwarams on four sides-East, West, North and South. Even Vaishnavite temples, where the worship is according to Vaikhanasa agama (as in Tirumalai) have such gopurams and gopura dwarams, for example, Sri Parthasarathy temple in Triplicane etc. In case of any objection to making structural changes in the temple area by the Agama pandits, the question of having a subway in the circumambulatory path going by the name of Vimana Pradakshinam or a staircase in the outer Pradakshinam lead- 
192 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
ing out, may be considered. The Varaha Purana and other puranas mention that one Tondaiman Chakravarthy was visiting the temple through a tunnel in the olden days, and opening a sub-way in the Vimana Pradakshinam would not be such a modern idea after all! 
(7) Agama Pandits and Religious Gurus Committee:- It is desirable to have a Standing Committee of Agama Pandits and Religious Gurus to discuss and advise the temple authorities in various matters connected with the temple as well as to consider and recommend suggestions which will improve the religious and sacred atmosphere of Tirumalai. 
(8) Strengthening the Administration:-There is need to strengthen the administration at Tirumalai. There is urgent need to strengthen the police force in particular. The need for establishing a prohibition station on the hills also has been recognised. The T.T.D. transport adminis- tration needs strengthening and at least a minimum of 100 buses should be on the road every day. 
The T.T.D. administration is at present controlled by a Board of Trustees constituted under section 86 of the Andhra Pradesh Charitable and Hindu Religious Institutions and Endowments Act. The question whether the number of members in the Board of Trustees can be reduced with ad- vantage to step up efficiency is an aspect which may be con- sidered by the Government. 
The Executive Officer (according to section 87) has to perform several statutory duties and delegation of powers by him is only possible to the Deputy Executive Officer under section 87 (2) of the Act, No. 17 of 1966. It is acknowledged that the Executive Officer is over-burdened with much avoidable routine work. It should be worth considering whether large scale delegation of powers can be given to 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
193 
officers working under the Executive Officer to enable him to exercise effective control over the huge administrative orga- nisation working under the T.T.D. Board. 
The Executive Officer is also at present subject to control and check by the Head of the department i.e. the Commis- sioner of Endowments and can approach the Government only through the Head of the Department and this results in delay. The question of upgrading the post of the Execu- tive Officer of the T.T.D. to that of the Commissioner of Endowments and vesting him with powers to deal directly with the Government needs urgent consideration. 
(9) A well-equipped Hospital at Tirumalai:-There is urgent need for a well-equipped hospital with sufficient bed- strength and modern equipment. 
(10) Second Ghat Road:-The work on the second ghat road should be expedited. 
(11) Aerodrome at Renigunta:-The construction of an aerodrome at Renigunta connecting important cities like Hyderabad, Madras, Delhi etc. and the construction of a Helepad with helicopter facilities at Tirumalai need urgent consideration. 
(12) Accommodation:-Accommodation available for the poorer sections of the pilgrims is very inadequate in spite of the valiant efforts being made by the temple. There is urgent need to provide more and more accommodation to the poorer sections of the pilgrims, in preference to the richer 
sections. 
(13) Pilgrim queues inside the Temple:-Pilgrim col- lection halls should be put up near the Pushkarani quadran- gle a little away from the temple and facilities for pilgrims such as bath rooms, urinals etc. should be made available to them. Pilgrim queues starting from this quadrangle can go around the temple prakaram i.e. near the back portion of the 
S.V.-8 
194 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
temple and be made to enter the temple from the police station side facing the temple Mukhadwaram. Sitting facili- ties should be made for the pilgrims, while they are moving outside the temple. The existing practice of leaving the pil- grims in ill-lighted and ill-ventilated queue-stands of the tem- ple for hours together should stop. The temple authorities should see that any pilgrim entering the Mukhadwaram of the temple should be able to obtain the darshanam of the Lord with minimum delay in about 20 to 25 minutes. Pilgrims specially women and children are not able to withstand the strain of waiting for long in closed stands and much nuisance is committed in the queue-stands of the temple by children, spoiling the sanctity of the temple. As these stands are very un-hygienic, queues may be regulated only outside the temple by providing shelter covers above the queue-stands and action taken to see that darshanam is given to pilgrims with minimum delay. 
(14) Pradakshinam around the Deity:-Pilgrims hav- ing darshanam of the Lord move about now from right to left and not left to right. This apradakshina movement may be avoided. 
(15) Better Drinking Water Facilities and First Aid Arrangements:-Better drinking water facilities and first aid arrangements should be made available to the pilgrims on the Hill. 
(16) Lighting at Tirumalai:-The electric lighting at Tirumalai may be further improved. 
(17) Pilgrim Guides:-At present there are no Pilgrim Guides on the hill to guide and assist the pilgrims. Pilgrim Guides may be appointed by the Devasthanam to assist the pilgrims. 
(18) Uniform to T.T.D. employees and Pilgrim Guides:- There is urgent need to give a distinctive uniform to T.T.D. 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
195 
employees and Pilgrim Guides. This will facilitate easy recognition by the pilgrims needing their help. 
(19) Pilgrim welfare:-The approach of the adminis- tration should be to cater to the welfare and needs of the pilgrim. Courtesy should be extended to every pilgrim visiting Tirumalai, howsoever poor. 
The District officers such as the District Collector and the Superintendent of Police should be more actively associat- ed with the functioning of the T.T.D. Board so that they may be able to help and be helped in the due discharge of their functions and promote the welfare of pilgrims visiting the Tirumalai. 
(20) Urgency to streamline the T.T.D. administration:- It is commonly felt that more could be done by the their Devasthanam administration taking into account resources position and the sympathetic attitude of the Govern- ment of Andhra Pradesh to help them in improving the tem- ple administration. It is felt that a very senior officer prefer- ably a retired Chief Secretary should be appointed as a one-man Committee to study in detail the working of the T.T.D. administration as it is at present and to recommend measures to streamline the administration to give it a pilgrim-welfare-oriented approach. 
(21) Perspective planning:-The problems of the T.T.D. administration vis-a-vis the pilgrim who goes in all humility and devotion to obtain the blessing of Lord Venka- teswara can only be solved by a study of all the problems as a single unit and by resorting to perspective planning over a period of the next 20 years. This perspective planning ap- proach is lacking at present. Unless such a perspective plan is drawn up and resorted to, pilgrim satisfaction will not be achieved. A scientific approach and scientific attitude should 
196 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
be developed to deal with some of the problems connected with pilgrim welfare. In problems connected with wor- shipping of the Lord and temple rituals, the administration should not interfere with existing practices unless a com- petent standing conference of religious Gurus and Agama Pandits sanction changes. The T.T.D. administration should more and more confine itself to religious duties and not fritter away its energies and resources in tackling secular functions and social welfare functions which rightly fall under the ambit of the Government of Andhra Pradesh and the Government of India. Devotees go to Tirumalai in all humility and devotion in thousands every day to worship Sri Venkateswara, the Lord of the seven hills. The aim of the administration should be to see that these devotees are assured peace of mind and celestial joy by giving them all facilities to have happy darshan of Lord Venkateswara. 
A heart-rending tragedy occurred in the precincts of the temple on 12th August 1967 resulting in the death of eighteen pilgrims waiting in the queue-stands of the temple. Such tragedies should not recur. The administration should be ever vigilant, reorganise itself and plan effectively for the future if Tirupati is to be 'Kaliyuga Vaikuntham'. If there is a paradise on earth, yes, it is at Tirumalai. As Nammalwar puts it 'the Valarvoli Mayan has manifested Himself at Tirumalirumsolai surrounded by Divya Udyana- vanas. Worship Him in this Vaikuntha on earth while you can and art young and before old age overtakes thee'. 
Several of these suggestions were made by the author of this book as District Collector, Chitoor, in the years 1966-1968. It is gratifying to record that many of them have been accepted and efforts made by the Tirupati-Tirumalai Devasthanams to cater to the requirements of the poor pil- 
THE ADMINISTRATION OF THE TEMPLE 
197 
grim. It is the poor pilgrim who stands uncomplainingly in the never-ending queues who is the real devotee of the Lord; and the objective of the T.T.D. administration should be to give full facilities for the worship of the Lord by these real devotees who are near and dear to Him always. 
CHAPTER XI 
CONCLUSION-THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTHI 
The Lord of Tirumalai, Sri Venkateswara, is the Lord of our hearts. He is the Lord of the Universe, the Lord of all creation and is the Light of the world. He has mani- fested Himself on Tirumalai in India as Shri Narayana to lead his devotees to ultimate freedom. This is clear from his being a Katyavalambita Murthi, a pose which indicates that the Lord's protection and release from all bondages is assured to those worshipping His feet with constant devotion. He is no other than Lord Krishna, the embodi- ment of Love. He is no other than Siva, the Lord of Aiswaryam and Destruction. He is no other than Shri Rama, the embodiment of Dharma, Truth, Beauty and Courage. The Lord is a Varada Murthi-one who bestows his divine grace liberally on his devotees. He is the Katya- valambita Murthi and protector or Rakshaka for his Bhak- thas who trust him and worship Him. The Katyavalambita Murthi is One who is easily realised through Bhakti and Prapatti. 
It is interesting to speculate here on the origin of Bhakti in India. Majumdar traces the beginnings of Bhakti in India to the hymns of Rigveda. Actually Bhakti is as old as Man, and the adoration or leaning on a higher power is an emotional quality in Man derived from the herd ins- tincts of the mammals and the instinct of Fear of the Un- known. In those dark ages of the Mind, when Man was evolving out his consciousness, he must have feared what- 
1. Bhakti Renaissance by A. K. Majumdar, Bharatiya Vidya 
Bhavan, Bombay, p. 1. 
CONCLUSION-THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 
199 
ever he did not understand and adored the Sun, the Moon, the rain, the trees, the Fire etc. which lent him support. The early man was afraid of darkness and the Sun dispelled for him this darkness. The Fire again was wonderful as it kept him warm and protected him from wild beasts. It is no wonder that the fear of the Unknown and the support which he obtained from natural phenomena gave birth in Man for the first time to emotions which fathered the ideas of Bhakti and love towards the Supreme Spirit. The begin- nings of Bhakti should therefore actually be traced to the days when the first prehistoric Indians inhabited the south- eastern regions of India in the stone ages. 
The Dravidians themselves are supposed to have come to India later and settled down to an agricultural life about the period between 3000 and 4000 B.C. According to Haimendorf they came to South India by sea from the West and not only imposed their speech on the people before them in the South, but soon learnt to integrate themselves with the beneficial Indo-Aryan influences from Northern India.2 The Mohenjo Daro and Harappa civilizations which were pre-Aryan civilisations dating back to the Dravi- dian period deserve our notice. These civilizations are ascribed to Dravidians. Whoever they were, Dravidians or others, they certainly had a well-developed religion wor- shipping a male God, who could perhaps be recognised as Siva, a Mother Goddess and other Gods in the forms of animals and snakes. Siva (Rudra) and Vishnu later be- came the prominent Hindu deities in Vedic India. Siva was of course the more ancient deity between the two. 
1. The Vedic rituals with the objective of propitiating the Gods through Fire must be traced to these prehistoric days when Man began to develop a consciousness of his own. 
2. Development of Religion in South India by K.A. Neelakanta 
Sastry, pp. 2 to 24, Orient Longmans, 1963. 
200 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
What exactly is Bhakti may then be briefly considered "The word Bhakti is derived from the root Bhaj by the application of the kti suffix, which expresses an action, (Panini, III, 3, 94) and means among other things service, devotion, attachment, loyalty, worship and homage. According to the Sandilya-bhakti-sutra, bhakti is paranu- raktir-isvare which may be rendered as 'infinite attachment to God' while according to the Narada-bhakti-sutra it is Sa-tasmin parama-prema-rupa; that is, infinite love for God. The Narada-bhakti-sutra then states:- 
"(1) According to the son of Parasara (i.e. Vyasa), bhakti is attachment to worship of God etc.; (2) according to Garge, bhakti is fondness for hearing the stories of the various sports of the Lord; (3) according to Sandilya, bhakti is such attachment to God which is not opposed to self; and (4) according to Narada, bhakti consists in offering all activities to God and inducing a feeling of extreme restless- ness and misery at the slightest lapse in remembrance of God; and then the author of the Narada-bhakti-sutra adds asty-evam-evam, that is bhakti is exactly as described above. Thus it is apparent that by the time that the Narada-bhakti- sutra was written, there were several definitions of bhakti, indicating several schools of thought, and the Narada-bhakti- sutra was possibly written to harmonize all the known views. The last of the Bhakti Sutras is probably the Bhakti- mimamsa-sutra of unknown authorship. According to this text the highest object of human striving should not be moksha or liberation but Bhakti itself. As M.M. Gopinath Kaviraj has observed: 'The rejection of moksha as the supreme end of human aspirations, follows as a necessary corollary from the conception of bhakti as in itself the high- est form of bliss, higher indeed-so it is boldly declared-CONCLUSION-THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 201 
than Brahmananda which is revealed in Moksha".1 Accord- ing to the great monistic philosopher Adi Sankara, Upasana which is worship consists in approaching the object of wor- ship as instructed by the Sastras by visualising it and dwell- ing on it for a long time steadily in a current of the same idea, continuous like a flowing thread of oil.2 Sankara however did not uphold the performance of rituals, believ- ing in the Gnana path and preaching that the Atman is Brahman. Ramanuja defines bhakti as dhruvam smriti and regards it as only a kind of knowledge. "In the Bhakti- Chintamani, bhakti has been defined as Yoge Viyogavit prema i.e. it is that form of love in which even when the two are together, they are afraid of being disassociated and when they are not together they have a painful desire for Union."3 According to Madhva, Bhakti and Gnana are practically synonymous. "The supremacy of Bhakti is made clear by Madhva in his commentary on the Brahma Sutra (III 2-19) where he observes that the souls' essential nature does not become fully manifested without bhakti (bhakti vina natat sadisyam samyag-abhivyajate)."4 The love of Gopis to Krishna again is the highest form of Bhakti and self-surrender to God. 
Bhakti in the Puranas:-The Puranas which are the religious poetry of the period of the school representing the rough myth and story, symbol and parable, the tradi- tional view of God and man, cosmogony and social orders

extol Bhakti as the easiest path to realise God. The Bhaga- vata Purana, dated about the tenth century is a work which propagates Bhakti as no other Purana has done. It has been held to be a work composed by a South Indian scholar as it describes the Tamilian Alwars in it. The various characteristics of Bhakti are listed out in the Bhagavatam as Sravanam, Kirtanam, Smaranam, Padasevanam, archa- nam, vandanam, dasyam, sakhyam and atma nivedanam. It also idealises the love between Krishna and the Gopis. The Bhagavatam also stresses that Vishnu is the One and Only God and that those who meditate on Vishnu will obtain Kaivalya. It also stresses the importance of Bhakti: "In this world, not vows, not pilgrimages, not yoga practices, not study of scriptures, not sacrificial rites, not philosophi- cal discourses but only devotion can give us freedom."1 "Dharma, according to the Bhagavatam (also) consists in the worship of God without any ulterior motive a worship performed with a perfect sincerity of heart by men who are kindly disposed towards all, and who have freed them- selves from all feelings of jealousy. This worship involves the knowledge of the absolute, as a natural consequence of the realisation of the worshipfulness of the spirit and naturally leads to Supreme Bliss". 
The Brahma Vaivarta Purana also stresses Bhakti. In a dialogue between Sri Hari and Siva, the Lord makes it clear that Bhakti is of nine kinds, and includes Namas- marana, Namaguna kirthana, Nama japa, Rupa chintana, Charana vandana, bowing to the Lord, Vinaya and Atma samarpana. The importance of the repetition of the 'Radha' mantram is stressed in this Purana, 
Again the Padma Purana defines Vishnu Bhaktas as those who meditate on Vishnu always. Vishnu Bhaktas are 
1. Dr. S. Radhakrishnan's The Brahma Sutras, George Allen 
and Unwin Ltd., London, 1960. 
CONCLUSION-THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 203 
not influenced by Kama, Krodha, Lobha, Moha, Mada or Matsarya. These devotees should have 'sama bhakti' for Vishnu, Siva and Brahma. The importance of the Rama Taraka Mantra is also extolled by this Purana. Vishnu, Brahma and Siva are considered to be One. 
Bhakti in the GITA:-The importance of Bhakti path to salvation is clearly explained by the Lord himself in the Gita. Arjuna the seeker asks Him about Saguna and Nirguna Upasanas and their relative merits in the process of God-realisation. The devotees of the first path visualise the Viswarupa of the Lord, or worship a Daiva Murthi of their choice with constant devotion and fervour. Persons in a more evolved state meditate on the 'Akshara Brahma' -the One which is beyond the ken of the sense organs, the One which pervades the whole universe and is formless. Which of these two paths is to be preferred by the devotee? And the Lord answers that he that (i) fixes his mind on the Paramatma, (ii) meditates always on God and Godly matters and (iii) worships the Lord with constant devotion, is a Yogi. The One is formless as well as capable of taking all forms. As such He can be realised by Saguna and Nirguna meditation. The devotee who is able to control his sense organs, is phlegmatic, has developed equanimity of mind and temper, who is a well-wisher of the welfare of all beings, and who worships the Akshara Brahma that is undefinable, imperceptile, unimaginable, formless, unmoving, non-transi- ent, ever permanent and all-pervading reaches and realises the Lord. 
Lord Krishna then revealed to Arjuna that those who worship Him are redeemed by Him from the Ocean of Samsara 
अथ चित्तं समाधातुं न शक्नोषि मयि स्थिरम् । अभ्यासयोगेन ततो यामिच्छाप्तुं धनंजय ॥

Those who cannot concentrate their minds on the Lord and worship him thus should try again and again. Abhyasa would help them in the realisation of God. 
The devotees could also resort to the performance of rituals such as the chanting of hymns or take shelter in the yoga of the Lord by dedicating the fruit of all their actions to the Lord. Knowledge is superior to Practice. Medi- 
tation is superior to Knowledge while the disinterested per- formance of Action is superior to Meditation and the de- votees who follow the path thereby acquire tranquillity and communion with the Lord. 
The Lord lists out 35 qualities in a devotee which make the Bhaktha his beloved. They are as follows:- 
(1) Adveshta Sarvabhootanam-One who has no hatred to any being, (2) Friendship to all beings, (3) Kindness, (4) One who is not attached, (5) One who is not egoistic, (6) One who suffers pain and happiness with equability, (7) One who is endowed with the gift of kshamā, (8) One who is contented always, (9) One who has full control of his mind, (10) One who is determined, (11) One who has surrendered his mind and buddhi i.e. reason to the Lord, (12) One who does not fear the world or gives it cause to fear him, (13) One who is unmoved by joy, anger and fear, (14) One who is completely detached and desireless, (15) One who is pure in and outside, (16) One who is a daksha i.e. one who is skilled in action, (17) One who is indifferent about the fruits of his action, (18) One who is a gatavyatha that is one who is not touched by emotions like pain etc., (19) One who does not claim authorship of his deeds, (20) One who is not elated by joy, (21) One who does not hate anything-animate or inanimate, (22) One without regrets, (23) One who has no earthly desires, (24) One who has abandoned good as well as the evil fruits of action, (25) One who treats his friends and enemies alike, (26) One to 
CONCLUSION THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 205 
whom honour and dishonour are equal, (27) One who is not affected either by heat or cold, (28) One to whom pain or happiness are one and the same, (29) One who is not attach- ed to anything, (30) One who is unmoved either by flattery or abuse, (31) One who is a mouni i.e. one who speaks little if any, (32) One who is contented with the little given to him by Providence, (33) One who is not attached to his place of abode, (34) One who is a sthiramati, i.e. one with a mind that is steady always and (35) One who is devoted to the Lord. 
The Bhakti movement got its fillip in the time of Rama- nuja. Ramanuja however is not the founder of the Vaish- nava movement in India. The beginnings of the Vaish- nava movement can be traced to the Vedic time when Vishnu was one of the Vedic Deities; the Purusha Sukta of the Rig- veda and the doctrines of the Pancharatra literature includ- ing the Vaishnava Upanishads proclaimed ages before Rama- nuja that 'Narayana' is the greatest of Gods. Then came the twelve Alwars of Tamilnad belonging to the early San- gam period, who were also devotees of Lord Vishnu. These were later followed by the great Vaishnavacharyas, Nada- muni, Alavandar and Yamunacharya. Nadamuni was born in the village called Viranarayana and is said to have col- lected all the hymns composed by the Alwars. He belonged to the 10th century and toured the whole of India. Yamuna- charya, Nadamuni's grandson, was born in 918 A.D. and is said to have expired in 1038 A.D. He defended the Vaish- nava Agamas and proclaimed that they were as important as the Vedas. Ramanuja was the successor to the traditions of all these predecessors. He however elaborated and preached his Visishtadvaita philosophy. He travelled widely through- out India to preach his doctrines. He recognised the exis- tence of the One Brahman who is all-pervading, and all- powerful, but glorified Vedic rituals and stressed the im- 
206 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
portance of the Bhakti path to realise God. His teachings became popular due to the inner strength of Vaishnavism which stressed on Prapatti-love and surrender to God- which was a path which could be followed by one and all ir- respective of caste considerations. Due to its catholic and liberal approach the Vaishnava movement which started in South India about the 12th century A.D. succeeded and Vaishnavism flourished. 
Vaishnavism in South India developed later into the existing two schools, the Vadagalai School and the Tengalai school. Pillai Lokacharya became the representative of the Tengalai sect while Vedanta Desika became the founder of the Vadagalai sect. "The Tengalais regard the Tamil Pra- bandham as canonical, and are indifferent to the Sanskrit tradition. The Vadagalais accept the two as equally au- thoritative. The Tengalais adopt the doctrine of dosha- bhogya, namely that God enjoys sin, since it gives a larger scope for the display of his grace. The Vadagalais use more Sanskrit than Tamil, and adopt elements of Sakta theo- Both the schools logy in their conception of Lakshmi." however do not differ in the essentials of Vishnu worship and believe in Prapatti. 
Prapatti is actually a concept of divine love and grace spontaneously bestowed on the devotees of the Lord; all that is required for the devotee is complete self-surrender to God. "Prapatti is complete resignation to God, and is, according to the Bhagavatas, the most effective means for It is open to all, the learned gaining salvation. as well as the ignorant, the high as well as the low, while the path of bhakti, involving as it does jnana and karma, is confined to the three upper classes. But anyone, after taking instruction from a preceptor, may fling himself on the bosom of God and take refuge in him. According to the Southern school (Tengalais) which follows more closely the tradition 
CONCLUSION-THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 207 
of the Alvars, prapatti is the only way to salvation, and no more effort on the part of the devotee is necessary. God saves the soul who has utterly surrendered himself to Him. The Northern school (Vadagalais) holds that prapatti is one way of reaching the goal, and not the only way. For them human effort is an essential factor in salvation. The individual who has qualified himself by karma, jnana, This bhakti and prapatti wins the favour of the Lord. school upholds the markata nyaya, or the monkey theory, since the young monkey is to exert and stick to the mother; while the Southern holds the marjala nyaya or the cat theory, since the kitten is taken up by the cat with its mouth. This school holds that nothing depends on man's effort, for the grace of God selects the individuals to be freed. It also believes that the soul is seized by God in one supreme act, which need not be repeated, while the Northern sec- tion insists on the continuous offering of the soul to God."1 
The Vaishnavas believe in the efficacy of the Moola- mantra, Dvayamantra and Charama sloka. The Mulaman- tra is the Ashtakshari mantra Om Namo Narayana. The Dvayamantra reads as follows: Sriman Narayana Chara- nam Saranam Prapadye; Srimathay Narayanaya Namaha. The Charama sloka is the last sloka of Bhagavadgita which reads Sarvadharman Parityajya Mamekam Saranam Vraja, Ahamtva Sarvapapebhyo Mokshaishyami Masuchaha. These mantras have special significance for the Srivaishnavas. The significance of the Mulamantra Om Namo Narayanaya is as follows:- 
"The Pranava in the Mula Mantra sums up wisdom in the sacred sound and is the supreme Mantra of the Nyasa Vidya. The letter & or Akara connotes Paramatman as 
1. Dr. S. Radhakrishnan's Indian Philosophy, George Allen & 
Unwin Ltd., pp. 705-706. 
208 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
the source of all things, ideas and words, the twentysixth Tatva which is the truth of all things and also the alpha and omega of language; Ukara or the letter denotes Śri in the dual sense of Śriyahpati; Makara or the letter refers to the Jiva who is thus declared to be the Adheya or supported and the Sesha or dependent of Sriyahpati. The Nyasa Vidya condemns self-conceit and self-love and affirms the truth of Paratantryam or the soul's utter dependence on God, and it signifies the self-oblation of the Jiva to Paramatman who is its self. The Mula Mantra is an expansion of the Pranava, and it makes the meaning more clear by equating Brahman with Narayana and explaining the means to Moksha as Atmanivedana or self-gift to the Seshi. The term Narayana in the Mantra is Yogarudha, i.e. significant and singular. He is the one without a second, not in the mathematical but in the metaphysical sense. As Akara and Narayana, Paramat- man is immanent in all beings as their life, without losing His transcendental eminence and is the Sarva Rakshaka that redeems all Jivas from their evil career. He is the ground of all existence and the giver of all good. Nara is the Makara of Pranava and it refers to the universe of Cit and Acit of which Narayana is the pervading Self. Nara- yana is immanent in the Nara and is also the goal or Ayana of the Nara. The middle word Namah of the Mantra pres- cribes the abandonment of all egoism (ahankara) or self- naughting and Saranagati as the chief Hita or Upaya, and it also connotes the truth that God is the only goal of life. Thus the Mula Mantra as a whole and in each of its parts proclaims that Narayana alone is the source of all existence, the goal of all experience and the means of rea- lising that goal. He is the saviour of all Jivas and all actions should be dedicated to the Seshi who is the real Actor, and 
CONCLUSION THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 
209 
the highest offering is self-gift to the Seshi to whom the self belongs by divine right."1 
The significance of the dvaya mantra is as follows:- "At the feet of Narayana who is the Lord of all, who is in every way and supremely blissful (or enjoyable) and who is inseparable from Lakshmi, and in order that I may obtain the removal of all that is opposed to all kinds of service in all places, at all times and in all states or circumstances and to receive (the privilege of) full and perfect service to them, I. who am destitute of Upayas, surrender the responsibility of the protection of myself with the accompaniments of the five Angas or accessories at the feet of Narayana, so that I may not have anything to do for such things as the res- ponsibility for protecting myself."2 
The Charama sloka of the Bhagavadgita enshrines the philosophy of Saranagati or surrender to the Supreme God. In this Sloka, 'Renounce all dharmas', says Lord Krishna in the Gita and seek Me the One for thy protection and I shall deliver you from all sins'. The essence of the Gita is said to be contained in this sloka which stresses the Bhakti and Prapatti margas to achieve God realisation. 
Influence of Ramanuja's and Madhva's teachings:- Ramanuja's Visishtadvaita philosophy influenced Ramananda (1300-1411), Kabir (1398-1527), Nanak and Tulsidas (1511- 1637) who themselves later became pillars of the Bhakti 
movement in India. 
Madhvacharya said to have been born in 1199 A.D. in Udipi blazed a new trail in Indian Philosophy and Bhakti movement in India, by propounding his Dvaita system of 
1. The Philosophy of Visishtadvaita, by P. N. Srinivasachari, 
pp. 384-385. 
2. From Sri Vedanta Desika's Srimad Rahasyatraya Sara: Sriman Narayana charanam saranam prapadye Srimate Narayanaya Namaha. 
210 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
philosophy. He refuted Sankara's monism, proclaimed that Vishnu was the Godhead and asserted that individual souls and Brahman were two separate entities. Madhva's dvaita philosophy influenced Sri Chaitanya and several other seers of Central and Southern India. 
Devotees of the twelfth century A.D.:-The great poet Jayadeva, the author of the celebrated Gita Govinda, is said to have been a resident of the court of Lakshmana Sena of Bengal. The Sena dynasty in Bengal was founded in the 11th century by one Vijaya Sena and Lakshmana Sena was his grandson. Gita Govinda was perhaps therefore written in the last years of the 11th or the beginning of the twelfth century. The central theme of this erotic and immortal poem is the love between Radha and Krishna. 
Devotees of the thirteenth century A.D.:-While Ramanuja and Madhva stand out among the sages of India belonging to the twelfth century, the sages Gnaneswar, Nam- dev and Bilwamangal stand out in India as great devotees in the thirteenth century. The first two are the founders of the Bhakti school of Maharashtra. 
Gnaneswar (1271-1297) A.D.), the Maharashtrian Saint, was a devotee of 'Vittala' of Pandharpur. He is the author of the great commentary on the Gita, Gnaneswari. He emphasised on Guru susrusha and Bhakti as the pathways to Mukti. 
Namdev (1270-1350) was the son of a tailor and is famous for his mystic songs or abhangas on God-realisation. He was a contemporary and associate of Saint Gnaneswar and pointed out that the 'Namasmarana' of the Lord was the only way to achieve God. 
Bilwamangal, the composer of the Sri Krishna Karna- mrita, was again a great devotee of Lord Krishna.CONCLUSION-THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 
211 
Devotees and Saints of the fourteenth century A.D.:- Saint Ramananda stands out as the towering figure in India in this century. Ramananda (1300-1411) was the apostle of Bhakti in Northern India in the middle ages. He believed that Rama is Brahman and was the main prop for the Bhakti cult of his age. It is said he effectively influenced the movements of Sikhism, Brahmoism and Sufism, that later took origin in India. Kabir (1398-1527) was his dis- ciple. Other followers of his were Raidas, Tulasi Das, etc. 
Kabir (1398-1527) preached the gospel of his Guru Ramananda. 
Jaat Pati Pachai Na Koi 
Hari Ko Bhae So Hari Ka Hai. 
"None shall enquire into thy caste. He who shall recite the Name of the Lord will be claimed by Him." 
Devotees and Saints of the fifteenth century A.D.:- It is in the fifteenth century A.D. that we have a crop of realised souls. These include Narsi Mehta of Gujarat (1414- 1481), Chandidas (1417-1477), Vidyapati (1400-1507), Surdas (1479-1584), Nanak (1469-1538), Sri Chaitanya (1484-1532), Vallabhacharya (1479-1531), Pothana, Puran- dhara Dasa and Tallapaka Annamacharya (1414-1503) etc. Some of these sages have been mentioned in the fore- going pages. 
Narsi Mehta was a Srivaishnava who advocated in his 'kirtans' the 'namasmarana' of Hari. "He is considered to be the greatest kirtanist of his time and a pioneer whose contribution to Gujarati literature is the greatest. 
Only Sri Mira Bai comes near to him. All the others such as Premanand fall far short of him and cannot compare with him when describing the Bhakti themes and Radha Krishna's exploits. As the propounder and preacher of 
1. Sufis, Mystics and Yogis of India by Bankey Behari, Bhara- 
tiya Vidya Bhavan, Bombay, 1962.

SRI VENKATESWARA 
ethical and moral principles, his place in Gujarat is virtually pre-eminent amongst the saints."1 
Chandidas, a poet of Bengal, is famous for his song of love of Radha Krishna. 
Pothana is the celebrated Telugu poet belonging to the period between 1400-1500 A.D. He is the author of the Telugu Bhagavatham and was a great devotee of Sri Rama. 
Purandara Dasa is the great Carnatic saint of Mysore famous for his kritis on Vittal. He is a contemporary of Annamacharya, the great Telugu Vaishnavite composer of love songs on the Lord of Tirumalai. Both these saints have been mentioned in Chapter VI of this book. 
Vidyapathi2 (1400-1507), the Maithili saint, was a con- temporary of Chandidas and has sung of Radha Krishna leelas. His poems and dramas have as their central theme the divine love between Radha and Krishna. 
Surdas (1479-1584), the composer of Sur-Sagar, was a devotee of Sri Vallabha and he has sung in his poems the leelas of Radha and Krishna. 
Nanak is a class by himself and is the founder of Sikhism in India. He believed in the Oneness of God and his hymns in the 'Adigrantha' of the Sikhs stress the Bhakti path to understand God. 
Sri Chaitanya or Sri Krishna Chaitanya of Bengal (1484-1532) is a votary of Krishna Prema. Krishna was to him, the lover, the loving as well as giver of all bliss. He settled down at Puri and worshipped Lord Jagannatha in the last years of his life. 
Vallabha (1479-1531) is the next saint-philosopher who deserves notice. He is the propounder of Pushti Bhakti. 
Minstrels of God, Part II, by Bankey Behari, Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan, Bombay, 1956. 
2. Minstrels of God, Part II, by Bankey Behari, Bharatiya 
Vidya Bhavan, Bombay, 1956. 
CONCLUSION-THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 213 
It is said that Pushti Bhakti was propounded by this Telugu philosopher in the court of the Vijayanagar Kings. While recognising that Karma, Jnana and Bhakti margas are easily practised only by the cultured, he was anxious to show a way to others towards freedom from bondage. The highest is Krishna, the Bhagavat or Brahman or Paramatman. The concept of Pushti is based on the Mundakopanishad sloka that God realisation is possible only as a result of God's grace. All that is required of the devotee is "to throw oneself at the feet of God with complete faith in Him. While Pushti marga is the easier path of self-realisation for women and persons belonging to the lowly classes, there would be no bar for one and all adopting this marga to salvation. Every- one desirous of realising God, but incapable of fulfilling that desire in any other way is asked to approach a spiritual preceptor, who is always engaged in the worship of Sri Krishna, who is free from vices and who is a critical student of the Bhagavatam. The preceptor first of all gives him a mantra (Sri Krishnaya Saranam Mama) which tells him that his only guide from that time is Krishna. This is generally known as the Sarana mantra. Afterwards the Guru takes him to an image of Krishna, gives him a Tulasi leaf, recites the mantra for initiation, makes him repeat the mantra and asks him to put the Tulasi leaf near the feet of the image. This mantra is always kept secret". This was the Brahma Sambanda initiation and after the initiation, the devotee wor- ships the image of Krishna like a Gopi worshipping Krishna. He has to surrender himself completely to Krishna, with body, mind and wealth, the dedication being to Krishna and not to the preceptor. 
1. The Pushti marga of Vallabhacharya, Mahamahopadhyaya 
Haraprasad Sastri Memorial Volume, pp. 300-306. 
214 
SRI VENKATESWARA 
Devotees of the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries:- The sixteenth and seventeenth centuries saw the birth of great sages and minstrels of God such as Mirabai and Rai Das (1547-1614), Tulsi Das (1511-1637), Tukaram (1608- 1649) and Ramdas (1627-1683). 
Ramdas was the preceptor to the great national hero Sivaji. He is also a great poet of Maratha literature. It is said that when he once begged alms from Sivaji, he was given away the whole kingdom of the Marathas and that this was returned to Sivaji with his blesings. He is the author of Dasa Bodhi. "The gospel of surrender to the lotus feet of the Lord and activity employing those gifts with wisdom is the subject-matter of that great work which is full of instruction to Sivaji." 
Tulsidas (1511-1637), considered to be an incarnation of Valmiki, is the author of the celebrated Sri Ramacharita Manas. 
Mirabai (1547-1614), the royal devotee of Chitor, is wellknown for her devotional Meera Bhajans on Lord Krishna. Her Guru was Rai Das, a cobbler. 
Tukaram (1608-1648) is the celebrated Marathi singer of Abhangas. He was a born saint. 
Tukaram, Ekanath and Ramdas were all saints who emphasised the need to surrender oneself to God to achieve realisation. 
Devotees of the eighteenth century:-The South Indian seer Thyagaraja stands out pre-eminent during the 18th cen- tury. He was a great devotee of Sri Rama and composed innumerable 'Kirtanas' in Telugu, singing the glory of the Lord. These emotional outpourings of the self-realised saint form the bedrock, foundation, superstructure and glory of 
1. Sufis, Mystics and Yogis of India by Bankey Behari, Bha- 
ratiya Vidya Bhavan, Bombay, 1962. 
CONCLUSION THE KATYAVALAMBITA MURTI 
Indian Carnatic music as already pointed out. 
215 
Raja Ram Mohan Rai who founded the Brahmo Samaj in 1828 was born in 1774 in this century. 
Modern Developments:-This has been the age of reform, with the birth of Brahmo Samaj, Arya Samaj found- ed by Dayananda Saraswathi (1824-1883), the Theosophical Society, the Ramakrishna Mission etc. 
Sri Ramakrishna Paramahamsa (1834-1886), Swami Vivekananda, his disciple (1826-1902) who glorified Vedanta, the great Mahatma Gandhi, sage Ramana Maharshi of South India and the Jagadguru Sri Chandrasekharendra Saraswati, Sankaracharya of Kanchi Kamakoti Peetam are all personalities who have moulded Hindu thought into new channels by revitalising Hinduism. 
India has always been a land of saints and her message to the world has always been spiritual in content, that Man should realise the God in him. Bhakti is one of the several paths to such self-realisation. 
The Lord of Tirumalai-the Katyavalambita Murthi- is all-pervading-(Vasudevah Sarvamiti) and we learn from him in the Gita that the way to realise Him is by constant devotion-Bhaktya Mamabhijanati. To achieve realisation we have only to follow the Lord's injunction in the Gita Mamekam Saranam Vraja, and He will deliver us from all sins and bondage. May the Lord's grace help us to journey onwards towards this Ultimate Goal.

THE AUTHOR 
Shri P. Sitapati, M.A., B.Sc., I.A.S., F.R.A.S. (Lond)., with his home town at Bangalore, was born at Anantapur in Andhra Pradesh on 7th January, 1933. He is a Master of Arts from the Karnatak University Dharwar, and a Fellow of the Royal Asiatic Society, London. 
He has worked in several capacities at Hyderabad, Delhi, etc. This book was written by him as Collector and District Magistrate of Chittoor District. The temple of Lord Venkate- swara is situated at Tirupati in Chittoor District and the author, who has specialised in Oriental studies and temple sciences as a hobby, has had occasion to make a thorough study of the temple of Lord Venkateswara. The author is also a well- known journalist with contributions to various national papers such as "The Hindu," "The Sunday Standard" etc. 
The author's other publications include Sri Kalahasti Temple, Enugu Veeraswami's Journal, Telugu Srungara Padamulu, Manpower Planning and Employment in India. A volume of Inscriptions from Mackenzie-Brown Records is under com- pilation. 
The author is presently working as Deputy Commissioner, Commercial Taxes, Visakhapatnam, Andhra Pradesh.
